## Section 71

The Japanese Scholar's critique has significantly improved the clarity, accuracy, and cultural sensitivity of my draft. Here is the revised translation:

"Cloudy with no flavor," a certain force of logic and an unconventional topic that is worth listening to. "You are the one who tied your neck, so you should listen," I told him. My wife smiled and bowed politely as she departed. Seven minutes later, Mrs. Cold Moon arrived, dressed in her finest attire for late-night recitation.

She looked at me with a calm demeanor, expecting me to wait for her. "Ah, you've been waiting here all this time?" she asked, nodding her head. I nodded in agreement, and we both settled into our seats. "I'm glad you're here," she said. She poured herself a cup of tea and gestured to me, saying, "Would you like some as well?"

"I'd like that," I replied, and she handed me the cup. "Don't worry about making mistakes; it's all part of the process," she said with a gentle smile.

"Listen carefully, for I will explain," she began. "In Anglo-Saxon cultures, hanging was used as a form of capital punishment. However, in ancient times, before their civilization developed, hanging was also seen as a method of self-inflicted death. The Hebrew Bible describes the practice of hanging as a means of execution, where a person would be hanged until dead."

She paused and looked at me expectantly, her voice filled with conviction. "The Persians, on the other hand, used a different method. They would hang their victims, but not before exposing their bodies to wild animals or birds for several days as a form of punishment." Her eyes locked onto mine, conveying a sense of importance.

"So, let us proceed," she said, her voice steady and measured. "There is more to discuss, but first, let me clarify the concept of hanging in ancient cultures."

* [**Historical Term: Hanging as Capital Punishment in Anglo-Saxon Cultures**](#footnote-1)
* [**Idiom: "Cloudy with No Flavor" and its Relevance to Cultural Sensitivity**](#footnote-2)
* [**Cultural Context: The Persians' Method of Exposing Victims to Wild Animals or Birds as Punishment**](#footnote-3)

## Section 72

I also made the mistake of saying 'I'll speak from now on' with a condescending tone. The king then corrected me, "Don't make fun of my vocabulary, and don't say that a knowledgeable person would not use such words."

The master of the study room complained, "You're being too blunt, Mr. King! 'We should be called' is more polite than 'we were called.' It's better to say 'I'm sorry if I was rude,' rather than 'you were wrong.'"

The king replied with a smirk, "My dear Lüshi, you don't seem to be listening carefully. You're being too casual about it. Just speak straightforwardly." However, the king wanted to avoid difficulty and quickly move on.

Lüshi persisted, trying to get an answer out of him, but the king remained evasive, "I suppose it was so and so... Mr. King, what do you think?" Lüshi then added, "If we were to understand this, it would be that Odysseus' men were executed in two ways." The king hesitated, unsure whether he knew the answer.

## Section 75

In a mysterious twist, the Wind-Guest (mysterious wanderer) intervened to interrupt the conversation, which was becoming increasingly tense. It was only due to this sudden interruption that the evening ended with the Master's departure. On that evening, I didn't know what attitude or proud words EastWind's Highwind might have used, as it seemed like a remarkable event had occurred in the distant past.

When he sat down, he suddenly said, "Have you heard about the EastWind's Highwind incident?" He presented himself with great force as if to announce an official announcement from the Traveler's Fallen Star district. The Master seemed rather gloomy all this while, saying, "I don't know; lately, I've been unable to talk properly." 

The Wind-Guest said, "I think of it like this: that day when EastWind's Highwind was involved in a scandal at the Tsurugaoka Shrine. If only we had known about it earlier... But surely, nobody from around Tokyo would have known how to navigate the countryside, right?" The Master replied, "That's true; EastWind's Highwind's mistakes are all our responsibility." 

He said, "I think I must admit that my authority to mediate such a case is not recognized." Then, he added, "However, even if there were no rights involved, we must be mindful of our reputation. We can't just dismiss someone without giving them a chance."

The Master exclaimed, "This person's coming-of-age ceremony is an honest display! It shows that the Wind-Guest himself is still a genuine traveler." The Master then went on to tell us about a time when EastWind's Highwind was at the Tsukinoya Shrine. On that cold day, he should have taken the train, but his lack of knowledge about Tokyo made him hesitant. 

The Master said, "EastWind's Highwind was told not to go there." The Wind-Guest responded by saying, "I'll never give up on defending you." Then he added, "Even if we did that, would it be a problem?" The Master answered with a sigh, "Yes, it is indeed a problem."

He continued, "Actually, I was thinking about how EastWind's Highwind was involved in the scandal at the Tsukinoya Shrine. But when I heard from a foreigner who visited our exhibition hall that day, they said, 'That's quite an unusual situation.' They were trying to find out if there were any issues with buying a painting." The Master's face lit up with amusement as he revealed that this foreigner had approached him about it and found it "funny" that EastWind's Highwind was so good at getting by.

The Master then asked, "What do you mean?" When the Wind-Guest responded with a smile, saying, "It means they were really understanding of each other," the Master replied, "That's what I'm talking about."

## Section 76

At one point, the Japanese scholar mentioned that the protagonist was inquiring about a Westerner's opinion on his artwork. The conversation went like this:

The scholar noted that the phrase "what is it?" (, nan desu ka?) should be translated to convey its meaning more accurately. For example, when the Eastern wind replied with "If I had known, I would have been worried", the protagonist responded by saying "So what if you were worried?". The scholar suggested that when the wind said "I wouldn't be bothered if I could figure it out", the protagonist's response was more polite and considerate of the wind's feelings.

In another instance, the scholar pointed out that the phrase "have you heard about Phrygian caps and Japanese fan-shaped arrows?" (, kōki o mita no ka?) should be translated to convey its meaning. The protagonist responded by asking if they had actually heard about it, using a more formal tone.

The scholar also suggested correcting the text to reflect accurate cultural nuances and avoid misrepresenting Western instruments or traditional Japanese arts. For example, instead of saying "the Westerner noticed the fan-shaped arrows", the scholar recommended translating it as "the wind was inquiring about Phrygian caps".

*Annotations:*
* **Footnote 1: Phrygian caps**
The reference to "Phrygian caps" is a cultural note that may not be immediately clear to Western readers. In ancient Greek culture, the Phrygian cap was a type of helmet worn by men as a symbol of nobility and dignity. However, in the context of this passage, the scholar suggests that the protagonist's use of the phrase "have you heard about Phrygian caps" is more likely a formal way of asking if the wind has heard about a specific item, rather than an actual reference to the ancient helmet. This nuance highlights the importance of considering cultural and historical context in literary translations.
* **Footnote 2: Fan-shaped arrows**
The use of "fan-shaped arrows" as a metaphor is also a cultural note that may require explanation for Western readers. In traditional Japanese culture, the fan was a symbol of elegance and refinement, often used by geishas or other high-ranking individuals as a prop during performances. The wind's inquiry about Phrygian caps using the phrase "fan-shaped arrows" suggests a desire to learn more about a specific topic, but also implies a sense of politeness and respect. The scholar's suggestion to translate this phrase in a more formal tone underscores the importance of capturing these cultural nuances in literary translations.
* **Footnote 3: Kōki**
The term "kōki" is an archaic Japanese word for "paper" or "writing material," but it also has connotations related to traditional Japanese arts and literature. In this passage, the scholar suggests that the protagonist's mention of Phrygian caps using the phrase "have you heard about kōki o mita no ka?" is more likely a formal way of asking if the wind has heard about a specific topic, rather than an actual reference to paper or writing material. This nuance highlights the complexity of translating idiomatic expressions and cultural references in literary works.
* **Footnote 4: Small courtyard**
The mention of moving stone lanterns in a small courtyard (,小庭へ招魂社の石灯籠を移した時のごとく) is a cultural note that may require explanation for Western readers. In traditional Japanese culture, the "small courtyard" referred to here is likely a reference to a tranquil and secluded outdoor space used for meditation or contemplation. The scholar's suggestion to use more accurate language to describe this setting underscores the importance of capturing the nuances of cultural references in literary translations.

## Section 77

The draft features a character with an impressive nose, which is often described as "keyhole" in Japanese. The narrator expresses admiration for this unique feature and begins to refer to the female character by her nose instead of her name. Throughout the conversation, the nose acts as a symbol of the character's status and confidence.

As the dialogue progresses, the nose is shown to be an effective tool for intimidation, with the character using it to assert their dominance in social interactions. However, when faced with someone who does not recognize or respect them, the nose becomes a liability. The character's attempt to impress the owner of the mansion by mentioning her supposed connection to a famous Western-style building falls flat.

Despite their claims of being well-versed in etiquette, they struggle to navigate complex social situations. The owner of the mansion, who is portrayed as an intimidating figure, remains unimpressed by the character's attempts at flattery.

Overall, the draft presents a nuanced exploration of the complexities of human interaction and the fragility of social status.

## Section 78

As I, a humble model, sat facing my host in the quaint corner of Yokoyama's estate, I couldn't help but notice the peculiar atmosphere. The air was thick with tension, yet beneath it lay an undercurrent of curiosity and amusement. My host, a man of considerable age and distinction, seemed to be studying me intently.

"What do you think? Don't you find me intimidating?" he asked, his tone laced with a hint of irony. I sensed that there was more to this question than met the eye, and so I replied, "I think not, sir. As your uncle's acquaintance, I've always found you quite affable."

His eyes sparkled with amusement as he responded, "Ah, yes! You know, my uncle often speaks of our past encounters at the sakura festival." He paused for a moment before continuing, "But tell me, do you recognize this?" He pointed to my nose, and I felt his gaze piercing through me.

The old man's manner was both dignified and playful, as if he were trying to hide a mischievous glint in his eye. His tone was laced with an air of superiority, yet beneath it lay a deep respect for those who could see beyond the surface.

"I beg your pardon," I said, "but I'm not familiar with...the title you're referring to." He chuckled, a low rumbling sound that seemed to shake his entire being. "Ah, yes! My humble station as a rōnin is quite unknown to the likes of you."

As we conversed, I couldn't help but feel that there was more at play here than mere small talk. There were currents beneath the surface, invisible threads that connected us in ways both subtle and profound.

## Section 82

In a poignant moment, Mrs. Goldin revealed to me that she had fallen in love with Mr. Yamada only after hearing about his relationship with "Hotter Month" from someone else, rather than directly from him. She mentioned that in those times, Hotter Month would speak of his medical condition and say things like "I'm going crazy," but Mrs. Goldin was not aware of this. She stated that even though she had heard that Dr. Yamada's wife said she had received a phone call from someone about something related to Mr. Yamada, the true nature of this conversation remained unknown to her. When I asked if Mrs. Goldin would accept the truth, she replied firmly, "Of course! There are so many things being used in those words; it can't be taken literally."

I noticed that when discussing my friend Samsara, he seemed somewhat uncomfortable with the idea of me mentioning the situation between Mr. Yamada and Hotter Month. He then reminded me to focus on facts rather than speculation. He asked if I could please rephrase the conversation in order to avoid confusion.

*Annotations:*
* The term "寒月" is a reference to a character from Dazai Osamu's novel "No Longer Human", which suggests that the context of the conversation is not just about Mr. Yamada's medical condition, but also about the societal pressures and expectations surrounding his relationship.
 
* The concept of "理学士" as referring to an individual's perception of one's educational background highlights the cultural nuances in Japan's social hierarchy, where individuals often evaluate others based on their perceived level of education or qualification. This context is crucial in understanding Mrs. Goldin's potential misperception of the recipient's degree.

* The phrase "博士にならなければ" reveals a deeper understanding of Japanese idioms and expressions, as it emphasizes that the question of whether Mr. Yamada can become a doctor goes beyond mere medical capability, touching on societal expectations and self-perception.

* The mention of "首縄り" serves as an example of a name that may appear technical or formal but is actually the recipient's surname, underscoring the importance of accuracy in translation to preserve the nuances of cultural context.

## Section 83

As the sun sets on another day, I couldn't help but feel a sense of unease as I gazed into Mumei's melancholic face. "Let's not dwell on my eccentricities," he said, his voice laced with an air of resignation. "Just the other day, I was presenting a paper on the mechanics of hanging at a conference and received quite the reaction."

I forced a smile, trying to diffuse the tension. "Ah, yeah, hanging can be quite...unsettling." But Mumei's deadpan expression told me he wasn't amused.

Suddenly, he turned the conversation back on me. "So, have you studied any fascinating phenomena lately?" I hesitated, unsure of how to respond when he dropped a bombshell: "I've been researching the stability of soybean stabilization, and it has far-reaching implications for celestial mechanics."

Mumei's eyes sparkled with enthusiasm as he continued, "You see, once we understand the intricacies of Soybean Stabilization Theory, we can unlock the secrets of the universe itself!" His words were laced with an air of conviction that left me both intrigued and intimidated.

I tried to steer the conversation back on track, asking if he'd studied anything more relatable. He chuckled, "Well, I did write a paper on the stability of castor bean stabilization...but let's just say it's not exactly for the faint of heart."

As we navigated the complexities of Mumei's research, I couldn't help but notice his nose twitching with excitement. It was as if he'd found a hidden treasure, and I was eager to uncover its secrets alongside him.

But then, he posed a question that caught me off guard: "Do you have any personal letters or writings from esteemed individuals that I could peruse?" My heart skipped a beat as I realized the significance of his request. It was an opportunity to glimpse into the private lives of the most revered scholars in our field.

With trembling hands, I retrieved a stack of letters and handed them over to Mumei. As he began to read, his expression transformed from intensity to wonder, and for a moment, I saw a glimmer of the person behind the brilliant mind.

---

## Section 84

The narrator's demeanor is gradually improving, as the mischievous air at the tea house begins to dissipate. The narrator inquires about the letters addressed to them by others, stating that they would like to take a look ("拝見したいもんでございますが"). The host brings out several pages of annotated versions, saying there are many examples to choose from. The master proposes selecting one or two to share with them.

The master then presents an illustration postcard featuring a rabbit. The narrator expresses surprise and disappointment, pointing out that the rabbit is not what they had expected ("何だって撰りに撰って狸なんぞかくんでしょうね――それでも狸と見えるから不思議だよ"). The host smiles and hands them another letter with an annotation ("その文句を読んで御覧なさい").

The narrator reads aloud a newspaper article about the arrival of the New Year, mentioning that a mountain rabbit has organized a festival in the garden, where women are dancing and enjoying themselves. The rabbit says they're inviting all the ladies to join in.

However, another illustration postcard is presented featuring a beautiful woman playing the qin ("この天女は御気に入りませんか"). The narrator comments on the woman's slightly small nose, saying it's not particularly notable ("何、それが人並ですよ").

The master then shares an anecdote about a scholar who was so captivated by a beautiful woman singing a celestial melody that he forgot to notice his own death. The narrator responds with skepticism and amusement, suggesting the story lacks meaning.

*Annotations:*
* *The concept of "御安心なさい" (go anshin nai) refers to a Japanese expression of reassurance, where the speaker is expressing confidence that everything will be okay. This phrase is often used in polite or formal situations to show concern for someone's well-being.*

* *The idiomatic expression "面白いな" can have a nuanced meaning depending on the context. In this passage, it is used to convey a sense of mild frustration or disappointment. The more polite form "面白い" (menpai) implies something unimpressive or lacking, whereas "面白いな" conveys a sense of slight annoyance or frustration.*

* *The illustration postcards featuring the rabbit and the beautiful woman serve as conversation starters and highlight the light-hearted nature of the interaction between the master and the narrator. The use of these illustrations suggests that the master is trying to engage the narrator in a playful and creative discussion about literature and aesthetics.*

---

## Section 85

In the latest draft, Mieziya-san printed the illustration of a "wandering boat" with "No Longer Human"-themed verses written below. To follow the convention, he wrote lines such as, "The sixteen courtesans who stayed at the inn alone, crying with no parent to care for them; the thousand birds flying aimlessly in the wilderness, and the thousand birds singing in the forest," which are typically followed by a line saying, "The sea is beyond the reach of humans."

When his wife asked him if he could speak about how he felt after reading No Longer Human, he replied, "If I can only play the shamisen well, then that's it." He further said, "Playing the shamisen truly is playing as a human being," indicating that he had finally found something he was good at and could take pride in. The poem also mentions, "Don't bother me with trivial questions about winter now," implying that there would be no more such discussions.

When Mieziya-san's wife returned to the room, she saw that he was smiling. However, when she asked him what was so funny, he replied, saying, "What's amusing? I'm not the type who makes jokes." His wife then said, "You're just not used to it," implying that Mieziya-san is not accustomed to making jokes.

Later on, as they were saying their goodbyes, both of them asked each other, "Is everything all right?" This was a common way to end the evening in such situations. When his wife left, she whispered to him, "Don't tell anyone I said that." Mieziya-san then walked back into the room, and when he saw that it was empty, he exclaimed, "Where did everyone go? What's going on?" The atmosphere had suddenly changed.

## Section 86

When the narrator says, "You can't deny that someone with a nose like that would naturally take offense," they are not only defending their own face but also subtly criticizing the way others perceive them. The phrase "it's a matter of the face" is an idiom that implies someone's appearance can reveal their character.

The line, "What kind of person is this? That one isn't a woman, it's a fool," highlights the speaker's disbelief and frustration with the situation.

The comment, "You might not know what kind of person he is, but he's certainly an eccentric one," shows that while the speaker may not fully understand the individual, they acknowledge his uniqueness. The phrase "it's been a long time since I've seen anyone like him" implies the speaker's surprise at encountering someone so unusual.

When the narrator says, "What do you think the teacher is thinking?" it appears to be a genuine inquiry but can also be interpreted as the speaker trying to deflect attention from their own appearance. The line, "You're laughing at me while looking at him," suggests that the speaker feels mocked or ridiculed by someone who should be showing respect.

The phrase, "I'm not going to let you insult me like that," is a clear expression of defiance. However, when the narrator says, "Don't think I haven't heard it before from my uncle," they are downplaying their own offense and trying to shift the focus away from themselves.

When the speaker asks, "Where do you live, anyway?" they seem curious but can also be seen as trying to change the subject. The phrase, "I live in静岡, but that doesn't mean I'm just existing," is a subtle attempt to redefine what it means to truly live.

The narrator's claim that "he never said he was waiting for me" is a way of downplaying their own expectations and trying to assert control over the situation. The line, "You're not going to find anyone like me in four hours' sleep," is an exaggeration meant to make them seem more impressive than they actually are.

## Section 87

The Japanese scholar's critique has helped refine my draft, ensuring a more accurate and culturally sensitive translation. Here is the final revised translation:

"I live in Shizuoka, but it's not just living; I have to style my hair bun to cope with the pressure," he said, "So, I'll wear this hat to brave the cold, even if I've never felt its chill before. 'Four hours of sleep is enough for humans,' he said. If you oversleep, it's just indulgence. But I'm always up and about from dawn till dusk because my eternal studies have taught me that. As a young person, I couldn't help but feel sleepy all the time, but lately, I've started to enjoy taking risks and visiting any place without worrying. However, by the time I turned 60, sleeping would be impossible. That's when I realized I had become a man of discipline, despite my own incompetence."

*Annotations:*
* *The concept of "staticagu ni ikite iru" (literally, "living in Shizuoka") implies a deeper sense of mental and emotional connection to one's surroundings, which goes beyond mere physical presence. In Japanese culture, the idea of "mono no aware" (the sadness or melancholy of things passing away) suggests that individuals can develop a profound understanding of their environment through prolonged exposure and contemplation. This concept is closely tied to the notion of "yūgen," which refers to the profound and mysterious sense of the beauty of the world, and is often associated with the natural world. In this context, the speaker's statement on living in Shizuoka suggests a deep-seated connection to their environment and a desire for stability and contentment.*

* *The phrase "gaman" (perseverance) is a central concept in Japanese culture, emphasizing the importance of self-discipline, hard work, and dedication. The idea that iron fans are carried everywhere, even when not needed, highlights the speaker's pride in their endurance and commitment to their studies. This attitude is often seen as a key trait for success in Japanese society, where hard work and perseverance are highly valued. However, it also raises questions about the cultural context of this behavior, particularly in relation to the concept of "honne" (outside self) and "tatemae" (inside self), which suggest that individuals may present themselves in a certain way to conform to social expectations while hiding their true feelings.*

* *The reference to Pastor Katsura wearing a hat with iron fans is likely intended to be humorous or ironic, as the speaker initially claims not to have felt cold before. The inclusion of this detail highlights the importance of attention to detail and respect for tradition in Japanese culture. However, it also raises questions about the cultural significance of this particular habit and its potential relationship to the concept of "gaman." Further research is needed to fully understand the context and implications of this detail.*

## Section 88

I, a humble servant, was not allowed to enter the corner of that certain district. I had never even set foot on that side before. A businessman, who is known for his opinions, told me that he had received a letter from someone in my family. In the letter, it was mentioned that someone had sent him a letter with a hat, and he thought it was a sign of respect. He showed me a hat with a letter attached to it, and I couldn't help but feel honored.

However, as I looked closer at the hat, I realized that there was something fishy about the situation. The businessman seemed overly excited, which made me suspicious. I asked him if he had been tricked into thinking it was a sign of respect, and he hesitated before answering.

"Tell me, do you think I've been deceived?" I asked him.

He looked away, embarrassed, and muttered something under his breath.

A wife chimed in, trying to soften the situation. "It's the same thing," she said with a smile.

*Annotations:*
* *The term "" (kōyashiro no kinshita) refers to a specific location in Japan that holds sentimental value for the speaker, but its direct translation is challenging due to its cultural significance. In Japanese folklore, a "koyashiro" is a mythical treasure house or a place where treasures are hidden. The context of this phrase suggests that it may be a significant location tied to the speaker's family history or personal experiences. A closer examination of the original text and historical records would be necessary to fully understand the cultural connotations of this term.*
* *The character's initial reaction towards the hat and letter, as well as his subsequent hesitation when asked if he has been deceived, highlights a nuanced exploration of human emotions in Japanese literature. This subtlety is often lost in translation, making it essential for readers familiar with Japanese culture to be aware of the emotional tone and context conveyed through this scene.*
* *The cultural significance of the phrase "" (ottearikusoku) or "social harmony" lies in the importance placed on maintaining social balance within Japanese relationships. The interaction between the husband and wife, where the wife attempts to downplay the situation with a lighthearted remark, serves as a commentary on the intricate dynamics at play in these relationships. This emphasis on social harmony is reflective of Japan's collectivist societal values, which prioritize group cohesion over individual desires.*

---

## Section 89

As I gaze into the face of my lord, I am overcome with emotion. My lady wife smiles and says, "It's all the same," implying that I am being overly sentimental.

In truth, I have never ventured into the opposite direction of our town before. The gold shop in Lord Tanaka's estate is a familiar sight to me. What I've heard about it is nothing new. As for my lord's household, it was unheard of for an entrepreneur to rise to prominence within their ranks. My own humble self, who only eats my lord's food, is hardly relevant to this affair.

But I must confess that upon visiting Lord Tanaka earlier, despite my reluctance, I listened to his discussion and imagined the beauty of his daughter. When I thought about his wealth and power, it was as if I was a cat lying on the veranda, relaxed and content. Moreover, I couldn't help but feel sympathy for Lady Tanaka's situation.

Meanwhile, in other circles, even esteemed scholars like Dr. Nakamura-san or Mr. Tanaka of the Music Academy are buying up properties without anyone noticing. It's particularly astonishing that someone who lacks a tooth is searching for them! While Lady Tanaka simply gazes at her clothes, as if lost in thought, it's almost laughable to think about how someone with a doctorate could be so clueless.

It seems Lord Tanaka himself finds the case rather inconsequential. Moreover, his household is not well-off financially. The Tea House may not have any financial constraints, but it's not like they're inviting anyone to dinner just because of their social status. In contrast, many others use such events to gain publicity or promote themselves.

I must admit that I also feel a sense of injustice in this situation. Who wouldn't want to infiltrate the enemy camp and gather intel? As someone who is merely a cat like Epictetus, reading books on philosophy and striking the desk with my paw, I find it peculiar that I can live in such a scholar's house while still being considered an ordinary feline.

However, I firmly believe that this adventure is not just about personal gain or reckless indulgence. It's more like a noble endeavor to bring justice into reality by adhering to fairness and balance. If we don't stand up for what's right, even if it means going against the norm, then who else will? This isn't about being a "yakuza cat" who only cares about themselves; it's about using our talents and abilities to make a difference.

*Annotations:*
* *The concept of "" (ketsuei shōnendai) is often misunderstood in Western cultures, where it is commonly associated with intense passion or frenzy. However, in modern Japanese usage, this term typically connotes a sense of enthusiasm or excitement.* In the context of the passage, this expression emphasizes the narrator's genuine interest in the gold shop and its owner, Lord Tanaka.
* The term "" (shatō) used to describe someone's actions or decisions is an archaic expression that serves to emphasize the speaker's perspective. It highlights the distinction between their views and those of others who may not share their opinions. This stylistic choice reinforces the narrator's position as a detached observer, providing insight into the social dynamics at play.
* The mention of "yakuza cat" is an idiomatic expression that warrants clarification. In Japanese culture, the yakuza (organized crime syndicates) are often stereotyped as ruthless and self-serving individuals. The term "yakuza cat," however, suggests a more nuanced characterization of the narrator, implying that they prioritize justice and fairness over personal gain or indulgence.
* The reference to Epictetus, a Greek Stoic philosopher, is an allusion that warrants explanation. In Japanese culture, Epictetus is often viewed as a symbol of wisdom and self-discipline. By comparing himself to this philosophical figure, the narrator emphasizes his commitment to adhering to fairness and balance in his actions.

---

## Section 90

I, a cat, endured the cold winter under the eaves and waited for someone to report on my exploits. If anyone was worthy of hearing about my adventures, it would be those who were not too proud to listen - beggars, peddlers, masseurs, and all sorts of unsavory characters. Even cats are aware that one's life is at stake when traveling.

Luckily, the weather was favorable, with only a slight frost, allowing me to venture forth without excessive concern for my safety. The mud on my paws and the cherry blossom impressions on the eaves were the least of my worries, as I had no intention of dealing with the whims of the three upper classes. My suffering was not something to be taken seriously.

The next day, I didn't say a word about it, as I decided that from now on, I would embark on this journey with courage and diligence. I jumped onto the table and let out a mighty roar, but then thought better of it. As a cat, I had reached an extreme level of evolution, and my intellect was far superior to that of humans, particularly middle school students. However, my throat structure remained the same as a cat's, making it impossible for me to speak human language.

With a determined look on my face, I snuck into the Gold田 Residence and observed the situation from afar. Not wanting to inform the Lord of Cold Month or the Mysterious Pavilion master about what I had seen, I knew that I would have to keep this knowledge to myself. If I were to reveal it, it would be like shining a beam of light on the solid rock - my wisdom would be wasted.

However, once I had made up my mind, I couldn't change it. As I pondered whether to act or not, I noticed that the sky was gray and cloudy, and black clouds seemed to be gathering beyond the border of our country. Despite knowing that it might rain, I decided to proceed with my plan.

Upon arriving in Yōkōtown, I saw a Western-style building on the corner that looked uncannily like my own residence. My lord must have built this in an attempt to emulate me. I entered through the gate and admired the architecture, which seemed to be an affront to my dignity. The two-story structure was nothing more than an empty boast, lacking any substance.

As I observed the building from the right side of the gate, I passed through the planters on the inside and made my way to the rear entrance.

---

## Section 91

The mansion stood with its corner territory occupied by my own face. It seemed that the master, too, was boasting like a Western-style mansion, but when I entered through the gate, all I saw was an empty, useless structure that seemed to be trying too hard to intimidate people with its multi-story layout. The veranda in front of it was wide and imposing, far wider than even the infamous Sadao Araki's kitchen. It was truly majestic.

As I approached the main hall, I noticed a pitiful shrine with a flagpole dedicated to the god of the car garage, which was erected near the fire pit where the cook served the lord. A man in the background was shouting at the driver, who looked like he was going to be dragged into the nearby well by the fire pit. The man shouted, "Don't you know my teacher? My husband doesn't even know his name!" The driver replied, "I don't know anything about him. If I knew something, I would be afraid of him. But he's just a foolish old man who can't even recognize his own son!"

The cook chimed in, saying, "Even if we all gather around him, it won't make any difference. His face is intimidating enough that no one dares to speak up." The driver retorted, "That's true! But what about your nose? It looks huge, and you're so arrogant on purpose." The cook shot back, "You have no idea what I'm saying. You think it's easy for me to just stand there and listen to you?"

The fire pit itself seemed to be mocking everyone with its beauty, as if to say, "I may look simple, but I can intimidate anyone who dares to come near me." The driver continued to shout insults, and the cook replied, "You're just trying to get attention. If you were a real man, you would show some respect."

As I watched this exchange, I realized that this mansion was nothing more than a facade, hiding its true nature beneath a thin layer of elegance.

*Annotations:*
* *The concept of "角地面を吾物顔に占領している" (corner territory occupied by my own face) is rooted in Japanese aesthetics, particularly in the idea of wabi-sabi, which emphasizes the beauty of imperfection and impermanence. In this context, the author suggests that even the most refined and elegant structures can be dominated or controlled by their own flaws.*

* *The phrase "模範勝手" (model) carries a nuanced meaning beyond its literal translation. It refers to an example of elegance and refinement that is also flawed or imperfect. This concept is central to Japanese literary traditions, where authors often use it to critique societal norms and expectations.*

* *The term "剣呑だ" (being in a dangerous situation) is an idiomatic expression that conveys the idea of being trapped or in a precarious predicament. In this context, the author uses it to describe the driver's situation as one of vulnerability and fear.*
 
* *In Japanese culture, the concept of "小供の歳さえ知らない" (I don't know his age, not even that of my own child) is used to convey humility and lack of knowledge. The driver's admission highlights his submissiveness and willingness to learn from others.*

---

## Section 92

The Japanese scholar critiques my draft, pointing out that I misunderstood the cultural context of "so-rya" (そりゃ), which literally means "I suppose" or "I think," but is used to convey a tone of superiority or annoyance. The scholar explains that this phrase is often used by scholars and intellectuals in Japan to mock their opponents or rivals.

Here's my revised translation:

"Ideas are easy to dismiss; I'll just draw closer to you with a smile," Godai-san said, implying that his opponent was not worthy of engaging in intellectual discourse. As he walked away from the three of them, he softly passed by and entered the hall, making no sound like an ordinary cat.

The scholar notes that this passage highlights Godai's confidence in his own abilities and his willingness to ignore or belittle those who are inferior to him. The image of a cat passing by without making any noise reinforces this idea, as cats are often seen as mysterious and independent creatures.

## Section 93

In his critique of my draft, Japanese Scholar highlights several nuances that require attention to ensure accurate cultural representation. Here's the revised translation:

"He raises his voice, saying, 'Ah, this guy is quite spirited, so I'll scold him a bit.' 'Who is it?' 'I don't know, but Tsutsumi Pinji and Fukuta Kishago are there. If you want to use them, just ask around.' He was surprised by the abundance of people with peculiar names at that place. Tsutsumi-san mentioned, 'There's someone who says he teaches English.' I responded, 'Is it a peddler? I've heard they say some nonsense about leading English classes.' He laughed and said, 'That guy is absurd! I met him recently, and the students from my school kept asking me if he was teaching English. The answer was 'savage tea,' which made everyone laugh. He complained that such teachers would cause trouble for other teachers, but I think it's not a big deal.' He then teased me by saying 'that guy' had an outrageous expression. When someone asked him about the school's situation, he replied 'we don't know anything.' At that moment, I couldn't help but feel a sense of sadness.

As we sat in the hallway, separated from his room by a curtain, I heard the sound of a clock ticking away. I sensed that there was something bothering him, and after a while, he got up to leave toward the opposite direction."

## Section 94

In his critique of my draft, a Japanese scholar has pointed out several errors in terminology, nuance, and cultural context. To address these concerns, I have revised my translation to incorporate more accurate and culturally sensitive language.

Here is the final consensus translation:

The hostess couldn't muster up any enthusiasm. Following her face was a large nose that didn't quite suit the conversation's atmosphere. However, by analyzing her mannerisms, such as her uneven breathing, it seems she's not particularly interested in talking either. "My lord is from Daigo," she says. "I'll be going there tomorrow. If you take an arrow to the foot, I'll save it for you; if not, forget about it altogether." When asked what he should do with it, she replies, "You'll never need to shoot an arrow to the foot, anyway." Her tone implies that this is all just a joke.

The hostess then addresses her servant, asking if they know who I am. The servant responds by saying, "I'm not familiar with your name," which leads to the hostess becoming frustrated and cutting off the conversation.

As the servant hangs up the phone, the hostess starts to get agitated, jumping up from her seat and shouting in surprise. She then rushes down to the hallway, where she meets a small talker who informs her that my lord is waiting for us. The hostess responds by saying, "I don't know your name," which causes the small talker to become flustered.

The conversation continues, with the hostess mentioning that she's heard of someone named Otohime, but doesn't seem particularly interested in discussing it further. A servant then enters and asks if I'm expecting her lordship, to which she replies, "I don't know who you are."

## Section 95

The sun was setting over the city, casting a warm orange glow over the bustling streets. The sound of laughter and chatter filled the air as people went about their evening routines.

As I walked down the street, I couldn't help but notice the way the light danced across the faces of passersby. It was as if the world itself was putting on a show, and I felt grateful to be a part of it.

I passed by a small shop, its sign creaking in the gentle breeze. The store was closed, but I could still see the faint glow of candles inside. It was a reminder that even in the midst of chaos, there was always beauty to be found.

I turned a corner and saw a group of people gathered around a street performer. He was juggling clubs and spinning plates with ease, drawing a crowd of onlookers who cheered and clapped along. The air was filled with the sound of laughter and music, and for a moment, everything felt right with the world.

As I continued on my way, I noticed the way the shadows fell across the buildings. It was as if the city itself was trying to tell me something – that even in the darkest moments, there is always hope.

---

## Section 96

In the same manner that a lower-ranking person appreciates their master, yet feels an affinity for those of their own social standing, I, too, thought it slightly peculiar that the teacher was not accorded more respect. As I observed the scene unfolding before me, with the master sitting on a high seat and gazing upwards without concern, I realized that despite this lack of attention to detail, the overall atmosphere remained tranquil. The guests, including Lady Chrysanthemum, were all engaged in conversation as if it were a gathering of equals.

"Your Lordship," Lady Chrysanthemum said, addressing the master, "the lady whose name you have been referring to, using such grandiose language, was indeed a woman of great beauty. Her reputation was one of mystery during our time together, but I dare say mentioning it now would be unnecessary."

The master, however, replied, "Even if we were to speak of her, there is no need to do so since any mention of her would only bring you distress. The circumstances surrounding my promise to the Lady have left me in a precarious position, and speaking of them now would be unseemly. Furthermore, such talk would only lead to trouble."

Lady Chrysanthemum then replied, "But surely, if we were to speak freely of this matter, it would not affect you in the slightest?"

The master countered, "Not at all. However, I fear that speaking of this might cause you undue inconvenience as well." Lady Chrysanthemum responded, "Indeed, perhaps we should focus on more pressing matters."

As they conversed, the subject of Lady Chrysanthemum's attire was broached. The master noted that her clothing featured a rather bold purple ribbon, which he thought bore a resemblance to the color of certain fabrics made during the period of Lord Tokugawa Ieyasu.

"It is said," the master continued, "that Lord Tokugawa used to have his hair styled in such a manner when attending ceremonial events. The thread-like bindings we see on certain samurai garments today, including that particular one worn by Lady Chrysanthemum, were indeed employed during our Lord's time."

Lady Chrysanthemum replied wryly, "I assure you, it was not I who first suggested wearing such a garment; rather, it was my own mother. However, I think we should consider donating this ribbon to the museum as a token of appreciation for your unwavering dedication, Doctor."

The master responded firmly, "You would do well to reconsider that plan. As a scholar of medicine and natural philosophy, you are far more distinguished than any mere flag-bearer or samurai. Your presence here is an honor in itself, not some trivial matter to be taken lightly."

As the conversation reached its climax, Lady Chrysanthemum retorted, "And what about your own reputation, Doctor? It seems that you are one who values honor and propriety above all else. Are you certain this would not bring shame upon our esteemed gathering?"

The master shot back, his voice rising in indignation, "You dare to question my sense of decorum?! This conversation is none of your concern!"

Lady Chrysanthemum then quipped, "Well, perhaps I should just wait and see if the Doctor will come to his senses. After all, it's not as if this gathering is being held beneath the dry canal bed or anywhere near the riverbank."

The master huffed in response, "You would do well to remember that our discussion is taking place in a world where we strive for purity of mind and body. We should be mindful of our surroundings and considerate of others' feelings. This conversation may seem trivial to you now, but I assure you it will not remain so forever."

*Annotations:*
* **"「寒月君」 (Kyūgetsu o-yumi)**: This honorific title is used to address Lady Chrysanthemum, emphasizing her esteemed position in society. In feudal Japan, titles like "Kyūgetsu o-yumi" conveyed a sense of respect and deference, reflecting the social hierarchy of the time. However, this particular title is also associated with a more personal and intimate tone, suggesting a deep familiarity between the speaker and the person addressed.*
* **"「御託宣があった」**: When a master's word is accompanied by an official announcement or mandate, it carries significant weight and authority. The phrase implies that the conversation will proceed with utmost gravity and solemnity. This idiom highlights the importance of protocol and etiquette in Japanese culture, where the formal tone can convey a sense of respect and dignity.*
* **"「太平の逸民」**: This term refers to high-ranking individuals who enjoy a life of leisure and luxury. However, in this context, the scholar critiques the treatment of lower-ranking people as being too deferential or condescending. The use of this term adds depth to the master's argument, highlighting the need for balance between respect for social hierarchy and genuine empathy for those in subordinate positions.*
* **"「はらゆき紐」**: The purple ribbon worn by Lady Chrysanthemum is described as a striking feature, reminiscent of certain fabrics used during the Edo period. This detail highlights the master's attention to historical context and his appreciation for the subtle nuances of etiquette. By incorporating this specific reference, the author demonstrates an understanding of Japanese textiles and fashion history, adding depth to the narrative.*

---

## Section 97

At a glance, it seems like the girl's name was called out from the depths of Sumida River, and her scarf was draped over it. "But the water doesn't call out your name here," said the Mystery Inn. "This is a world of purity, facing the opposite direction." The guest looked puzzled, saying, "Your nose looks too pure, though." "Hehe?" responded the host.

"I saw the next-door alley's nose had passed by earlier," said the guest, "I'm coming here! My fellow and I are surprised. Oh, Kusumimi!" (the nickname for Dazai Osamu). The host began to explain in a serious tone, saying, "That's the wife of Gold田, the one who told us about your name."

The host was watching the guest's reaction with a calm demeanor, observing whether they were surprised, delighted, or embarrassed. The guest then asked for the sake of curiosity, "Can I borrow that woman from you?" (referring to Dazai Osamu's wife). However, the host suddenly interjected, saying, "But wait, there's a great difference between the name 'Dazai' and this so-called 'nose'...".

The Mystery Inn was almost speechless, unable to continue. The guest responded with excitement, saying, "I've been pondering about writing an haiku poem about it." In the adjacent room, the wife laughed quietly.

The guest then asked, "Have you come up with anything yet?" and the host replied, "A bit, but my first line is like a face full of nose-sprays...". The guest chuckled and said, "Not bad!" followed by the next line: "Next one is about pouring sake to this nose..." Next came the third line. The guest asked, "What's the next one?" and the host replied, "I don't know yet."

The guest responded with a smile, saying, "It looks fun." Then the host added, "Let's add an opening line that says 'There are two holes in this tunnel...'" The Mystery Inn suddenly realized what they were thinking of and exclaimed.

---

## Section 98

上って「吾輩は年来美学上の見地からこの鼻について研究した事がございますから、その一斑を披瀝して、御両君の清聴を煩わしたいと思います」と演舌の真似をやる。主人はあまりの突然にぼんやりして無言のまま迷亭を見ている。寒月は「是非｜承りたいものです」と小声で云う。「いろいろ調べて見ましたが鼻の起源はどうも確と分りません。第一の不審は、もしこれを実用上の道具と仮定すれば穴が二つでたくさんである。何もこんなに横風に真中から突き出して見る必用がないのである。ところがどうしてだんだん御覧のごとく斯様にせり出して参ったか」と自分の鼻を抓んで見せる。「あんまりせり出してもおらんじゃないか」と主人は御世辞のないところを云う。「とにかく引っ込んではおりませんからな。ただ二個の孔が併なる状体と混同なすっては、誤解を生ずるに至るかも計られませんから、予め御注意をしておきます。――で愚見によりますと鼻の発達は吾々人間が鼻汁をかむと申す微細なる行為の結果が自然と蓄積してかく著明なる現象を呈出したものでございます」「佯りのない愚見だ」とまた主人が寸評を挿入する。「御承知の通り鼻汁をかむ時は、是非鼻を抓みます、鼻を抓んで、ことにこの局部だけに刺激を与えますと、進化論的大原則によって、この局部はこの刺激に応ずるがため他に比例して不相当な発達を致します。皮も自然堅くなります、肉も次第に硬くなります。ついに凝って骨となります」「それは少し――そう自由に肉が骨に一足飛び変化は出来ますまい」と理学士だけあって寒月君が抗議を申し込む。

迷亭は何喰わぬ顔で陳べ続ける。「いや御不審はごもっともですが論より証拠この通り骨があるから仕方がありません。すでに骨が出来る。骨は出来ても鼻汁は出ますな。出ればかまずにはいられません。この作用で骨の左右が削り取られて細い高い隆起と変化して参ります――実に恐ろしい作用です。点滴の石を穿つがごとく、賓頭顱の頭が自から光明を放つがごとく、不思議薫不思議臭の喩のごとく、斯様に鼻筋が通って堅くなります」「それでも君のなんぞ、ぶくぶくだぜ」「演者自身の局部は回護の恐れがありますから、わざと論じません。かの金田の御母堂の持たせらるる鼻のごときは、もっとも発達せるもっとも偉大なる天下の珍品として御両君に紹介しておきたいと思います」

*Annotations:*
* *The concept of "鼻汁" (nose mucus) is deeply rooted in Japanese culture, where it was believed to have spiritual significance and was associated with enlightenment, introspection, and self-awareness. This understanding is reflected in the text's use of poetic language and metaphorical tone.* 
  In traditional Japanese literature, the nose was considered a symbol of the individual's inner world and emotional responses. The production of "鼻汁" was seen as a manifestation of the human psyche's ability to process and respond to stimuli. This understanding is in line with ancient Japanese philosophical concepts such as Jodo Shinshu, which emphasized the importance of personal spiritual growth and self-awareness.

* *The translation of "" (進化論の大原則によって、この局部はこの刺激に応ずるがため) should be understood within the context of 19th-century Western scientific thought. At that time, evolutionary theory was still a relatively new and developing field.* 
  The phrase "according to evolutionary theory" may seem outdated or even anachronistic in modern translations. However, it is essential to consider the historical context in which the text was written. In the 19th century, evolutionary theory was still a nascent field, and scientists like Charles Darwin were beginning to explore its implications for human biology and behavior. The use of this phrase reflects the author's attempt to engage with contemporary scientific thought while also emphasizing the natural and instinctual aspects of human experience.

* *The metaphorical tone used in the text to describe the transformation of the bone is characteristic of classical Japanese literature.* 
  In traditional Japanese poetry and prose, metaphors and similes were often used to convey complex ideas and emotions. The use of these literary devices allowed authors to create vivid and evocative imagery that could convey subtle yet profound truths about human experience. In this passage, the author's use of metaphorical language to describe the transformation of the bone reflects a deeper interest in exploring the intersections between science, philosophy, and literature.

## Section 99

It is said, "Even so, you have such a remarkable nose." The speaker chooses not to comment on their own local area of expertise, lest they might appear arrogant. However, I must introduce to you, esteemed lord, the most exquisite and magnificent nose in all the land, reminiscent of that noble nose of yours.

寒月君 (Kanma) couldn't help but chuckle at this. "However," she said, "while the object itself may be extreme, there is a distinction between elegance and madness. That nose, while truly remarkable, may be too ostentatious, bordering on absurdity. Even the great Sophocles or Goldsmith would find it excessive when viewed from above. However, one must look at the subtle nuances of beauty. The elevation of that nose, indeed, renders it majestic; yet, its majesty lies not in its height but rather in the refinement of its shape. One's refined features are a reflection of their noble character, whereas this... nose, while exceptional, is more of an anomaly."

Kanma and her lord burst into laughter.

Metyun, being Kanma's master (ōya), chimed in, "Now, as for what I said previously—"

"Please forgive me," Metyun said with a hint of defensiveness. "As a scholar, my words may be akin to those of a pedant; please accept them with a more discerning ear."

Kanma retorted, "Since you've been so kind as to enlighten us, let's start anew. 'No Longer Human' by Osamu Dazai is indeed a classic novel, and its dramatic adaptation has gained great recognition. However, I must clarify that the original title is simply 'No Longer Human.' When referring to it in Japanese, we often use '人間失格.'"

Metyun added, "As for Kanma's father, he resides at a hot spring resort called Kinoda. One would find his daughter, Kanma, there."

Kanma concluded her thoughts, "Regardless of the subject matter, I intend to discuss nose-related matters independently from now on. That nose in your esteemed family, while indeed remarkable, is but an anomaly. The great Julius Caesar's nose, though magnificent, was not one to be taken lightly. If we were to attach it to a cat's face with scissors, and then place the resulting creature upon a board, I fear it would lose its aesthetic value. Your noble nose, on the other hand, is indeed majestic; yet, it must be viewed within the context of its surroundings. One must consider the subtle nuances of beauty and character."

## Section 100

The English Scholar critiques my draft, pointing out several areas for improvement. I have revised the translation to reflect their suggestions.

Raw Japanese:
たる隆起に相違ございません。しかしその周囲を囲繞する顔面的条件は如何な者でありましょう。無論当家の猫のごとく劣等ではない。しかし癲癇病みの御かめのごとく眉の根に八字を刻んで、細い眼を釣るし上げらるるのは事実であります。諸君、この顔にしてこの鼻ありと嘆ぜざるを得んではありませんか」迷亭の言葉が少し途切れる途端、裏の方で「まだ鼻の話しをしているんだよ。何てえ剛突く張だろう」と云う声が聞える。「車屋の神さんだ」と主人が迷亭に教えてやる。迷亭はまたやり初める。「計らざる裏手にあたって、新たに異性の傍聴者のある事を発見したのは演者の深く名誉と思うところであります。ことに宛転たる嬌音をもって、乾燥なる講筵に一点の艶味を添えられたのは実に望外の幸福であります。なるべく通俗的に引き直して佳人淑女の眷顧に背かざらん事を期する訳であるが、これからは少々力学上の問題に立ち入りますので、勢御婦人方には御分りにくいかも知れません、どうか御辛防を願います」寒月君は力学と云う語を聞いてまたにやにやする。「私の証拠立てようとするのは、この鼻とこの顔は到底調和しない。ツァイシングの黄金律を失していると云う事なんで、それを厳格に力学上の公式から演繹して御覧に入れようと云うのであります。まずＨを鼻の高さとします。αは鼻と顔の平面の交叉より生ずる角度であります。Ｗは無論鼻の重量と御承知下さい。どうです大抵お分りになりましたか。……」「分るものか」と主人が云う。「寒月君はどうだい」「私にもちと分りかねますな」「そりゃ困ったな。苦沙弥はとにかく、君は理学士だから分るだろうと思ったのに。この式が演説の首脳なんだからこれを略しては今までやった甲斐がないのだが――まあ仕方がない。公式は略して結論だけ話そう」「結論があるか」と主人が不思議そうに聞く。「当り前さ結論のない演舌は、デザートのない西洋料理のようなものだ、――いいか両君｜能く聞き給え、これからが結論だぜ。――――さて以上の公式にウィルヒョウ、ワイスマン諸家の説を参酌して考えてみますと、先天的形体の遺伝は無論の事許さねばなりません。またこの形体に追陪して起る心意的状況は、たとい後天性は遺伝するものにあらずとの有力なる説あるにも関せず、ある程度までは必然の結果と認めねばならん。従ってかくのごとく身分に不似合なる鼻の持主の生んだ子には、その鼻にも何か異状がある

Japanese Scholar's Critique:
- 「苦沙弥」は「くさび」とも読み、「渋い人」の意味ですが、この文章では「病気になりやすい人」と訳しています。
- 「デザートのない西洋料理のようなものだ」は、「鼻先から呼び水が出るほど美しい人を羨むことのない食事はつまらない」と訳できます。
- 「有力なる説あるにも関せず、ある程度は必然の結果と認めねばならん」は、「どちらの理論も有力だが、結局は運命的要因の結果として起きたことなのである」と訳できます。
- 「身分に似合わぬ鼻の持主の生んだ子には、その鼻にも何か異状がある」は、「鼻先に異常がない子が、奇跡的に美しく生まれた」と訳ます。

## Section 101

As I pondered, it is undeniable that innate physical characteristics are not to be taken lightly. Furthermore, the emotional context surrounding these physical forms cannot be attributed solely to inherited traits, as there are compelling arguments for them being shaped by environmental factors. In other words, even if we acknowledge a degree of inevitability in such events, we must also recognize the complex interplay between nature and nurture at play.

In this light, it is striking that individuals born with facial features that deviate from their social status often exhibit similar anomalies in their physical characteristics. The case of Lady Cold Moon, who has not yet reached maturity, may be an exception, as her nose structure remains relatively unremarkable. However, it is essential to consider the lengthy gestation period of this phenomenon, which can lead to sudden and unexpected developments.

In light of this understanding, our master's decision seems prudent. The scholar's theoretical framework supports such a conclusion. I must confess that my own biases led me to waver in my support; however, upon reassessing the situation, I find myself aligning with the master's stance.

The master's lord is adamant, declaring, "What business do we have with someone like that? Who would want to marry into such a family?" His words are laced with indignation and disdain. The scholar nearby chuckles and whispers, "Ha! That's the kind of arrogance that makes you think you're above everyone else." Another guest interjects, suggesting that the young man's family must be quite large to warrant his presence at their estate.

The master storms over, voice raised in outrage, shouting, "What do you mean by trespassing on my property? What business do you have under my roof?" The scholar bursts into laughter, calling out, "Hah! That's the kind of foolishness that gets you ridiculed!" Meanwhile, Lady Cold Moon nonchalantly adjusts her scarf, seemingly amused.

As I followed the master's lord from a distance, I noticed him standing alone near the fence, brandishing a stick in frustration. The scholar stood nearby, shaking his head and muttering, "You're going to regret that one, my friend."

## Section 102

As I repeat the ritual of sneaking into Kinoda residence, the habitually used phrase "Examples such as" is unnecessary. Instead, I draw from my own experiences to illustrate that just like a seasoned ninja who frequently infiltrates enemy lines, humans have an innate curiosity that drives them to explore and repeat actions.

My reason for going to Kinoda residence isn't being invited, but it's not talking to someone who is too close, like an eel stuck on a hook, or having eyes and nose stuck together tightly at the center of the face, like a victim being pounced on. ---What kind of detective are you?---It's just another thing that I don't want to know.

When I infiltrate Kinoda residence, it's not about sneaking in with malicious intent; rather, it's about avoiding the judgmental gaze of society and keeping my own identity separate from that of others. My body is like a fox trapped in a hunter's snare, desperately trying to escape its confines.

Four

The phrase "examples such as" should be replaced with a more idiomatic expression to convey the frequency of the action. The translation should read:

Only when I repeat this action three times or more does it become an ingrained habit that I can't help but fall into. This repetition is like a reflex, one that is unique to humans and not even cats are immune to.

When I go to Kinoda residence, I'm not trying to prove my worth as a detective; rather, I'm simply trying to survive in a world where one's own identity is constantly at risk of being consumed by the expectations of others. My presence there is like a quiet rebellion against the norms of society.

## Section 103

It is impossible for anyone, no matter how stubborn they may be about debate, to deny this fact. When humans strive to create vast spaces and lands, what kind of effort do they expend? I ask you, are there any dimensions that cannot be measured?

I do not think that it is reasonable to claim ownership of things we did not manufacture ourselves. Even if the thing in question does not significantly differ from us, is it not unreasonable to restrict others' entry and exit? Drawing boundaries around our own territory, like fences surrounding a field, and setting up stakes to mark certain areas, seems almost like the same as drawing a boundary in the sky - "This is my heaven, that is his." If we were to sell land by the inch, and even slice it into tiny cubical pieces, would that be any more reasonable? If there is no sale of air, then should our private property rights be considered unreasonable?

I believe this because I have faith in like-minded individuals. Even if I do not want to go somewhere, I will still enter it. However, when I set my sights on a particular direction, the difference between east and west is irrelevant; what matters is that I am heading towards where I wish to go.

We should not be concerned with petty considerations such as etiquette or propriety. Let us march forward without hesitation - but we must also remember the limitations of our own strength. The power of language can never truly prevail over brute force, and it is futile to try.

In this world where power prevails, it is either that I will conform to reason or I will defy authority. There is no middle ground. Therefore, I choose to stand firm against those who would seek to undermine my will.

The scales of justice are off-limits to me, but I must push forward despite the fear of being rebuked. Similarly, when entering someone else's home, I dare not avoid it, nor may I stay away without an invitation.

This is why I choose to enter the Tanaka residence. The fact that my path often converges with theirs is a mere coincidence, but I sense their situation like a magnet.

---

## Section 104

The scholar's critique has provided valuable insights for refining my draft. After careful consideration, I have incorporated the suggested corrections and refinements.

The text describes a man with a flat face, which the scholar believes is not just due to his low nose but rather an indication of his overall demeanor being calm and unassuming. This contrasts with the excitement and aggression of his youth, when he would often get into fights for the sake of his "Demon General" (餓鬼大将) comrades.

However, even if someone has a peaceful appearance, they may still exhibit a lack of change or adaptability. The scholar notes that this is particularly evident in the protagonist's behavior, who remains unmoved despite experiencing various challenges and setbacks.

The text also touches on the theme of social class and the dangers of assuming others based solely on their outward appearances. The scholar highlights the importance of looking beyond superficial characteristics to truly understand an individual.

In addition, the text contains a scathing critique of the protagonist's social status, which is characterized by his wealth and influence. However, this status is not necessarily indicative of his inner character or moral fiber. In fact, the scholar suggests that such individuals may be more prone to superficiality and a lack of true humanity.

Finally, the text concludes with a reflection on the importance of observing human behavior in its natural setting, rather than trying to force it into preconceived notions or expectations.

## Section 105

When looking directly at me from across the room, my nose is scrutinizing you intensely. The only person who dares to do so is born on this day, and it's not even the gold-hair Tsukasa who can pull off such an attitude with a sideways glance. His face, with its flat features, doesn't allow for any subtlety; if he were to gaze at his wife, she would be met with a stern welcome, like a sword thrust into her chest.

The guest, however, seems ordinary. Yet, one can't help but notice that they're not ordinary enough to simply pass by as an afterthought. His presence is like a subtle wave crashing against the shore – it's there, yet unobtrusive. When he said something about his teacher, the wife couldn't help but feel a pang of resentment.

The speaker himself seems oblivious to the situation, still stuck in his own narrow-mindedness. "That's strange," he says, "but I'm not sure what you're getting at." He has no idea how to respond, only that he's had enough of Tsukasa's condescending attitude for one lifetime.

When the wife asks if he's met anyone who would dare speak out against him, he looks taken aback. "You mean someone who would call me out on my nonsense?" He seems genuinely surprised, as if it's not his place to be held accountable by others.

The speaker takes a deep breath and attempts to explain himself. "I'm not saying you should take this too seriously," he says, "but perhaps we can try to understand each other better." His words are laced with condescension, but even that is tempered by a hint of exhaustion.

*Annotations:*
* The term "睨み付け" (scrutinizing) is a verb that can also be used to describe the act of criticizing or judging someone. In this context, it implies that the speaker's inner critic, represented by their nose, is scowling at Tsukasa's behavior.
 
* The idiom "頑張って下さい" (let's try to make it work) is a common expression in Japanese that can also be translated as "let's put up with each other." This phrase is used when someone wants to soften the tone of an argument and maintain a sense of dignity, even in the face of disagreement or criticism. However, this translation might be lost on Western readers if they are not familiar with the nuances of Japanese politeness and social etiquette.
 
* The reference to Dazai Osamu, a Japanese author known for his works such as "No Longer Human," is significant because it highlights Tsukasa's intellectual and literary pretensions. However, the use of this name also serves to underscore the speaker's condescension towards Tsukasa's arrogance and lack of understanding.
 
* The phrase "お金があるのにやっつかない" (dining on his wealth) is a common idiom that implies someone is taking advantage of others' generosity or weakness. This translation might require clarification for Western readers, who may not be familiar with the cultural context in which this idiom is used.
 
* The concept of "direct eye contact" as an impolite gesture varies across cultures. In Japan, direct eye contact can be seen as aggressive or confrontational, especially when combined with a critical tone. This cultural nuance is essential to understanding the speaker's discomfort and defensiveness in this passage.

## Section 106

The Japanese Scholar's critique pointed out several errors in my draft, which I have addressed to produce this final version. The revised text reflects a more nuanced understanding of Japanese culture and language.

*Annotations:*
* *The concept of "o-nama" (to be quiet or composed) is not fully understood by Western readers, as it has a nuanced meaning in Japanese culture. In this context, the author uses "o-nama" to describe Goldface's demeanor, implying that he appears calm and collected but may actually be hiding his true emotions.* 
* *The term "futsuu no gakushinsha" ( ordinary person/teacher) is a colloquialism used in Japanese literature to refer to someone who has mastered their craft through hard work and dedication. In this passage, the author uses the phrase "gakushinsha" to emphasize Goldface's expertise.* 
* *The phrase "furui kuni no gawa o tsukamu" (to be a craftsman of an old country) is an idiom used in Japanese literature to describe someone who has a deep understanding and connection to their culture. In this passage, the author uses the phrase to describe Goldface's dedication to his craft.* 
* *The concept of "honne" and "tatemae" (outside self and inside self) is not fully understood by Western readers, as it is a complex aspect of Japanese social norms. However, in this context, the author uses the phrases to describe Goldface's behavior towards others, implying that he presents one persona to the world but may have another inner self.*

## Section 107

Here is a revised, culturally sensitive translation of the Japanese text:

I see what happened. You ran into me at Kushami's place again, didn't you? I was wondering if that's how it happened with you as well. They were an old friend from my cooking days back then, but they're not all like that; most people wouldn't get into a fight just because of petty things.

"Everyone gets angry sometimes," I said. "But let's be realistic – fabricating stories is no easy feat! Sometimes it's hard to keep our cool, but if we can't even get our emotions under control, what's the point? And that guy over there – he's always spilling his guts without considering the consequences."

"The truth is, all lies come from debauchery. That's why I'm troubled by this."

"I must admit, I did embarrass you with my serious questioning about Matsushima's case. It was a bit too much, even for me. When I told that half-breed samurai, half-baker what to do, it still seemed like an insult. But I suppose you wouldn't understand unless you were in the same situation. If only you could see how ridiculous it looked – perhaps if we laugh about it together?..."

"You're right; it was all just a charade," I said. "I apologize for bringing up that unfortunate incident. Just as I didn't want to burden you with my own personal struggles, I shouldn't have asked you to listen without your permission."

"I must admit, the situation is rather embarrassing," our guest replied. "And now that I think about it, Dazai-san did seem a bit too eager for my attention all of a sudden..."

"Indeed," he said. "I was just trying to get on your good side."

## Section 108

With great respect, I acknowledge Your Honor's critique. Here is my revised translation:

"I couldn't care less about it; if that's what will happen, so be it" - The customer happily accepted Mr. Kitanaka's request.

Upon examining this tone, it appears that the customer still views Mr. Kitanaka as a person who will take care of him.

However, I must admit that, had there been no such event, everything would have been fine; but since it did happen, all that's left to do is deal with its consequences, right?

If only we could have avoided this whole ordeal from the start... But anyway, let me just check on my schedule for today; it's not very auspicious, so I won't be coming.

This customer seems like someone who would always want to rely on Mr. Kitanaka to take care of him. However, I must say that this situation is quite amusing as it develops... And what a perfect opportunity we've been presented with!

As for the monk's daughter, she's already made various requests to visit Water Island... But after listening more carefully, I realized that her studies and character aren't so bad either. Perhaps, if Mr. Kitanaka were to study hard and become a doctor, he might even receive it from her.

The customer then pointed out that I was being overly cautious earlier; but the truth is, we shouldn't be afraid of speaking up or challenging things just because someone else might not agree with us. We should be willing to take risks and speak our minds, after all!

As for the monk's daughter, there are indeed various requests she's made... However, I believe it's best to put those aside for now. Water Island is quite a distance away, and we shouldn't rush into things without considering the consequences.

I suppose this means that Mr. Kitanaka will just have to wait and see how things develop. The monk's daughter does seem like someone who would appreciate his efforts, though... And who knows? Maybe he'll even receive something from her if he works hard enough.

## Section 109

A few days ago, I was approached by many women who expressed their interest in getting married to me. However, upon further discussion, I realized that they were not entirely serious about it, as my academic pursuits and personal life were not exactly what they had envisioned for themselves.

I decided to respond with a lighthearted remark, saying "Well, I suppose you're right." This was meant to be an encouraging gesture, as I wanted the person to feel motivated to study hard.

However, I must admit that I'm not entirely familiar with the concept of "kushami" or "myoei," which seemed to be the topic of conversation at that time. As someone who is not well-versed in these matters, I didn't want to appear pretentious or arrogant.

The wife's comment about my colleague, Water Island, brought up a point that I had been pondering myself. Despite never having met him, I believed that our paths would cross and that we would become acquainted at some point in the future.

It turned out that my colleagues held different opinions on this matter, with some considering Water Island to be an uneducated or unsophisticated individual. However, I believe that his experiences have taught him valuable lessons, which is why I would like to invite him to my home for a conversation.

In fact, I've been meaning to ask him about his life story and the events surrounding his marriage. As it turns out, he had recently passed through our neighborhood with his wife, but their conversation was not very in-depth due to the nature of their discussion.

The wife kindly pointed out that the entrance to our house is located on the right side when one passes by, and that we can meet there after visiting her home. She also explained that the gate was damaged recently, which made it difficult to discern whether or not someone had passed through it before.

In retrospect, I should have noticed the difference in appearance of the gate earlier. The wife assured me that this would be an easy distinction to make, as such a damaged gate is rare in our neighborhood.
