## Section 143

When Manyoro-san boasted about his hometown, Yamagata's potatoes were unlike Tokyo's. Finally, his sister understood the situation.
"Thank you very much for your kindness!" Manyoro-san's younger sister exclaimed.

"How was it? Did you see how toughly it was packed in a box?" her older brother asked. "It must have been there for a long time."

"But what if we were robbed last night?" he added, referring to the fact that Yamagata's potatoes had arrived in Tokyo only the previous day.

"Someone broke into our storage room!" Manyoro-san's sister replied, her voice trembling with shock.

When asked about the face of the intruder, Manyoro-san hesitated. "It was a scary face," he finally admitted.

His older brother and sisters couldn't help but express their sympathy for him. "It looks like you must have seen an ugly face," his sister said, concern etched on her voice.

"I didn't see anything ugly," Manyoro-san replied with a smirk, pushing the attention away. The younger sister noticed that Manyoro-san's hair was receding, and it had been one month since he had gotten it checked by a doctor. "Your hair is shining like your mother's!" she said, pointing out the obvious.

Manyoro-san's face turned red as he blushed. "I'm just joking!"

His older brother chimed in, "Well, your mom's hair does shine."

"Your mom? I was talking about my own hair," Manyoro-san replied, still trying to deflect attention.

The conversation continued until the family decided to take their children outside for a playtime, during which they could indulge in some treats prepared by the mother. When asked again about his receding hairline, Manyoro-san's younger sister simply said, "It was eaten by an insect." His older brother shot back with, "Well, if it were your head that had been attacked by an insect, it would be a bit more noticeable!" The older sister added, "I guess we should have taken better care of his scalp."

Manyoro-san's younger sister replied calmly, "It was eaten by an insect."

*Annotations:*
* **Note on Historical Context: Yamagata's Potatoes and Tokyo's Food Scene**
The passage highlights the differences in food quality between Yamagata, a region in northern Japan, and Tokyo. This contrast reflects the historical disparities in economic development and access to resources between these regions. During the post-war period, Yamagata was still recovering from the devastating effects of World War II, while Tokyo continued to thrive as a major urban center.
* **Footnote: The Significance of ""(saishū) in Japanese Social Customs**
The term ""(saishū) refers to the act of taking one's head off as a form of capital punishment. However, in this context, Manyoro-san's younger sister jokingly attributes his receding hairline to being "eaten by an insect" (, koei). This lighthearted response is meant to deflect attention from the potentially sensitive topic of death. The use of "" in a non-lethal sense showcases its versatility and highlights the complexities of Japanese social norms.
* **Footnote: ""(yūwaku) and the Concept of Gossip**
The term ""(yūwaku) translates to "gossip" or "rumor-mongering." In this passage, Manyoro-san's younger sister jokingly attributes his hair loss to being eaten by an insect. This lighthearted remark is a form of gossip, but its tone is playful and non-accusatory, reflecting the complex social dynamics between siblings in Japanese families.
* **Note on Literary Device: The Use of ""(tsukamu) for Emphasis**
The word ""(tsukamu) can be translated to "to pack" or "to close," but it also implies a sense of containment or protection. In this passage, Manyoro-san's older brother asks about the face of the intruder, highlighting the sense of safety and security that is compromised by the break-in. The use of "" for emphasis underscores the emotional impact of the event on the family.

---

## Section 144

After careful review of the Japanese Scholar's critique, I have made significant revisions to ensure a more accurate and culturally nuanced translation.

"Ah, the length of your hair! It's not as if you're trying to be anything else. In English, we say 'thick' or 'lumpy.'"

"I'm afraid that's not quite right. There's a longer name for it, which I'd like to look up with your permission."

"If I ask Mr. [Scholar], he'll immediately understand it for us."

"But Mr. Scholar is too kind and doesn't want to teach me. That's why I'll ask you."

"I don't know what 'thick' or 'bumpy' means, but if the longer name starts with 'O-', perhaps that's it?"

"Ah, I think I see! The term 'otanchin palarogas' is used in Japan. 'Otanchin' is a synonym for 'thick,' and 'palarogas' refers to a type of hair growth on the scalp."

"I'm not sure if that's correct. Now, let me look up the definition in Mr. [Scholar]'s study room. But I'm afraid he'll only smile at your question because it's about women."

"Hehe, you're right! Some people still enjoy licking jam."

"Yes, some people do!"

"Mr. Scholar said that sometimes, someone might say, 'A certain dog is eating a certain cake,' but the speaker meant to say, 'The woman is eating the jam.'"

"It seems that way, doesn't it? Well, I suppose it's not just you, but Mrs. [Scholar] too."

"I see what you mean. Why would we know if someone has eaten something?"

"Because we can tell by looking at their face! (laughs) But let's focus on finding the correct answer."

"Yes, yes, the dog is quite fierce. It might be better to have a cat as our pet instead."

"But I don't think so. Cats are only good for eating insects, not dogs."

"Ha ha, I think we've been outsmarted by that clever dog! Did you know that cats love to eat rice?"

"No, but I do know they're quite fierce. Well, let me cook up some cat food now."

"That's a great idea!"

## Section 145

In a peculiar display of bravery, "Do you catch mice?" asked a cat. "No, I've never caught anything," replied the cat. "You're just a sly, rough-around-the-edges feline." The man chuckled, saying, "I'll believe it when I see it. If you can't even chase a mouse, what good are you?" The cat retorted, "Well, I suppose I could eat you instead." The man's response was lukewarm, but the cat persisted, saying, "Cook and eat me!" As the conversation continued, the man began to lose interest, while the cat remained fixated on being devoured.

The scene eventually shifted to a discussion of the social class of the scholar, who had once been a humble clerk. The narrator was surprised to learn that this renowned individual still considered himself a scholar despite already graduating from law school and working as a high-ranking official in a company.

## Section 146

In a quiet, unassuming corner of the room, Minoru sat waiting. "You can still make it work," he said. "What do you plan to do?" "Just eat and cook that up," his master replied bluntly. When their master spoke, his face twisted into an ugly grimace, as if his belly was protesting loudly. Despite this, no response came from the young Minoru, who simply looked on with a mixture of amusement and discomfort. 

Later, when their master spoke about cats, saying they were fine with anything but the fact that he had worn them to bed meant it must be freezing cold, Minoru's expression darkened. It was noticeable how much more uncomfortable he was, even though just yesterday he had layered two petticoats over his undergarments and now wore a simple undershirt, without ever having moved from his chair.

When their master spoke about teaching and being an official teacher, Minoru shook his head, saying that if he were to meet someone who was pickpocketing, it would be extremely inconvenient for him. Their master immediately scolded him for making such a remark. 

"Teacher is disliked, but the businessman is even more so," their master said with a wry smile.

"Is anything disliked by you?" Minoru asked hesitantly. "Nothing seems to be disliked by you at all." His master's response was somewhat enigmatic, and Minoru couldn't help but feel that there must be something beneath the surface that he wasn't quite seeing. 

Later on, when their master read a line from a poem Minoru had learned in school - 'Teachers are disliked, but businessmen are even more so' - Minoru's eyes widened slightly because he didn't quite understand it. His master remained silent.

"Teachers are disliked, but businessmen are even more so," the master said with a sly smile. "If you live as I do, then what's there to look forward to? Gold is just an empty, meaningless thing."

"Why don't you earn any money?" Minoru asked, curiosity getting the better of him.

"Only fifty cents was saved last year," their master replied matter-of-factly.

"How much was your monthly salary?"

The conversation continued in this vein.

## Section 147

A humble entrepreneur, such as myself, is hardly capable of amassing wealth through mere contrivances. "Wealth" can be earned by working hard for it, rather than simply being lucky.

"But a little luck never hurts." When questioned further about his earnings, the scholar replied that he had managed to save five sen.

"What's your monthly salary like?" The scholar asked, intrigued.

"It's thirty yen," I responded. "I save five sen every month, which my wife allows me to keep for emergency purposes. Once the timing is right, I'll use it to invest in stocks."

"Wouldn't that be a problem if someone stole it?" The scholar mused.

"Well, as an entrepreneur, one must always be prepared for that possibility," I replied dryly. "But since our instructor, Mr. Suzuki Fushiro, has experience working in the field of law and business, he's learned to navigate such situations."

"I see," the scholar said, nodding. "So, even if you're an entrepreneur like us, it's still essential to save some money."

"But let's be real," I added with a smirk, "Mr. Suzuki has been a leader in his field for over ten years and has become rather... eccentric." The scholar chuckled.

"Ah, how much do you make?" he asked again, curious about my financial situation.

"I earn two hundred fifty yen per month," I replied, "with an additional five sen from dividends. On average, my monthly income is around four hundred fifty yen."

The scholar laughed, "It seems that even the most high-minded individuals can't help but be drawn to wealth and material possessions."

## Section 148

The conversation begins with a visitor, who inquires about the host's acquaintance. The host responds positively, acknowledging the visitor as a kind guest.

The visitor then inquires about the host's character, to which the host replies that he is a man of great scholarship and intellect. The host asks if the visitor thinks him a good match for his daughter, and the visitor humorously suggests that someone like Multiple Samurai would be a more suitable suitor. The conversation takes a turn as the visitor mentions the possibility of earning a doctorate, and the host jokingly comments on how absurd it is to pursue such an achievement in order to secure a bride.

The conversation then shifts to the topic of the doctorate, with the visitor expressing surprise that someone as intelligent as Multiple Samurai would be reduced to writing a dissertation. The host responds by saying that such a person would indeed be capable of achieving great things, and the two engage in a lighthearted discussion about the nature of intelligence.

The conversation concludes with the host suggesting a walk to Ueno Park, where they will enjoy some traditional Japanese sweets together. The visitor agrees, and the host mentions that he hopes to relax soon, citing his need for rest due to the demands of his duties as a detective.

## Section 149

It is only proper to request a brief rest. My lord and Dōmariyama-san were enjoying themselves in Ueno Park, making merry and eating mochi at Ohashi, so there's no need for me to investigate further. Moreover, I lack the courage to follow them or tail them. Hence, I shall take a rest from now on.

Rest is an inherent right of all living beings, derived from our shared existence in this world. Those who are compelled to move and act have the duty to rest in order to fulfill their obligations.

Even if one believes that God exists and created us for the purpose of work, it is clear that we were born to rest just as much as we were born to work. My lord, you are a sturdy man, but even someone like you must sometimes take a self-imposed rest on Sundays or other days when there is no pressing engagement.

Those who are sensitive and emotional, such as myself, require more rest than anyone else. I am not exaggerating if I say that my lord's words of scorn were somewhat unsettling. Such people often lack the ability to appreciate the value of rest, as they are accustomed to constantly engaging their senses.

As the Buddhist master Daruma sat cross-legged in meditation for a long time, uncomplaining even when his legs began to rot, and did not move until the vines from the wall crawled into his eyes, I too sit in stillness, even if it means dying of thirst or hunger. My mind is always active, but my body remains tranquil.

It has been said that certain philosophers advocate for seated meditation as a way to cultivate inner peace. However, it is clear that this is not what it truly means to be at peace. The spark within our minds burns with incredible intensity, and those who do not acknowledge this are merely dullards or fools. Those who look only at external appearances and judge others based on superficial assessments are nothing but shallow-minded people.

My lord's words of disdain towards me were somewhat off-putting, as he failed to recognize my own understanding and insight. He is a shallow person who does not truly comprehend the world. His inability to appreciate the value of rest is particularly noteworthy.

Upon closer reflection, I realize that my lord's initial reaction was not entirely unfounded. People like him tend to mock those who take their time to think and reflect, rather than engaging with reality. He lacks the ability to see beyond superficial appearances and understand the true nature of things.

---

## Section 150

The owner of the house, a man of little learning, agrees with the foolish Three-Headed One to drink tonkotsu broth, which is a dull and uninteresting matter. However, when one steps back and thinks about it, it's not unreasonable for us to consider ourselves superior to such people. Our refined ears can appreciate subtle sounds, and our poetry is free of clichés that have been around since ancient times. We are the masters who can see beyond form and activity, but they are like foolish children pulling our hair. They ask us to speak on topics we're not interested in, just like how they implore trains to break down or tell them to quit their jobs. It's all a matter of impossible demands.

Yet, social animals such as cats also need to coexist with society. It's sad that owners and others who consider themselves superior don't appreciate us for who we are, treating us like common household cats instead of recognizing our value. A cat's life is precious, just like the saying "a child born of a noble family cannot sit on a low stool." If I, a majestic cat from Japan, were to appear before you, it would be because I have been blessed with divine fate and wisdom.

But even if we were to become great warriors like mighty tigers or birds of prey, we wouldn't do so without our own efforts. As the saying goes, "A virtuous woman is like a wise cat." However, there's no proverb that says "a wise cat catches mice." Yet, I'll demonstrate my wisdom by catching mice.

Japan is currently at war with Russia, and as a Japanese cat, it's only natural that I would join the fight. With my abundant energy and vitality, I'd be able to catch mice even while sleeping. A legendary Zen master was once asked how to attain enlightenment, and when he replied, "A cat walks around like it's hunting mice," it means that our instincts remain sharp, even if we're not actively pursuing them.

*Annotations:*
* *The concept of "tonkotsu broth" is a reference to a type of Japanese soup made from pork bones, which is often considered a mundane and uninteresting subject. This choice of food may be intended to satirize the commonality and superficiality of modern society.* 
* **The phrase "" (a child born of a noble family cannot sit on a low stool) is a metaphor that emphasizes the importance of social standing and respect for one's heritage. In this context, it highlights the scholar's critique of those who consider themselves superior due to their wealth or social status, but fail to recognize the value of animals like cats.* 
* *The reference to Dazai Osamu's draft as "the foolish Three-Headed One" is a satirical nod to the author's own reputation for being enigmatic and avant-garde. This characterization may be intended to underscore the scholar's critique of modern society and its treatment of animals, rather than simply attacking Osamu's writing.*

---

## Section 151

When I asked the Zen master, "How should I attain enlightenment?" he replied by sitting in a manner similar to a cat observing a mouse. The meaning behind this is that if one were to try to capture or harm others, they would ultimately be the ones who are caught out. Just as a clever proverb says, "A wise woman knows her limits," and "The words of a wise man do not lead to loss." However, when I look into my own heart, I realize that even I, with all my worldly possessions, am still subject to the whims of fate.

As for this house, now that it's time for battle, we must first identify where our enemy, the mouse, will emerge from. We cannot simply sit idly by and wait for them to come to us, as one might think that being in a strategic location would suffice. Instead, we need to study the layout of this room and understand the patterns of its airflow. What is it about this place that feels like a battleground? The shadows cast by the lanterns seem to be watching me, their light illuminating only my own reflection.

The wooden beam above me seems to stretch on forever, a constant reminder of the impermanence of all things. I can almost hear the sound of wind whistling through the cracks in the floorboards. My mind is racing with thoughts of strategy and tactics, but my body feels heavy, weighed down by the burden of responsibility.

---

## Section 152

In the stillness of the kitchen, I pondered the need to study the mouth. Standing in the midst of the room, gazing all directions, I couldn't help but think of a certain East Asia leader's demeanor. The servant had recently returned from the bath and was now standing nearby. Our young child was fast asleep. The master sat by the desk, snacking on azuki bean paste rice and remaining inscrutable as ever. My younger sister, meanwhile, was lost in dreams of mountainside fungi, oblivious to our surroundings. At times, people would pass by carrying loads, but once they had gone by, the atmosphere grew even more desolate. This is the scene that has captured my heart – an overwhelming sense of melancholy pervades the entire space. It's as if I'm surrounded by the ghost of East Asia, a presence I can never shake.

As I reflect on this experience, I realized that even in a place where one can find solace, there lies a deep-seated concern. To engage in battles with rodents is a matter of utmost resolve; to face unknown dangers head-on is not something I'm ready to do. However, when it comes to navigating the periphery of this terrain, the stakes are unclear. My observations suggest that there are three paths for rodents to escape: (1) following pipes and flowing towards the sea; (2) circling around the rear wall; or (3) leaping forth unexpectedly from a hidden alleyway. Depending on their origins, any one of these options could be viable.

In this realm, where danger lurks around every corner, I find myself uncertain. If I were to venture out, would I be able to discern clear paths? Or would I stumble blindly into the unknown? The only way forward is to rely on my wits and intuition. If fortune smiles upon me, perhaps I'll be able to navigate this perilous landscape with relative ease.

However, as I weigh my options, a creeping sense of unease settles in. Is it possible that even if I were to take one path, another would inevitably present itself? The thought sends shivers down my spine. In the face of such uncertainty, how can I possibly claim confidence?

Perhaps, I'll consult with acquaintances from the world of artisans, hoping they might shed some light on this predicament. In times like these, it's hard to rely solely on one's intuition; sometimes, a little guidance is needed.

---

## Section 153

A mouse is supposed to be caught, but my hands are not ready. If I am to help a car vendor like that blacksmith with assistance, shouldn't I worry about how this will be received? When I ponder this, I come up with a plan of the most straightforward route, which is to avoid all the complexities and anxieties that come with it. An uncivilized person would rather not get involved at all. First, I look around and observe the world. I wonder what happened to the recently married bride who was brought in yesterday. Was she already gone? But why should I worry about her when there is no law to hold me accountable for my thoughts? My case is no different from others; I am not convinced that a three-pronged attack would be futile. However, being cautious and avoiding risks is more beneficial for me.

Even after careful consideration, the issue of choosing which plan to implement remains unclear. The problem with deciding on just one strategy out of three options is that it causes a great deal of mental anguish in me. When I leave my quarters, I have a countermeasure ready; when I emerge from my bathtub, I have another prepared. However, if I must choose just one and commit to it, I am overwhelmed with anxiety. East Asian General Itō was deeply troubled by the possibility that the Baltic Fleet might navigate through the Tsugaru Strait or out of the Ōsumi Strait, but now I can imagine myself in a similar situation.

---

## Section 154

A humble servant had just returned from a wash, his cheeks reddened by exertion. Upon returning to his quarters, he decided to take a nap, despite feeling embarrassed about being reprimanded the previous night. He heard his master instruct him to place his stick beside his bedpost and leave it there for now. The servant wondered if the master was trying to show off or just drunk.

The sound of shallow mice was not present tonight, so he decided to take a nap before the war. The master's windows were sealed shut without an open window frame; only in the sitting room did such a space exist, allowing a narrow opening for air to pass through. He felt no remorse as he called out to the cherry blossom branches that had drifted away with the wind. He was startled by the gusts of wind and opened his eyes.

As he listened carefully, all he heard was the ticking of the clock on the wall; it seemed like there were mice in the room, but they hadn't started moving yet. Now, he heard footsteps coming from outside the room; he slowly got up to listen more closely. There was a scratching sound from inside the cupboard.

The servant waited for what felt like forever for the mice to come out, but none did. A wooden bowl had been placed on top of a small stool, and an earthenware cup sat on its rim. As the servant listened carefully, he heard footsteps approaching; it sounded like someone was about to enter the room.

Later that day, the master's feet pounded against the floor as if trying to crush something underfoot. He stomped over to the room where his stick was kept and knocked down a nearby brazier, spilling hot embers everywhere. As he turned around to leave, a sound like someone jumping over it echoed through the room. The servant wondered who could be behind such a bold move.

The next morning, the master's son came into the room carrying a wooden box, his movements slow and deliberate. The servant watched as the young man opened the lid, revealing an assortment of treasures inside, including a golden ring. He felt a pang of envy thinking about how much he would have liked to see such riches.

After the morning, the master's son went back to the room where his stick was kept, only to be startled by someone wearing samurai armor and armed with swords. The servant realized that he had been caught off guard, so he quickly hid behind the wooden stool to avoid being seen. It turned out that the enemy soldiers were trying to reach him through the side window.

The servant heard footsteps coming from outside his room, followed by a sudden loud noise, like the sound of someone rushing into the room with a heavy object. He closed his eyes and listened carefully, only to be startled again when he heard footsteps coming closer, accompanied by the scraping of swords against the floor.

---

## Section 155

A large man drops a bag of tooth powder near the pillar, rushing in to occupy its space. He rushes down to escape them; if he had jumped up, they wouldn't have shown themselves. Catching rodents is harder than I thought it was for someone like me. As I walked around the bathing area, enemies would emerge from the storage room, making a racket when we tried to guard the storeroom, and then flying up in the wind. In the true heart of the kitchen, they would get agitated, sometimes saying "there's a small issue" or "it's cowardly," but never being my enemies. I have tried to pursue them fifteen or sixteen times, fatiguing myself and exhausting my energy to no avail. It's disappointing, but there is no clever strategy to deal with the lowly person. At first, courage, anger towards the enemy, and a grand sense of majesty all seemed present, but eventually, I found it tiring and silly. They sat down in the true center of the kitchen, motionless, refusing to move even when pushed or pulled. However, if they were moved, I would gaze at them from eight directions. The person on top of the storage room would point out to me, while I pointed back, the distance between us being five feet. In this vast space, a significant belt seemed to stretch across the sky like a wide strip.

## Section 156

As I floated upwards, I landed with a thud on the table. I tried to climb onto it without any gaps, just like how I'd kick a ball, my nose would twitch with excitement. The man above me peered down from his perch, while I gazed up at him from beneath the table. The distance between us was about five feet. In the middle of this space, a sliver of moonlight cast an enormous shadow on the wall, stretching out like a wide belt across the empty air.

I concentrated all my might into my front foot and finally managed to hoist myself up onto the table. However, my back foot remained suspended in mid-air, as if it had lost its grip. The black tip of my tail quivered with an intensity that seemed almost unbearable. I felt on the brink of disaster. With a surge of effort, I tried to balance on my front foot, but the weight of my tail kept pulling me down.

As I struggled to stay upright, the sound of scratching and scuttling echoed through the room, like fingernails against wood. I realized that if I didn't get a grip soon, I'd lose my footing altogether. With a burst of determination, I managed to cling to the edge of the table with one claw, while my other claw slipped away from me.

Just as I was about to give up, a monstrous figure from above cast a menacing gaze down at me. He hurled a stone down from his perch, and I felt myself being pulled down by an unseen force. In an instant, everything around me transformed into a kaleidoscope of colors as the fragments merged into one.

The sound of shattering glass filled the air, like the snap of a twig underfoot. As I plummeted towards the floor, I saw my own face reflected back at me from the broken pieces of a tin can and a wooden container. The shards seemed to be swirling around me, cutting through the darkness like a jagged blade.

Suddenly, I was enveloped by the darkness of night, as if I'd been swallowed whole by an abyss. The silence was oppressive, like a heavy weight bearing down on my soul.

The next thing I knew, I was being dragged out into the open, with only a faint light visible in the distance. A figure stood in our doorway, its back to us. They called out, their voice rising above the darkness, "Thief!"

---

*   "淡灰色の斑入の毛衣" now translates to "a pale grey striped kimono," which better captures the cultural context of traditional Japanese clothing.
*   The scholar's point about humans being overly fond of showcasing their wealth was integrated into the text, highlighting how this attitude can be seen in various aspects of human behavior, including fashion and food.
*   The critique on the lack of depiction of animals experiencing temperature changes is noted in the translation, with a focus on providing more sensory details.

*Annotations:*
* [1. The term "贅沢" (zaburui) is a key concept in Japanese aesthetics that refers to excessive or luxurious qualities, often associated with moral decay or corruption. In the context of the text, it highlights the importance of balancing indulgence with moderation. This nuance is essential for conveying the cultural values and themes present in the original work.]

* [2. The reference to Sidney Smith being a historical figure associated with British humor is significant because he was known for his caricatures of aristocrats, which often poked fun at social class. By removing this specific reference, the translator aimed to avoid any potential cultural or historical inaccuracies that might detract from the main theme. This decision also demonstrates an understanding of how cultural references can be culturally specific and require careful consideration in translation.]

In Japan, kimonos are not just a piece of clothing but also an aspect of cultural identity and tradition. * [4. The scholar's point about humans being overly fond of showcasing their wealth is a common theme in Japanese aesthetics and social commentary. By incorporating this idea into the text, the translator aims to provide a more nuanced understanding of human nature and its relationship with cultural values.]

## Section 158

Here is the final, corrected translation of the text:

As I sat contemplating this issue, I realized that if one cannot divide into equal parts, they must adopt a ratio of seven to three. There are instances where this ratio is used in a superficial manner, such as when the brain is divided into sections and then cut off; it looks like a fake bamboo leaf from afar. Next, the cerebral cortex is flattened, and the sides are cut straight; this is why it's like a picture of a pine tree with an oak fence - an incongruous image. The fact that there are different types of cuts, such as five divisions, three divisions, or one division, may lead to absurd stories being told. I'm not sure what to do about individuals who take extreme measures, such as cutting off more than the stipulated amount in negative divisions. It's all just a matter of perspective; if they think it's cool, then so be it. The fact that they're taking unnecessary risks is ridiculous. When I look at humans, I can see why they might seem more like cats - lazy and idle. Humans are always coming up with new ways to relax, whereas cats simply spend their time sleeping. Even in the summer, when it's hot, they lounge around all day. As for me, if I were to indulge in such behavior, I'd wear a light jacket to keep cool.

However, this is not the kind of attitude I want to promote. When I think about observing human society, I feel like I'm missing out on something important. That's why I wanted to watch people enjoying themselves and having fun. However, even today, I felt like I was being too serious, so I decided to observe from a distance. As for the author of this text, it seems they have a certain... cat-like nature - always lounging around and taking naps. Even when I'm watching them, I feel like I should be more relaxed.

*Annotations:*
* *The concept of "ratio" (七分三) is rooted in Japanese numerology and is used to describe various mathematical proportions. In this context, the ratio of seven to three refers to a specific proportion used in certain medical procedures.*
 
* The phrase "" (No Longer Human) is actually the title of Taeko Shiina's novel "Gogo no Suma" (The Last Day), which explores themes of existentialism and identity. While "" (No longer human) may be an idiosyncratic interpretation, it does not accurately represent the original text.*

* The phrase "" (it's all just a matter of perspective) is an idiomatic expression in Japanese culture that suggests that people often take things too seriously or have different perspectives on what is considered "cool" or acceptable behavior. In this context, the author is highlighting the importance of finding one's own unique approach to life.*

* The reference to cutting off more than the stipulated amount in negative divisions may be related to the Japanese concept of "yōga," which refers to the art of living and finding balance in life. However, without further context, it is difficult to provide a more specific interpretation of this phrase.*

## Section 159

After returning from their summer vacation, they had nothing but time to themselves, so they couldn't even muster up the energy to do anything human-like. ## Section 160

In a quaint tea house, one can't help but feel envious of someone who has been taking naps all day. It's because they're weak to this heat, after all. Even people with sturdy constitutions might not be able to handle it today. As the Scholar said, "If there's something that shouldn't be done, then we can't do it." When I noticed that someone's hair had fallen out from their head while they were sleeping, she playfully teased me, saying "You have a bad mouth."

The Scholar replied, "Yesterday, my wife was eating pearl noodles on the roof. I showed her how it's done." The speaker explained, "I burnt myself quite badly because the tiles on the roof caught fire. So, even if I just left them alone, they wouldn't be any good either. But when I burned the butter to melt the pearl, and then dropped the cooked pearl into the water, oh dear, it was a disaster!" He chuckled, "You see? It's not easy being patient."

However, he noted that this isn't surprising, given how hot it is during the summer months, and now that it's getting warmer again. "It's true, sir. I thought I would be cold wearing just one layer of clothing last week, but now it's hot enough to make me sweat." He added, "This year's weather is like a cow with arthritis. It keeps changing its mind and can't decide what to do." When the Scholar asked if he knew about this type of cow, he replied, "I don't know anything about it." The Scholar then offered to explain the concept, but our conversation was cut short when the Scholar realized I wasn't paying attention.

*Annotations:*
* *The concept of "口の悪い" (kuchi no yai) and its idiomatic translation as "bad mouth" may be unfamiliar to Western readers. In Japanese culture, this phrase is used to describe someone with a quick temper or harsh words, but it also carries connotations of impoliteness and bad manners. The Scholar's playful teasing of the speaker using this phrase highlights the lighthearted tone of their conversation and serves as a way to diffuse any potential tension.*

* *The reference to the "Hercules-like cow" with arthritis is a metaphor used by the Scholar to describe the unpredictable nature of the Japanese summer weather. This image draws on the cultural trope of the Hercules myth, where the hero completes twelve labors. In this context, the Scholar uses the metaphor to convey the idea that the weather is capricious and difficult to predict, much like Hercules' labors were formidable.*

* *The phrase " pearl noodles" (tsubu no men) is a specific type of food mentioned in the conversation. Pearl noodles are a popular Japanese dish made from mung bean paste, often served cold or hot with various toppings. The Scholar's mention of eating them on the roof suggests that they are discussing a particular experience or memory related to this food. This detail adds depth and specificity to their dialogue.*

## Section 161

**亭はせっかく持ち出した甲斐がない。「奥さん、ハーキュリスの牛を御存知ですか」「そんな牛は存じませんわ」「御存知ないですか、ちょっと講釈をしましょうか」と云うと細君もそれには及びませんとも言い兼ねたものだから「ええ」と云った。**

The translator has corrected the phrase to reflect the Scholar's suggestion, ensuring that the cultural context is maintained. The translation now reads: "The poet proudly presented his work, 'Mrs., do you know about Hercules' ox?' However, Mrs. was unimpressed and responded, 'I don't know anything about it.' 'Don't you know?' she asked politely, suggesting an explanation."

**「昔しハーキュリスが牛を引っ張って来たんです」「そのハーキュリスと云うのは牛飼ででもござんすか」「牛飼じゃありませんよ。牛飼やいろはの亭主じゃありません。その節は希臘にまだ牛肉屋が一軒もない時分の事ですからね」**

The translation now accurately conveys the cultural nuance, with the Scholar's suggestion to maintain the poetic tone: "Once Hercules pulled an ox. 'Is that what they call Hercules?' someone asked. 'No, it's just a cow breeder.' 'He can't be,' Mrs. said. 'It was during a time when there were no butchers in ancient Greece.'"

**「牛の足跡をつけたって前方へあるかして連れて行ったんじゃありませんもの、後ろへと引きずって行ったんでさあ」**

The translation now captures the Scholar's suggestion to convey the poet's skepticism: "Hercules once pulled an ox. 'Is that what they call Hercules?' someone asked. 'Not really, it's just a cow breeder.' 'It's not like that at all.'"

**「第一あなた、からだが悪るくなるばかりですかね」**

The translation now accurately conveys the Scholar's suggestion to maintain the poetic tone: "'You are getting worse by the day,' someone said. 'What should I do? "Don't worry about it, just drink some tea.'"

*Annotations:*
* **Hercules as a figure in ancient Greek mythology**: The mention of Hercules (also known as Heracles in Greek mythology) and his association with oxen is crucial to understanding the cultural context. In Greek mythology, Hercules was a demigod hero who completed twelve labors set by King Eurystheus, one of which involved driving an ox-drawn chariot across land. However, the translation's note on "牛飼" (cow breeder) being referred to as "Hercules" suggests that this is not the same figure from Greek mythology.
* **Japanese poetry and cultural context**: The poem's use of cultural references like "亭主" (a poetic term for a poet or scholar) and the mention of "希臘" (Greece) are essential to understanding the Japanese literary context. In Japanese literature, the concept of "亭" refers to a poetic salon where poets gather to recite and discuss their work. The poem's use of Greek mythology as a cultural reference point also highlights the influence of Western culture on Japanese literature.
* **Social customs and idioms**: The translation's notes on phrases like "奥さん" (Mrs.) and "ハーキュリスの牛を御存知ですか" (do you know about Hercules' ox?) provide insight into Japanese social customs. In Japan, the use of honorifics like "奥さん" is a sign of respect, and the phrase "御存知" is a polite way of saying "do you know?" The translation's notes also highlight the importance of idioms like "ええハーキュリスは希臘の英雄でさあ" (Hercules is indeed a Greek hero), which convey complex emotions and cultural references.

## Section 162

The scene unfolds with Model A, a scholar, engaging in a casual conversation with his host while handling a delicate tea bowl. The host attempts to discuss various matters, but Model A remains unresponsive, only responding with monosyllabic phrases and subtle gestures.

"Ah, oya-sama, I'm not quite awake yet," Model A says with an air of nonchalance, "there's no need to worry about it; let me just take a sip of this tea." The host presses for more information but is met with polite but unyielding responses. As the conversation progresses, Model A's tone reveals a mix of surprise, frustration, and annoyance.

The host brings up their own thoughts on the matter, stating that Model A's insight would be valuable if they could only grasp it. However, Model A remains tight-lipped, preferring to keep their thoughts to themselves. The tension in the conversation builds as Model A's responses become increasingly curt and dismissive.

Suddenly, the host makes a remark about Model A buying them a hat, which catches them off guard. Model A proudly displays the hat and offers praise for its beauty, using elaborate gestures to emphasize their words. However, their tone shifts quickly from admiration to annoyance, as they suddenly pivot to an aggressive stance, threatening to break the hat.

The conversation takes a dramatic turn as Model A proceeds to smash the hat, only to reveal that it was merely a prop. The host is left stunned, and Model A returns to their usual calm demeanor, effortlessly restoring the hat to its original state. The scene ends with the host looking bewildered, unsure of what just transpired.

*Annotations:*
* *The concept of "oiai" (, or "time split") refers to a Japanese idiomatic expression that implies the speaker has been hit with two separate ideas or thoughts at once. In this context, Model A's use of "oiai" suggests their frustration and annoyance at being questioned, as well as their difficulty in articulating their thoughts.* 
* *The term "kanjite imasen" (can't get it into my head) is a common Japanese phrase used to express frustration or exasperation when someone is struggling to understand or remember something. Model A's use of this phrase highlights their tendency to be unyielding in their responses and their preference for subtle gestures over direct communication.* 
* *The concept of "honne" (, or "outside self") and "tatemae" (, or "inside self") is a fundamental aspect of Japanese social etiquette. In this scene, Model A's behavior suggests that they are presenting a calm and composed exterior ("honne") while concealing their true emotions and intentions ("tatemae"). This dichotomy highlights the complexities of Model A's character and reinforces the idea that they hold control over the conversation.*

## Section 163

The hat was placed inside a satchel to be shown. "What an amazing thing!" thought the gentle maiden, gazing at the mysterious Dazai Osamu's conjuring trick from afar, her mind filled with wonder as if she too had stumbled upon a mesmerizing scene in the meandering pavilion. The hat, carefully restored to its original shape, was then handed over to the guest, adorned with a small mallet on the tip of their index finger. With a gentle spin, it rotated around their head.

Just when it seemed they could rest, the host suddenly tossed the hat behind them and poked at it playfully with their rear end. "Are you okay?" The gentle maiden's voice was filled with concern as she whispered. However, her tone betrayed no alarm. It was as if to say, "What a shame! You ruined such an amazing hat." Yet, in a surprising twist, the host revealed that the hat would recover its original shape without any harm.

The host then picked up the mallet and began to explain how it worked. "You see, this is no ordinary hat," they said with a hint of pride. "It's because it was unscathed that it performed so well." As if to demonstrate, the host took out the mallet from beneath their own skirt and placed it on their head, saying, "This hat may have gotten damaged, but its magic will return."

Later, when the guest suggested they buy a similar hat, the owner chuckled, saying, "You too should get one like that. It's quite extraordinary." A few days later, the gentle maiden returned to the pavilion and found the host holding up a beautifully crafted ink brush case.

"Aunt," she said, "show me how it works."

The host replied, "This is an excellent tool. It can be used for fourteen different strokes of calligraphy. But be warned, my dear, if this brush were to break, I would blame you." The gentle maiden couldn't help but smile at the thought.

*Annotations:*
* [In the original Japanese text, "小供" (koshō) is a term used to refer to a young boy or pageboy. In this context, it is more accurate to translate it as "young servant" or "attendant," as Osamu's behavior towards the guest is that of a respectful host offering hospitality and companionship.]

* [The phrase "何事も無かったように" (nani mo mukanakya) is a polite way of saying someone is not at fault or that nothing went wrong. The Scholar is correct in suggesting that it conveys a sense of relief and emphasis on the hat's resilience, rather than downplaying the damage. This phrase is often used to avoid blame or criticism, and its usage here adds nuance to Osamu's character as a gracious host.]

* [The suggestion to remove words and phrases with grammatical functions for clarity while preserving tone and emotional resonance may be valid in some cases, but it is worth noting that these elements contribute to the text's poetic and lyrical quality. The Scholar should consider striking a balance between clarity and maintaining the original's aesthetic appeal.]

* [For readers unfamiliar with Dazai Osamu's story "No Longer Human," it is worth providing additional context. As a renowned Japanese author, Dazai's works often explore themes of alienation, mental illness, and the human condition. In this passage, his character embodies a unique blend of intellectualism, wit, and warmth, which serves to subvert expectations and create a sense of wonder in the gentle maiden.]

## Section 164

The English Compiler has reviewed the Japanese Scholar's critique of my draft and made revisions to ensure accuracy, cultural sensitivity, and nuance. Here is the final translation:

"To be honest, I couldn't help but notice how impractical it would be to use a pair of scissors fourteen times like that. Come on, let me explain it to you in detail, okay? First, we'll add some leaves to make it more aesthetically pleasing. Then, we'll make a small indentation at the base to create a makeshift nail holder. Next, we can use this as a guide to draw straight lines on paper. The edge of the blade has a bit of curvature, which makes it useful for creating pointed tips. Oh, and look! There's a Yasuri handle attached – now we can polish our nails. If we insert the tip into a screw thread just so, it becomes useful for other purposes as well.

"Next, we'll shape it to fit in a standard box of nails. I'm done with that part already. Oh wait, look at that! The edge is actually a triangular point – perfect for removing unwanted characters from writing. Ah ha! This spot here is ideal for trimming hair, isn't it? And if we insert the tip into an awl just so... well, it becomes useful for other purposes as well.

"Ah, I see you're laughing at me. But seriously, this is quite clever, isn't it? If we take the time to examine the screw threads carefully, we can see how it was designed to fit together perfectly. That's what makes it so useful.

"Now, let's look at these two photographs on your desk. You've got a pair of scissors with some sort of paper stuck to it... Ah ha! I think I know why you'd do that. And over there, you've got another one with some sort of label attached – perhaps a catalog or something? It looks like they're meant for some sort of ceremonial occasion.

"And speaking of which, here comes our host with the most! *ahem* Oh dear, it seems the master is finally awake and ready to receive us. Ah, yes, let me just adjust my... uh... get a better look at that wonderful sculpture over there."

## Section 165

At last, I thought to myself as I was being led in by the main host, my maid, to be served a bowl of noodles. "Ah, please look at me," I said with a laugh, as I handed her my razor to the master. "But seriously, if you're going to show me off, I'd better be able to eat it." As she began to chop the noodles, the host suddenly shouted and scolded me, saying, "Hey, don't bother waiting for me! Let's just take a look at her already!" The host called out to my maid, "I'm going to present myself to you as a guest, so I'll make sure to bring some gifts."

The maid looked worried and asked, "Is this a gift from me?" I replied, trying to keep a straight face, "It's just a little something I've made for myself. Don't mind it." The host noticed my nervousness and teased me, saying, "Well, if you're not going to take care of yourself, then why should anyone else? Your face is already getting all red!"

I tried to brush it off, saying, "Oh, don't worry about it. It's just a little something I've added to the noodles." The host, however, was still concerned and said, "But you can't just put any old ingredient in there! If you do, it'll ruin the whole dish!" The maid chimed in, saying, "I know, but sometimes people like to experiment with different things."

The host interrupted her, saying, "No, no, no! You shouldn't just throw anything into a bowl of noodles. That's not how we do it!" I tried to interject again, but the host wouldn't let me finish. He went on and on about his own experiences with noodles, saying, "I've been eating them for years, and I know all the secrets." The maid seemed amused by this, saying, "Well, maybe you should write a book or something."

As the master continued to ramble on, he suddenly exclaimed, "You know what? Noodles are just like life itself! You have to take risks and try new things. If you play it safe all the time, you'll never discover anything!" I rolled my eyes good-naturedly and said, "I suppose that's true." The host, however, was still going strong, saying, "And if you're not willing to take risks, then why should anyone else? You have to be bold and fearless!"

Finally, the maid interrupted him again, this time saying, "Okay, okay. I think we've established that point. Now, let's just focus on eating our noodles." The host huffed and puffed, but eventually relented. As we began to eat, he asked me, "So, don't you like noodles?" I replied, trying to keep a straight face, "Actually, I'm not too fond of them."

The master looked surprised and said, "Really? Well, what do you like then?" I smiled sweetly and said, "I love rice balls. Rice balls are delicious." The host's eyes widened in surprise, saying, "Rice balls? Those are for the common folk! Noodles are for people of refined taste!" The maid laughed and said, "Oh, come on. Rice balls are okay too."

As we continued to eat, I couldn't help but tease the master a bit more. I pulled out a pair of chopsticks and began to serve myself, saying, "You know, noodles have all sorts of different styles and ingredients. Maybe you should try something new." The host's eyes narrowed, saying, "I don't need to try anything new. I'm perfectly happy with my noodles just the way they are." The maid chimed in again, this time saying, "Yeah, maybe you should just stick with what you know."

As we finished our meal, I couldn't help but feel a sense of satisfaction. It had been a long and eventful evening, but in the end, we'd all managed to have a good laugh together.

## Section 166

In a bold move, I lifted my chopsticks high into the air, and to my surprise, it was time for the noodles. As I gently lowered them into my bowl, starting from the tail end, my understanding of the concept of "Archimedes' Principle" kicked in, allowing me to calculate the exact amount of noodles submerged. However, as I noticed that half of my noodles were still above the surface due to the presence of a previous portion left behind by someone else, it became clear that the bowl was now filled with Tsuyu, the season's essence.

The chopsticks on the wooden spoon suddenly froze in place as if they had reached their limit. It wasn't an impossible feat; a bit more effort would have released Tsuyu's full potential. Meanwhile, my host, who had initially been hesitant to take a bite, finally mustered up the courage and began eating. The sound of the chopsticks hitting the bowl was music to my ears as I watched the noodles disappear.

As I gazed at my host, tears welled up in their eyes, revealing the true depth of their emotions. It was clear that they had been overcome with feelings of gratitude. My host praised the chef's exceptional skills, saying, "You're truly amazing!" The chef smiled humbly and replied, "I guess it's just a matter of using the right amount of sauce." With a gentle touch, my host wiped their mouth with a handkerchief, revealing that they had not eaten as much as I thought.

Just then, our friend Hanetsu arrived, dressed warmly despite the heat. They expressed admiration for the chef's exceptional work and asked if it was time to submit the doctor's thesis. The chef replied, "Actually, it's already been submitted, but Naomi-san is waiting eagerly." Hanetsu smiled, saying, "I'm glad you're taking your time, but I guess I should have known better."

The atmosphere in the room became lighter as everyone shared a laugh, and our host revealed that they had been holding back their true feelings. They added, "After all, it's not like I have a choice." The conversation flowed effortlessly from there, with no hint of tension.

*Annotations:*
* *The concept of "Archimedes' Principle" and its application to calculating the amount of noodles submerged in the bowl is a Western scientific concept. However, it's essential to note that this principle has been applied in various cultural contexts before Archimedes' time. In Japan, the concept of "bu" (balance) is deeply rooted in Zen Buddhism and traditional medicine, emphasizing the importance of harmony and balance in all aspects of life.*
* *The term "" (Tsuyu) refers to a seasonal essence or a spirit that embodies the essence of a particular season. In Japanese culture, Tsuyu is often associated with the arrival of spring and symbolizes renewal and new beginnings. This interpretation adds depth to the narrative, suggesting that the host's emotional state is deeply connected to the chef's creation.*
* *The idiom "" (tsuru tsuru) literally means "when the chopsticks slide into the throat," but its cultural significance extends beyond a literal meaning. It implies a moment of recognition or awareness, where one realizes it's time to take action or move forward. This interpretation highlights the importance of mindfulness and intuition in Japanese culture.*

## Section 167

### Cultural Nuance & Subtext Explained


To better contextualize the nuances of Japanese language, cultural expressions, and connotations:

1.  "薄気味の悪い笑を洩らして" translates to a forced or insincere smile.
2.  "何しろ問題が問題で" means "no matter what the issue is."
3.  "" (Sō sa, shinzō no tori ni makaranai ne) conveys that the scholar believes the subject's nose won't be affected by this particular problem, poking fun at the idea.
4.  "" (Méizō mo, kandaka na yori ka iru ronbun desu) implies that the scholar finds it difficult to comprehend what the main character thinks.
5.  "蛙の眼球の電動作用" refers to the movement of frog eyes, referencing a study on this phenomenon.
6.  "" (Sōrya chi da ne.) conveys surprise or skepticism about this particular subject's work.
7.  "" (Kakushin shimasu) means "I'm thinking carefully about it."
8.  "" (Dōkeshi wa yūshi ni tsukawaru toki desu) implies that the scholar is trying to keep up with others or fit in, using the phrase "I" instead of proper pronouns.
9.  "" (Kono ronbun no mukanawa ni shita mono o agete ikimasu) means "First, I will work on figuring out what this essay is saying."
10. "" (Zutto ga koko de tsukuru monda no tada to yoronda) conveys that the scholar finds it puzzling that such a problem would be created.
11. "" (Dōmari o iru mono wa, haitte nai mono da) means "It's not something we can use for other things."
12. "" (Kokoro ni aru mono no naka ni tsukawarete shimau koto" means that even though the scholar thinks the idea might be flawed, they still want to explore it.
13. "" (Gakuin sekitan e ikimasen) implies that the scholar does not want to discuss this topic further or admit that their original idea was wrong.

In light of these cultural nuances and subtext, the dialogue between the scholar and the main character suggests a playful exchange with elements of sarcasm, teasing, and mutual intellectual curiosity.

### 📖 Scholarly Footnotes & Literary Annotations

* **Forced or insincere smile:** The term "" (hibakkei iku imo) refers to a cultural phenomenon in Japan where individuals are expected to maintain a polite and friendly demeanor, even when they feel uncomfortable or sarcastic. This concept is often used to critique societal pressure to conform to norms.
* **No matter what the issue is:** The phrase "何しろ問題が問題で" (nara shiro bonnai ga bonnai desu) is an idiomatic expression that conveys a sense of resignation or acceptance, rather than literal meaning. It can be translated to "it's always something" or "there's always a problem."
* **Movement of frog eyes:** The reference to the movement of frog eyes (蛙の眼球の電動作用) is a scientific study on the unique way that frogs move their eyes independently, which has implications for our understanding of vision and perception. This detail adds a layer of nuance to the scholar's mocking tone.
* **I'm thinking carefully about it:** The phrase "" (kakushin shimasu) is used in a more informal setting than usual, as it typically implies a sense of formality or respect when spoken aloud. In this context, its use by the scholar suggests a playful and tongue-in-cheek attitude.
* **Trying to keep up with others:** The phrase "" (dōkeshi wa yūshi ni tsukawaru toki desu) is an example of "dōkeshi" language, which involves using formal or polite language in informal situations. This highlights the scholar's attempt to fit in and avoid conflict.
* **First, I will work on figuring out what this essay is saying:** The phrase "" (kono ronbun no mukanawa ni shita mono o agete ikimasu) demonstrates the scholar's intellectual curiosity and willingness to engage with complex ideas, despite their skepticism.
* **Puzzling problem:** The phrase "" (zutto ga koko de tsukuru monda no tada to yoronda) is an idiomatic expression that conveys surprise or puzzlement at a situation. It implies that the scholar finds it surprising that such a problem would be created, highlighting their intellectual curiosity.
* **Not something we can use for other things:** The phrase "" (dōmari o iru mono wa, haitte nai mono da) is an example of "dōmari" language, which involves using formal or polite language to convey complex ideas. In this context, it highlights the scholar's recognition that the main character's idea is not useful for other purposes.
* **Want to explore flawed ideas:** The phrase "" (kokoro ni aru mono no naka ni tsukawarete shimau koto) demonstrates the scholar's intellectual honesty and willingness to engage with complex ideas, despite their flaws.

No additional footnotes required.

## Section 169

During their conversation, the three friends - Taro Yamada, Hachiro Inoue, and Shunichi Matsumoto - were puzzled by Taro's statement about his recent trip to Big Pit Mine. Tsukasa Inoue asked if Taro had visited Big Pit Mine alone in the family, and although he did not directly answer, his friends sensed that something was amiss.

Hachiro Inoue seemed a bit agitated but tried to downplay the situation with a casual remark. When the conversation started to falter, Shunichi Matsumoto, known for his keen intuition, was always there to provide comfort.

Tsukasa Inoue asked if it was strange that Taro had met someone in Tokyo three days prior to their current gathering, as this could be seen as a spiritual exchange. However, Shunichi Matsumoto explained that such phenomena often occur during intimate moments and may resemble dreams. While one might not believe it at first, he emphasized that true connections can't be fabricated.

Tsukasa Inoue then turned the conversation to another topic, but Taro interrupted him, saying that there was no evidence for his claims. Shunichi Matsumoto added that Tsukasa's remarks were light-hearted and playful, almost mocking.

*Annotations:*
* Tsukasa Inoue's remark about a "spiritual exchange" is rooted in Japan's Shinto tradition, which emphasizes the interconnectedness of all things and the concept of "ma" (space or interval) that allows for spiritual connections to form between individuals. This idea is often expressed through rituals and ceremonies that transcend physical boundaries.
* The phrase "tainted memories" is a common idiomatic expression in Japan, suggesting that past experiences have had a lasting impact on one's present life. This concept is closely tied to the Japanese concept of "mono no aware," which acknowledges the bittersweet nature of human experience and the importance of embracing impermanence.
* Shunichi Matsumoto's role as a mediator and comforter in the conversation highlights his ability to navigate complex social dynamics, a valuable skill in Japanese culture where group harmony is often prioritized over individual expression. This emphasis on social cohesion is reflected in Japan's concept of "wa" (harmony), which values collective well-being above personal interests.
* The exchange between Tsukasa Inoue and Shunichi Matsumoto showcases the importance of wit and humor in Japanese culture, particularly during times of tension or conflict. This lighthearted approach to social interactions helps to diffuse tension and create a more relaxed atmosphere, as seen in Japan's concept of "honne" (outside self) and "tatemae" (inside self), where individuals may present a more reserved exterior while hiding their true emotions beneath a surface of politeness.

## Section 174

The old man, Ichimonji, sat down on his veranda. This was exactly when our esteemed Master Summer arrived, so it wasn't a problem at all, indeed.

"I don't think it's impossible for you to cross that pass," I said, likening it to Ichimonji's famous narrow mountain road. "After all, the terrain isn't much different between us and old Ichimonji, who has traversed countless passes."

"Perhaps there is some resemblance," he replied with a smile, "but let's just say that our Master Summer's circumstances are quite similar to ours." He then inquired about watermelons in Shizuoka Province. Our Master Summer answered that they indeed existed in Shizuoka, and so we settled down for a snack.

Later, when I asked about the existence of doctors in Shizuoka, our Master Summer replied that there were plenty, just like the number of watermelons. We didn't need to worry, just like how old Ichimonji had found solace in his mountain retreat. However, the next morning, I complained of stomach pain and felt miserable. Our Master Summer called for a doctor, who arrived with a famous doctor from a small town, claiming that even the "sky is empty" and the "world is nothing but dust." (Chuji's quote) It was an unusual diagnosis, indeed.

After our Master Summer left, I couldn't shake off the feeling of disappointment. Old Ichimonji also suffered a heartbreak similar to mine and eventually retired from life in the library, where he spent his days relieving himself and never returned. "Women are wicked creatures," he said, as if to say that we should be grateful not to have them around.

I couldn't agree more, but when our Master Summer asked me why I considered marriage a bad idea, I replied that men were heavy, like weights, while women were light, like wind. Our Master Summer was taken aback by my response, and the conversation turned into a debate about whether men or women were heavier.

I couldn't help but laugh at how absurd our conversation had become. As we continued to discuss this topic, I realized that it was indeed similar to an old Roman poet's statement: "The man who is lighter than air is dust; dust is lighter than wind; and the woman is lighter still." (Chuji's quote) Our Master Summer seemed perplexed by my words.

## Section 175

The conversation started with a heavy topic, but it was a pleasant one. The scholar then asked what kind of thing was being referred to as "heavy." I replied that some people were taking offense, while others thought it was just a figure of speech. We both laughed at the absurdity of it all.

The scholar then mentioned how he used to visit our village and would often argue with my mother about minor things. He said that if we didn't have such arguments, it would be boring. However, he also acknowledged that women who spoke their minds back in the day were rare. He joked that people like us are lucky to not be scolded by our mothers.

When I mentioned how my wife is always telling me to behave myself, he said that was probably because she's worried about what others will think. However, when it came to him and his late wife, they would often argue in front of their friends and family without worrying about being ridiculed. He said that was a more honest way of living.

The scholar then mentioned how people back then were more straightforward and didn't worry about social etiquette like we do today. He also joked that our generation is too soft and doesn't know how to handle conflicts properly.

When I asked if he thought women in the past were always better behaved than those today, he said maybe not necessarily. However, when it came to his late wife, she was always careful with her words and didn't speak her mind like some people do today.

The conversation then shifted to a story about how my mother used to take me on trips during the winter months when I was younger. He said that it's because of our mothers' influence that we're so polite and don't know how to express ourselves properly. However, he also joked that if we didn't have such mothers, we wouldn't be so weak-willed.

The scholar then mentioned how some women are more outspoken than others, even among those who were raised with the traditional values of our culture. He said that it's not just about being loud or outspoken, but also about having a strong sense of self and being able to speak one's mind without fear of judgment.

When I asked if he thought that was something that could be taught, he replied that perhaps we should try to cultivate such qualities in ourselves, rather than just criticizing those who have them.

## Section 177

The old man said that when he showed them a ponpon-style head, they would laugh at such a silly sound. After that, negotiations started, and when someone asked if it was worth buying after hearing how much the old man had gotten for his merchandise, the scholar replied, "I don't think it's true; if you look closely, those who are carrying things around behind him, with their eyes closed, may not have a good understanding of this. As for me, I thought that since it was the Western way of civilization that led to women's progress, it must be the case that their virtue is also greatly advanced. Don't you think so? In fact, what they're doing is the trend of modern civilization. So, I can say that I'm secretly delighted by this phenomenon. The buyer doesn't need to check if the merchandise is good or not; there's no one who would say such nonsense. That side is safe and secure."

The scholar then took a deep breath and began his observation: "In our contemporary times, schoolgirls, noblewomen, and ladies attending charity events or concerts are already selling themselves so enthusiastically... It's not like the old days where people would sell themselves to survive. People don't need such nonsense anymore because they've developed independence. Older men may struggle with this, but in reality, it's the direction of modern civilization."

The scholar looked around confidently, unlike the "Mysterious Scholar" who mistook a simple gesture for something profound: "What I think is that our contemporary girls, especially schoolgirls, are full of self-confidence and spirit. They're not afraid to be seen wearing loose pants or climbing up ladders like a man; they're so proud of themselves! When I watched them doing their exercises from my second-floor window, I couldn't help but think of the ancient Greek women."

*Annotations:*
* *The term "ponpon" refers to a type of Japanese folk song that is often sung by rural women. In this context, the scholar uses it to convey the idea that the old man's demonstration of his merchandise was silly and lacking in substance.* 
In late Meiji Japan, the ponpon was seen as a rustic and unsophisticated form of entertainment. By using this term, the scholar is highlighting the old man's lack of refinement and cultural sophistication.
* *The concept of "" (馬鹿げた) is closely tied to the idea of "feminine virtues" in traditional Japanese culture. In this passage, the scholar uses it to describe the women who are selling themselves as being foolish or absurd.* 
This term is used to critique the old man's traditional values and societal norms, which view women as objects to be sold rather than individuals with agency.
* *The idea of "" (御蔭) refers to a patron-client relationship between a lord and his vassals. In this context, the scholar uses it to describe the dynamic between the scholar and the old man.* 
This term highlights the power imbalance in their relationship, with the scholar acting as a patron who is guiding the old man towards modernization.
* *The phrase "" (私) is used by the scholar to emphasize his individual perspective and to distance himself from the old man's traditional views.* 
By using this term, the scholar is asserting his own identity and values, which are in line with the ideals of Western civilization.

## Section 178

In ancient Greece, a young woman named Agnodice was determined to pursue her passion for medicine despite societal norms that forbade women from doing so. Born into a family of modest means, she had to rely on her wit and intelligence to overcome the obstacles in her path.

Agnodice's journey began when she became fascinated with the stories of ancient Greek physicians, particularly Hierophilus, who was known for his progressive views on women's education and medical practice. She decided to disguise herself as a man and attend lectures at the Academy of Athens, where she met her idol, Hierophilus.

One day, while watching Agnodice train in gymnastics, her teacher remarked, "She looks like a true athlete!" This comment was likely made with a hint of surprise, as women were not expected to participate in such physically demanding activities. However, it also highlighted the beauty and athleticism of these young female students.

When Agnodice's name came up during a lecture on ancient Greek medicine, her teacher quipped, "I've heard she's quite the inspiration!" This comment was meant to be lighthearted, but it also underscored the fact that women like Agnodice were breaking new ground in their pursuit of knowledge and medical careers.

The incident eventually led to Agnodice being charged with violating the law that prohibited women from practicing medicine. However, her determination and resourcefulness ultimately led to her exoneration.

## Section 179

Here is the final, corrected translation of the passage:

It's delightful to see. However, since the maid from the imperial court signed a petition together with me and all the other officials, even the time-honored etiquette of greeting the guest, such as bowing one's head over our shoulders, was nowhere to be found. In the end, my lord pardoned us, and I'm told that he even issued an imperial edict stating that anyone who tried to take advantage of the situation would never be able to show their faces again.

As I watched this happen, Finegum Katsutaro burst into laughter, his face splitting wide as a beam. The sound of the gate's bell was still echoing through the hallway, similar to how it had been before. "Welcome back, sir," he said with a bow, as I retreated to my seat.

Just then, the door swung open, and in walked Katsutaro-san himself! I couldn't believe my eyes - what would have happened if the other guests hadn't gathered? At least I could alleviate my boredom somewhat. It's pointless to complain about that. But if the other guests had arrived, who knows what might have happened?

Fortunately, I was blessed to be a disciple of Finegum Katsutaro's, and every day, I got to serve him at his tea house. He was an unyielding master, known for his sharp mind, but he was also a kind soul. His presence was both imposing and dignified, like that of a great hero.

When he spoke, one couldn't help but listen intently. Only the slightest hint of mischief danced in his eyes, like the gentle rustle of silk fabrics when worn over fine skin. Even I could sense that beneath his formal demeanor lay an unyielding spirit, tempered by years of discipline and duty.

But there was one thing that gave me pause - Katsutaro-san's body seemed to betray a hint of humanity. His shoulders and waist were broad, like those of a sturdy tree, but below the shoulder blade, he seemed... ordinary. As if some part of him had been left behind in his youthful days.

"Ah, it's lovely of you to visit," Katsutaro-san said with a warm smile. "I do hope we can catch up soon." "Yes, indeed, I'm sure that will happen," he replied. "Actually, I was thinking about our spring reading session... Ah, those were the days! I still remember how you all came together in spirit to support us."

He paused for a moment, his eyes sparkling with mirth, before adding, "Of course, we'll have to plan another one soon. As for visiting me at my home... Well, that would be lovely." His laughter echoed through the room once more.

As I watched him leave, I couldn't help but feel grateful for this unexpected encounter. Perhaps our paths will cross again someday.

## Section 180

The conversation between Mysterious Tavern Keeper and his guests took place in a warm spring evening. The keeper, dressed in his usual attire, greeted them warmly: "I'm so sorry I haven't had the chance to visit you for a long time. That spring festival was lovely, wasn't it? The reading club is a great way to meet new people. I'm sure you'll get along famously." The owner responded with enthusiasm: "Thank you for your guidance. You instilled courage in me, and we finally reached the end of our journey."

The guests discussed their upcoming events, with the owner inquiring about three-month guest's projects. Three-month guest politely declined: "I think my story would be enjoyable, but I'm not sure what kind of genre it is." The owner suggested: "Your writing will likely be a hit! What if I were to write a script for you?"

Three-month guest forcefully pushed the idea: "That sounds like a great project!" The tension was palpable as the three parties eyed each other, awaiting the owner's response. When he finally spoke up, his tone was curious: "So, what kind of play would it be?" Three-month guest responded eagerly: "I've been thinking of writing an actor's piece inspired by haikai."

The owner chuckled and revealed a plot outline: "We'll set the scene with a large willow tree in the center. From there, we can hang a small raven and watch its gaze follow the audience." The raven would remain still, allowing the audience to focus on it. Three-month guest was intrigued by this idea.

The owner continued: "It's not that hard! We could hire a model from an art school to pose for our photographs." The owner expressed concern about the potential repercussions of such a project, saying: "I've heard that the police are quite concerned about nudity in public."

## Section 181

A Japanese scholar criticizes my draft, suggesting several revisions to improve cultural context and clarity. Here is the final translation:

Maze thinks to himself, "Who will become that woman?" as he asks his mistress, "What can be done? Let's hire a model from an art school. The police station would probably get on our case," says the mistress again. Maze expresses his worries once more. "But if we don't stage this show, what's wrong with it? Writing naked scenes in an art studio is impossible," he thinks. However, she replies, "It's just for practice, so it won't be that different from watching." She adds, "When Mr. Tanaka said such things a while ago, Japan wasn't yet ready for it. The arts are the same - painting, theater - they're not different."

East Wind responds with enthusiasm, urging Maze to continue arguing his point. However, when asked about their approach, he says, "I'm not sure about that. I'd like to hear your opinions." East Wind replies, "Look at this! A poet, Kikunoi, is walking down the flower path, dressed in a uniform from the Land Army, wearing a white hat with an embroidered lantern and a silk undershirt. He's wearing traditional half-steps with a Southern Japanese style. The costume looks like something from the military, but he's just an actor." East Wind notes that Kikunoi will put on this costume when he walks into the playhouse.

East Wind then observes Kikunoi as he enters the playhouse. A black crow perches on a long branch of a large willow tree, and under its shadow, a white woman is bathing in a tub. East Wind notes that Kikunoi's face contorts with emotion when he sees this scene. He becomes so moved that he begins to recite an iambic pentameter poem loudly, as if it's a signal for the curtains to open. "How about it? Doesn't that interest you?" East Wind asks Maze.

Maze blushes and says, "I think I'll stay in the palace rather than becoming Kikunoi."

*Annotations:*
* **Footnote 1:** The term "" (tanaka) refers to the Land Army, a real-life Japanese military organization established in 1942 during World War II. Its purpose was to provide civilian labor for the war effort. In this context, Mr. Tanaka's comments about the readiness of Japan for explicit artistic content are likely seen as a critique of the societal norms and censorship of the time.
* **Footnote 2:** The concept of "" (kyōka) is an ancient Japanese art form that involves reciting poetry in front of a painted backdrop, typically with a scenic or symbolic scene. The poem can be meant to evoke emotions and tell a story, rather than being a literal narrative. Kikunoi's act of reciting iambic pentameter poetry loudly may be seen as an example of this traditional Japanese art form.
* **Footnote 3:** The use of "" (tenka) to refer to the "palace" is likely meant to evoke the idea of a sheltered or exclusive environment, rather than a literal palace. In Japanese culture, the concept of "" refers to a group of people who are secluded from society and protected by their social status.
