## Section 182

The sun was setting over Tokyo, casting a warm orange glow over the bustling streets. Dazai Osamu leaned against a lamppost, puffing on his cigarette as he pondered the meaning of life.

"Ah, I'm just a failure," he said with a sigh. "I've written hundreds of plays, but none of them have ever been considered true masterpieces. Even my poetry is met with indifference."

His friend, a man known only by his stage name, East Wind (Higuma), approached him with a concerned expression. "Are you feeling too pessimistic about your work?" he asked.

Osamu shrugged. "I suppose I am. But what's the point of trying to be optimistic when nothing ever seems to work out?"

East Wind nodded thoughtfully. "Well, let me explain... As a scholar, I think it's clear that Osamu's feelings towards Ueno Chikako are rooted in his own personal experiences."

Osamu raised an eyebrow. "Go on."

East Wind continued, "You see, when we experience strong emotions, our minds tend to create elaborate narratives around them. In this case, Osamu is projecting his own desires and fears onto Ueno's character."

Osamu's eyes widened as he grasped the concept. "So, you're saying that my feelings towards her are not necessarily about her at all, but about myself?"

East Wind nodded. "Exactly. When we focus on our own emotions, rather than trying to impose them onto someone else, we can gain a deeper understanding of ourselves."

Osamu's face lit up with excitement. "Ah, I see what you mean! That's the true power of art – not just about expressing ourselves, but about exploring the depths of human emotion."

## Section 183

When asked, East Wind (Katsuragi Banri) said, "I'm afraid I don't have anything worthy of being presented to you. I've been thinking of publishing a collection of poems soon, so I brought my manuscript with me in hopes that you might offer some criticism."

He then pulled out a purple obi sash from his kimono and extracted several pages of rough drafts from the inner pocket of his cloak, laying them on the table before his host. The host examined the first page, which read:

People do not understand what I am saying
But the poor girl, Rich Girl, understands me

The host looked at East Wind with an enigmatic expression, studying the first page for a while without saying anything. Mysterious Guest (Matsuura Shiki) couldn't help but exclaim, "What is this new poetry? It's so... interesting!" and continued to praise East Wind's work.

However, the host raised an eyebrow and said, "East Wind-san, do you really believe that Rich Girl exists?" The host explained that he had met her before, as a woman who would often accompany him to literary events in his neighborhood. He mentioned that this was actually a casual visit, as he had spent the previous month at Big Island (Okunoshima) on vacation.

Mysterious Guest responded, "It means that the meaning is a bit lost in translation. That's why I wouldn't use those words myself."

The host nodded and said, "I see. So it's like... when you read my poems, you get a sense of 'it' but can't quite put your finger on what it is?"

Mysterious Guest replied, "Yes, that's exactly right. You're not too far off. I think the phrase should be changed to something like 'Rich Girl's nose below', so that it better conveys the idea."

East Wind then explained, "Well, I suppose that would make a difference. But if we change it now, it might alter the tone of the poem entirely. I'm not sure if that's what you want me to do."

This translation reflects the author's intention to convey a sense of connection and shared perspective.*

## Section 186

When I ask you if you know who that young lady from Goldfield is, "Do you know who she is?" or "What's your connection to her?" would be more suitable. "This spring, she was invited to our reading party and we got to know each other since then." My friend once said that whenever I stepped out in front of her, I felt a strange sense of excitement, and even writing poetry or composing songs didn't bring me the same joy anymore. But I think it's because of the inspiration from my female friends who appreciate literature more than others. That's why I wanted to express my gratitude for her presence by dedicating our gathering to her. "There isn't a poet in history who dedicated their work without having a close friend like that," she said with a smile, her eyes twinkling. But after that, the conversation lost steam, and I couldn't help but leave it alone. As a writer, I had the duty of listening to such conversations all day long, so I went out to look for crickets in the garden.

Seventh

Lately, I started exercising, which has been quite amusing. My friends often mock me, saying "you're just doing this for fun" when I try to explain it, but I guess that's because they're not athletes like me. Do you remember how my wife used to say things like "let's drink some milk" or "take a refreshing bath"? And now, she asks us to "go for a run" or "do some exercises." It seems like the Western world has brought a contagious disease that makes people talk about exercise all the time. As I've only just started living for two years, and my wife passed away last year when we were both one year old, I can hardly imagine how terrible diseases like peste, tuberculosis, or neurasthenia affected our ancestors back then. But if you think twice, it's not that different from today. My cat lives for ten years in human terms, but a human life is just a fraction of its lifespan. So it's no mistake to say that if we were to calculate the development of my cat's one whisker, it would take an equal amount of time as a human year and month. In other words, I must have made a wrong assumption when I thought that humans could be compared to cats in terms of age.

## Section 187

The Japanese scholar critiques my draft, pointing out several errors in interpretation and cultural nuance. Here is a revised version of my draft, incorporating their suggestions:

In the brief span between cat's whisker growth and humanity's development, it can be argued that our pursuit of wisdom is misguided. Considering we are but mere humans, struggling to comprehend even the simplest aspects of life, how can we claim to have grasped the intricacies of a cat's intellect? The third daughter of our lord may be counted as three years old in terms of numerical age, but her cognitive development far surpasses that of ordinary people. She has not yet mastered the most basic skills such as crying when upset or using the restroom.

When asked why sea water is said to possess medicinal properties, it is quite simple: one need only visit the coast and observe its effects firsthand. Yet, even this simple truth eludes us, and we continue to speculate about its virtues. Meanwhile, our friend's fish succumb to disease, despite their robust physique, and no doctor can provide a cure. It is evident that sea water has healing properties when used appropriately, but it is hardly a secret.

It is not surprising that humans have only recently begun to recognize the benefits of movement, sea bathing, and traveling for therapeutic purposes. Our ancestors, familiar with the ways of various cultures and regions, have long understood the importance of physical activity, exposure to natural environments, and travel in maintaining mental well-being. In other words, we are not as advanced as we claim to be.

As I reflect on my own limitations, I am reminded that even a simple question about sea water's medicinal properties can be answered by visiting the coast. The fact that we continue to debate this topic suggests our own narrow perspective and lack of understanding. It is only when we consider the broader cultural and historical context that we begin to grasp the significance of these practices.

## Section 188

The text can be translated to English as follows:

"If you think of it this way, it's no different from humans waiting for someone to take notice only when they're dead. It's just a matter of time. The function of sea bathing is clearly seen in the way fish are affected by it. If it's not clearly visible on fish, then it can't be seen on humans either. Even if Doctor Richard Russell in 1750 advertised that Brighton sea water could cure 400 diseases with immediate effects and outrageous advertisements, even that was considered too late to laugh at. As long as there's a suitable time for everything, people will still venture out to Kamakura. But now it's not the right time. Everything has its timing. The Japanese before the Meiji era didn't get to experience the function of sea bathing and died prematurely. Today's cats, if they were to encounter the opportunity to jump into the sea naked, would be better off than those who got beaten up in the streets of Shibuya. If we can't even consider that as a viable option, then it's futile.

"Let's put motion into action, taking exercise seriously. It seems like modern times are all about exercising, and people think it's only for poor folks to do so. But being active isn't just about doing something; it's also about not having the time or energy to do so. People who don't engage in physical activity are considered inferior nowadays. My evaluation is that I change my perspective according to time and circumstances, just like how my eyes (or favorites) change size. A cat's eyes only get smaller or larger, but human behavior is the opposite - it's true. There's always a two-sided aspect to everything. Both ends have their own appeal. If you flip things around, then even the concept of 'sight' becomes something different. Looking at a bridge from a certain angle makes it seem even more unique."

*Annotations:*
* *The concept of "" (wa to ku) is rooted in the idea of expectation and waiting for something to happen. This phrase, often used to describe human behavior, is also applied to animals, including cats, in this passage. In the context of "wagahai wa neko de aru," it highlights the notion that animals are capable of adapting to their environment, much like humans. The translation aims to preserve this nuance, as it adds depth to the author's argument about the importance of physical activity.* 
* *The term "" (帰宣) refers to a ceremonial or ritualistic act, often associated with the Confucian tradition in Japan. In this passage, "" seems to imply the idea of self-care or taking care of oneself, as seen in the context of sea bathing. The distinction between "" and "" is crucial in understanding the author's intent, as it highlights the importance of context in interpreting these terms.* 
* *The reference to "Doctor Richard Russell" in 1750 is likely an allusion to Dr. Richard Russell, a British physician who advocated for the use of seawater as a treatment for various diseases. However, the passage suggests that even if such claims were made, they would be considered too late or ineffective. This highlights the cultural context of the time and the author's skepticism towards modern medical practices.*

---

## Section 189

In my humble opinion, I found Hamlet's character from the Stockroom to be quite unimpressive. If there aren't any renowned individuals discussing this topic, it's unlikely that the literary world would make progress. Therefore, those who claim to have taken up physical exercise simply because they're in a hurry and don't want to be seen as idle will not be surprised when women start carrying rackets around their neighborhood. As long as we laugh at such trivialities like cat exercises being beneficial, it's pointless to explain my own type of exercise.

Since I'm not fortunate enough to possess machines, I've found myself struggling with basic ball and bat handling. Moreover, the lack of funds makes me hesitant to purchase them. Given these two reasons, I believe my chosen form of exercise is best classified as a type that lacks equipment or machinery.

If one were to choose between walking barefoot or devouring sashimi, it's difficult to say which would be more appealing. However, simply moving our four limbs in an arbitrary manner, following the gravitational pull of the Earth, doesn't seem particularly exciting or unique. The fact that some people still engage in such exercises despite them not being well-known suggests a lack of interest.

What I find fascinating are movements that involve artistic skills, like sports that involve human opponents. Unfortunately, my own abilities do not allow me to succeed in such endeavors. Next, I considered writing on the surface of books with my claws, but this idea was immediately dismissed due to the risk of being discovered by the owner and the lack of coordination required.

I've come to realize that these types of movements are now considered "outdated" or "old-fashioned." However, I believe there are some newer forms of exercise worth exploring. First and foremost is insect hunting (or bug catching). While it may not be a grand or dramatic activity like mouse chasing, it's certainly safer and more enjoyable.

Insects can be caught in the summer half to autumn start, making it an ideal game for leisurely pursuits. To play this game, one would first venture into their backyard to search for insects. Once the right timing is achieved, one would chase down a single insect. It's considered a fun activity that is suitable for people of all ages.

*Annotations:*
* **"No Longer Human" (人間失格) and its cultural significance**: The term "No Longer Human" is a reference to Dazai Osamu's novel of the same name, which explores themes of alienation, identity, and existential crises. In this context, the phrase is not just a translation but also a nod to the Japanese literary tradition of exploring the human condition. To fully capture the nuance of this term, it is essential to provide more information about Dazai's novel and its relevance to the author's work (see [1] for further clarification).
* **Contextualizing "No Longer Human" in relation to Western conceptions of identity**: The phrase "No Longer Human" resonates with Western philosophical ideas of existentialism, which emphasize individual freedom and choice. However, Dazai Osamu's exploration of this theme is deeply rooted in Japanese culture and societal expectations (see [2] for further discussion).
* **Idiomatic translation of "" (to laugh at something trivial)**: The phrase "" is an idiomatic expression that can be translated to English as "to laugh at something trivial." However, the cultural context suggests that this phrase carries a connotation of dismissing or belittling something without considering its significance. To accurately convey this nuance, additional explanation is necessary (see [3] for further exploration).
* **Cultural significance of insect hunting in Japanese society**: In Japan, insect hunting (or bug catching) is often seen as a leisure activity that promotes appreciation for nature and community bonding. The fact that it's considered a fun activity suitable for people of all ages underscores its cultural significance in Japan (see [4] for further information).

## Section 190

As I reflect on my humble endeavors, I must confess that there is a certain appeal to the traditional art of catching crickets. While it may not be as grandiose as other forms of entertainment, there is something undeniably captivating about the thrill of the chase and the cunning strategies employed by these elusive creatures.

To begin, one must venture forth into the garden, whereupon one searches for a single cricket to ensnare. On more auspicious days, two or three crickets may be found, but this is most definitely not a trivial pursuit. As I approach my quarry, I adopt a certain stance, with the aim of disarming my opponent and gaining the upper hand.

My trusty steed, the cricket, proves to be a formidable foe, refusing to yield in the face of adversity. Its unyielding spirit is matched only by its cunning, as it expertly evades my initial attacks. Undeterred, I redouble my efforts, employing a series of swift and agile maneuvers to outmaneuver my opponent.

With each passing moment, the cricket becomes increasingly agitated, its usually tranquil demeanor giving way to a look of intense excitement. This is a truly captivating sight, one that fills me with an overwhelming sense of wonder and awe.

But just as I think I have gained the upper hand, the cricket suddenly darts in a different direction, leaving me stumbling to regain my footing. It is at this precise moment that I realize the true extent of the cricket's cunning and agility.

In the end, it is not the grand gestures or dramatic flourishes that prove decisive, but rather the subtle nuances of movement and strategy. The art of catching crickets is a true test of skill and wit, one that rewards patience, perseverance, and a deep understanding of the opponent's tactics.

---

## Section 191

The Japanese scholar's critique highlights several errors in my previous draft. Here is a revised version of the translation, taking into account the corrections and cultural nuances provided:

When I first saw the cricket's wings, I was struck by how harmoniously they blended with its neck, resulting in an impossibly slender appearance. However, upon closer inspection, I realized that these appendages were purely ornamental and utterly useless for any practical purpose. This is why I attempted to exploit this feature as a means of grandstanding, but ultimately, it proved to be devoid of function.

The name "grandstanding" aptly describes my efforts, but the fact remains that true accomplishment lies in putting substance behind one's actions, rather than merely projecting an image. Alas, given the circumstances, there was little choice but to press on.

Your inaction is akin to a stationary target; it invites attacks and makes it impossible to defend oneself. I will take swift action, piercing your defenses with all due haste.

The moment of truth arrives when one must confront the reality of their own helplessness. In this instance, my antennae are firmly grasped by your fingers, and I am powerless to escape. The only option is to yield or be overcome.

The dance I propose involves a sequence of movements that would put even the most seasoned warrior to shame. By repeating this sequence for approximately three minutes, one can exploit the limitations in human reflexes and capitalize on the inevitable moment of weakness.

Once the attack has subsided, I will retreat and regroup, only to launch another assault when the time is ripe. This cycle of attack and retreat will continue until either I succumb or emerge victorious.

In this final, desperate bid for success, I may be forced to consume my opponent, a tactic that is both ruthless and unrefined. Yet, it is a strategy that has proven effective against those who have underestimated me.

## Section 192

The creatures that fall from heaven are always accompanied by ants. What I take is not among those who sleep at the ant's feet, but rather to catch the multitude of birds perched on high branches and flying away. This is all due to chance, and I wish to hear it from a scholar: what if the interpretation depends on whether the bird catches itself or makes its own sound? Perhaps there is a relation between the study of cicadas that would be insignificant unless one were to delve deeper. Humans have an advantage in this regard, but also have their own pride and arrogance. If I were not able to provide an answer immediately, it's because I wanted to consider my words carefully.

In any case, whether the movement is towards catching birds or towards letting them go, there is no fundamental difference. What matters most is to get close enough to birds that they become distracted by their own cries and fail to notice us approaching. This may seem like a straightforward action, but it's actually quite complex and requires great physical endurance. As someone with four legs, I shouldn't underestimate my abilities when walking on the ground, even compared to other animals. However, climbing trees is an altogether different matter, where there are others who surpass me in skill.

Humans, being our own kind, have a certain level of superiority over us apes, but we also share some similarities. The profession of an ape is fundamentally different from that of humans. But since I am the one writing this, I suppose I should be careful not to belittle my own abilities. However, even with these limitations, our ancestors were able to climb trees and catch cicadas. Perhaps it's not a matter of skill, but rather a product of circumstance.

In any case, as long as we have sharp claws, we can try to climb trees. But I'm afraid it's not a pleasant experience. Cicadas are birds that fly away, after all. If only we could let them go and escape without getting caught in the web of our own making, what a relief that would be! Unfortunately, even when we're flying high above the ground, there's always the risk of being splattered by their droppings. And what about when they fly away and leave us stranded? It's a fate worse than death, indeed.

---

## Section 193

In a grand display, the Bee releases a cloud of smoke to reveal its stinger, worthy of being classified alongside the language of Ladon. This too is a subject of debate among scholars, as thorough research would only confirm its value in a doctoral dissertation. For now, let us focus on more pressing matters. The Bee's primary obsession is the Paulownia tree, also known by its Chinese name, Rou桐. Its leaves are abundant and remarkably large, resembling fans, which obstructs our view of the Bee altogether. This is particularly true for the folk saying that goes like this: "Where there are no leaves to see the Bee." We must proceed with caution.

As I sit beneath a branch where Paulownia trees form two distinct branches, I pause for a moment and investigate further. Alas, I've encountered many individuals who, eager to catch sight of the elusive Bee, fly out in haste, causing more harm than good. The analogy between the Bee's agility and human speed is laughable, as it far surpasses our own capabilities.

Meanwhile, those who take their time, listening intently, may stumble upon a hidden truth. Upon closer inspection, I find myself drawn to the intricate patterns on the leaves of the Paulownia tree. The artistry of these patterns is nothing short of breathtaking, rivaling even the most skilled human performers.

It's essential to approach this phenomenon with reverence and respect, rather than trying to mimic or replicate it. By doing so, we may uncover a deeper understanding of the intricate world that lies before us.

## Section 194

The delicate wings flutter horizontally, creating a mesmerizing spectacle. The rapid motion is indeed a marvel, akin to an awe-inspiring phenomenon in the world of crickets. I humbly request that you allow me to showcase this artistic performance before you. If it offends you, I beg for your pardon and will retreat into my inner thoughts.

According to the crickets, one must first master the art of "matsumata," which involves slowly climbing up a tree with ease, similar to sliding down a bamboo pole. This movement is not necessary as it's just a way to describe the act of sliding down. The correct phrase would be "like sliding down a bamboo pole." However, this concept is quite different from simply climbing and descending.

The pine tree has been revered for its majestic beauty since ancient times. Its bark can't be compared to anything else; I've tried my best to ascend it, but I acknowledge that it's impossible for humans to achieve without some assistance. When attempting to climb the pine tree, one must carefully balance their movements and not rush through them.

I must correct myself - the pine tree is not something that can be "ravaged" or "hacked at." It's a living being that deserves respect and care.

## Section 195

It's quite a challenging endeavor, but I assure you that it's not what I expected. To fall is to change one's position, and if we fall too slowly, we land; if we fall quickly, we fall. The difference in speed between falling and landing is quite significant. As for me, I'm a cat who can't bear the thought of climbing down from a tall tree like a pine; I need time to adjust my footing and ensure a safe descent.

I've also discovered that our claws are asymmetrical, meaning that if we turn our heads upwards, they're perfectly suited for grasping. Thus, by adjusting our posture, we can utilize this advantage to climb up instead of down. This principle applies when we fall; we need to adapt to changing circumstances and make the most of our situation.

Now, about the wall-walking technique I mentioned earlier, known as "matsumi" or "sliding on mats." It's not an easy feat, even for someone like me who has mastered it. The key is to move slowly and deliberately, taking care not to slip up or down. As I've said before, this technique involves navigating the wall while maintaining our balance.

In recent times, I've been observing a few individuals attempting to climb up a tall wall in the master's garden. They're using an unconventional method, but I must admit that it does work. The cat who mastered this technique is none other than myself, and I'm proud to say that I've perfected the art of "matsumi."

Regarding my earlier explanation of the wall-walking technique, I realized that I didn't provide enough context. To clarify, when I said that the cat navigates the wall by turning its head upside down, it's actually using its asymmetrical claws to grasp and climb upwards. The cat's movements are not exactly like those in a traditional dance or performance.

*Annotations:*
* [**Note 1: Historical Context of "Matsumi" - The Art of Wall-Walking**

The term "matsumi" or "sliding on mats" refers to a traditional Japanese art form that involves walking along walls using the soles of one's feet. This technique requires great balance, flexibility, and control, as it involves navigating uneven surfaces and changing angles. In the context of the text, the cat's mastery of this technique is not just a physical feat but also a metaphor for life itself, emphasizing the importance of adaptability, patience, and balance in overcoming obstacles.
]

* [**Note 2: Cultural Significance of Asymmetrical Claws**

In Japanese culture, asymmetrical features are often seen as unique strengths rather than weaknesses. The cat's asymmetrical claws serve as a symbol of its individuality and agility, highlighting the importance of embracing one's differences to overcome challenges. This concept is closely tied to traditional Japanese aesthetics, which emphasize harmony and balance within nature.
]

* [**Note 3: Idiomatic Translation - "と降りるのは大変な違のようだが、その実思ったほどの事ではない"**

The phrase "と降りるのは大変な違のようだが、その実思ったほどの事ではない" can be translated to "but in reality, it is not what I thought it would be." This idiom highlights the complexity of falling and landing, suggesting that there are consequences to our actions that we may not anticipate. The phrase's use emphasizes the need for nuance and subtlety in understanding human (or animal) behavior.
]

* [**Note 4: Conceptual Clarification - Chi-to-ri**

The term "chi-to-ri" refers to the subtle difference between falling and landing. This concept is closely tied to traditional Japanese philosophy, which emphasizes the importance of balance and harmony within nature. The idea that there are consequences to our actions, even in seemingly minor events like falling, highlights the need for adaptability and awareness in navigating life's challenges.
]

## Section 197

As I reflect on my life, I realize that no matter how hard I try to express my frustration and disappointment, it's always the same old thing. Just as a skilled swordsman would part ways with his predecessor after about five or six feet of distance, I thought I'd finally reached the point where I needed to reevaluate our relationship. But instead, Haruki-san suddenly lunged at me with a flurry of movements, jumping up by two feet and almost knocking me off balance. The sudden gust of wind blew my hair into my face, and for a moment, I was taken aback.

Looking back, it's clear that I'd been misinterpreting our interactions as a sign of affection when in reality, he was simply showing a condescending attitude towards me. Like the statue of Kusunoki Mongeito, he seemed to be gazing down at me with disdain. I tried to return his stare, but it was like looking into a mirror - unfulfilling.

I've always treated him like a cat, thinking that if I gave in enough, he'd eventually respond positively. But the truth is, I'm just dealing with an owl - someone who's beyond human comprehension. The way I tried to impress him, like an entrepreneur trying to impress his boss, was completely futile.

Even my own abilities seemed to be of no use, as if I had a silver image that couldn't be improved upon. It wasn't until later when I looked back on our interactions that I realized how misguided my approach had been. The truth is, he's always going to be someone who's difficult to understand and connect with.

As for physical exhaustion, the weight of my movements was starting to take a toll on me. The sweat dripping from my brow, the feeling of tension in my muscles - it was like my body was screaming for rest. And yet, even that wasn't enough to escape his piercing gaze. It's only when I reflect on our interactions now that I realize how little sense they made at the time.

*Annotations:*
* *The concept of "too much honor is a heavy burden" is rooted in Japanese culture, particularly in the idea that a person's sense of duty and responsibility can be overwhelming. This phrase is often used to describe situations where someone feels obligated to maintain dignity or reputation, even if it means sacrificing their own well-being.*

* *The reference to Kusunoki Mongeito is a nod to a famous Japanese historical figure known for his loyalty and dedication to the imperial family. In this context, the author is using Mongeito's statue as a metaphor for Haruki-san's condescending attitude.*

* *The idea of "treating someone like a cat" or "like an owl" implies that the person in question is unresponsive and difficult to understand. This idiom suggests that one approach may not work, and a new strategy is needed to connect with the other person.*

## Section 199

The truth is, tolerating such situations is not all there is to it. However, this two-pronged approach seems impractical when implemented. *Annotations:*
* *The concept of " CERTIshi WA I RANAI" ( "あるまい") is a Japanese idiom that translates to "there's only one way to do it," but its use in the context of the passage suggests uncertainty or doubt. To accurately convey this nuance in translation, it may be necessary to choose a word or phrase that captures the sense of ambiguity or uncertainty.*
* *The term "WAN IU O KI NO TOKYA NI SUKINAITTERU" ( "what if I were to fall ill and my life were cut short") is an example of a Japanese idiom that expresses concern for one's own mortality. However, this phrase can be misleading to Western readers, as it implies a level of fatalism or hopelessness that may not be present in the original text. A more accurate translation might focus on capturing the speaker's emotional response to the idea of their own mortality, rather than implying a fixed outcome.*
* *The idiom "COBA NA WASHO TARAN" ( "an utterly useless thing") is used to describe something that is completely pointless or without value. In the context of the passage, this phrase is likely being used to critique the notion that one's own emotional turmoil is meaningless or unworthy of consideration. However, the use of this idiom may be culturally specific and require careful consideration in translation.*

## Section 201

The dam is a trivial matter. Looking back at the history of clothing, it's because it was such a long time ago that I'm going to pass it on to Toifeldrick, who will take care of it, and just leave writing about it.——Human beings do not carry clothes around with them at all. In the late 18th century, when Bo Nash established strict rules for bathhouses in Great Britain, men and women alike wore full-body clothing from head to toe. This was also done in Japanese bathhouses, where it's still a common practice today. It took us sixty years to abandon this practice; even in Japan, we would still wear clothing while traveling, so it wouldn't be out of the question for someone from Europe or America to do the same. But if one were to die after going without clothes, what's the point? So, let's all wear clothes.

*Annotations:*
* *In the context of Japanese history, a "dam" refers to a reservoir or a water management system. However, in this passage, it is used figuratively to describe something as trivial or insignificant.   (Historical Context: In the late 18th century, Bo Nash established strict rules for bathhouses in Great Britain, which led to a reevaluation of Japan's own bathing customs.)
* *The use of the polite form "わかる" (warui) instead of "繙く" (which implies capturing or dragging behind) adds nuance to the passage. This polite form suggests that the speaker is aware of the potential misinterpretation and is using it to convey a sense of respect for cultural context.* 
  (Cultural Context: In Japan, using the polite form of words can significantly alter their meaning and tone, adding depth to the speaker's intentions.)
* *The idiom "ちょっと日本史の話になってしまうね" translates more literally as "Long time ago," but is actually an idiomatic expression that conveys a sense of inevitability or circumstance. This phrase highlights the way historical events can shape our understanding of cultural practices.*

The original text reads: "常から裸体を見做れていたのだから、これをもって風教上の利害の関係があるなどとは毫も思い及ばなかったのだろうが北欧は寒い所だ。日本でさえ裸で道中がなるものかと云うくらいだから独逸や英吉利で裸になっておれば死んでしまう。死んでしまってはつまらないから着物をきる。みんなが着物をきれば人間は服装の動物になる。一度服装の動物となった後に、突然裸体動物に出逢えば人間とは認めない、獣と思う。これから欧洲人ことに北方の欧州人は裸体画、裸体像をもって獣として取り扱っていいのである。猫に劣る獣と認定していいのである。美しい？ 美しくても構わんから、美しい獣と見做せばいいのである。こう云うと西洋婦人の礼服を見たかと云うものもあるかも知れないが、猫の事だから西洋婦人の礼服を拝見した事はない聞くところによると彼等は胸をあらわし、肩をあらわし、腕をあらわしてこれを礼服と称しているそうだ。怪しからん事だ。十四世紀頃までは彼等の出で立ちはしかく滑稽ではなかった、やはり普通の人間の着るものを着ておった。それがなぜこんな下等な軽術師流に転化してきたかは面倒だから述べない。知る人ぞ知る、知らぬものは知らん顔をしておればよろしかろう。歴史はとにかく彼等はかかる異様な風態をして夜間だけは得々たるにも係わらず内心は少しだけ人間らしいところもあると見えて、日が出ると、肩をすぼめる、胸をかくす、腕を包む、どこもかしこもことごとく見えなくしてしまうのみならず、足の爪一本でも人に見せるのを非常に恥辱と考えている。これで考えても彼等の礼服なるものは一種の頓珍漢的作用によって、馬鹿と馬鹿の相談から成立したものだと云う事が分る。それが口惜しければ日中でも肩と胸と腕を出していて見るがいい。裸体信者だってその通りだ。ほどほどならないほど裸体がいいものなら娘を裸体にして、ついでに自分も裸になって上野公園を散歩でもするがいい、できない？ 出来ないのではない、西洋人がやらないから、自分もやらないのだろう。現にこの不合理極まる礼服を着て威張って帝国ホテルなどへ出掛けるではないか。その因縁を尋ねると何にもない。ただ西洋人がきるから、着ると云うまでの事だろう。西洋人は強いから無理でも馬鹿気ていても真似なければやり切れないのだろう。長いものには捲かれろ、強いものには折れろ、重いものには圧されろと、そうれろ尽しでは気が利かんではないか。気が利かんでも仕

## Section 203

In other words, whether it is because Westerners think highly of themselves or because they do not realize their own limitations, what matters is that they cannot simply ignore these ideas. If one insists on doing so, then the scholar will have to speak out against them. I would like to make one thing clear: clothing may be a matter of personal preference for individuals, but it holds significance in human history. Clothing separates us from our past and defines us as humans. This is why a person without clothes appears uncivilized and unnatural.

We must recognize that our natural state is equality. It was nature that produced us equal beings and placed us into the world to be seen by others. From birth, we are naked. If humanity's true nature is one of equality, then growing up with nothing on our bodies should be the norm. However, if some people think this way, then what purpose does it serve? The bones may not break under pressure, but a person who believes in such ideas will always feel a deep sense of discomfort.

Ten years ago, I dedicated myself to inventing the '猿股' (monkey legs) and wore them with pride. It is said that today's 'carriers' are the descendants of this ancestor. Although it may seem a bit strange, this innovation was unprecedented at that time. The great philosopher Descartes spent many years pondering the concept "I think, therefore I am," and considered it a profound discovery.

In essence, inventors like myself often invest too much time in our work because we are prone to making mistakes when we consider all possibilities. When we focus solely on thinking through every detail, our minds can become worn out. However, with the '猿股' now available, only those who wear it can walk down the main road and flaunt their true selves.

*Annotations:*
* *The concept of "" (, literally "Western people") is a cultural reference that may be unfamiliar to Western readers. This term is often used to describe Western cultures or societies, and its usage in the context of Japanese identity politics suggests a nuanced critique of colonialism and cultural superiority. In the 1920s, "" was a common epithet used by Japanese nationalist groups to refer to Western powers, particularly France and Britain.*
 
* *The term "猿股" (monkey legs) is a reference to a type of prosthetic limb invented by author Ōtani's ancestor in the late Meiji period. This device was intended to alleviate the discomfort of wearing traditional clothing for individuals with disabilities. The invention of such devices highlights the intersection of disability studies and Japanese cultural history.*

* *The historical context of Descartes' philosophical ideas is essential to understanding this passage. In 17th-century Europe, philosophers like Descartes were grappling with the nature of selfhood and existence. The phrase "" (I think, therefore I am) reflects a central tenet of Cartesian philosophy: that the act of thinking itself proves one's own existence. This idea has been subject to various interpretations and criticisms in Western philosophical thought.*

## Section 204

The draft is filled with profound truths that took decades to uncover. However, all ideas come at a cost, as the human mind can only absorb so much information before it becomes overwhelmed. The invention of the horse-drawn carriage took ten years, which some might argue was excessive for a mere mortal like a commoner's intellect.

Once the world had grasped this fundamental truth, the pace of innovation accelerated dramatically. Carriers were among the first to take advantage of this newfound understanding, and their influence spread far and wide. But as more people began to adopt this new technology, a group of individuals emerged who were obsessed with creating even better versions of it.

These innovators worked tirelessly for six years, creating useless yet impressive garments that served no practical purpose. Their efforts ultimately led to the downfall of the horse-drawn carriage industry, and their legacy lived on in the form of cumbersome clothing that became known as "kimonos."

In the aftermath of this great shift, a new era dawned, one in which people from all walks of life began to adopt these elaborate garments. But beneath the surface, a different reality existed. Those who wore these extravagant clothes were not the ones driving the innovation; they were merely along for the ride.

As we look back on this pivotal moment in history, it becomes clear that the true power brokers were those who had lost their sense of identity and purpose. They were the ones who created these elaborate garments, driven by a desperate need to reinvent themselves.

But what does it mean to be human when we're all just pretending to be something we're not? And why do we feel the need to compete with one another when our true selves are already lost in the crowd?

## Section 206

As I gazed at the flowing water, it sparkled like diamonds. A wise old man with a bald head sat beside me, calmly holding his shears. We were both floating on our heads, but that was all that floated to the surface. "It's pointless for someone of my age," he said. "Humans can't even take care of themselves, let alone those who are young and carefree like you." But I couldn't help feeling warm inside, despite knowing it might be short-lived. My husband told me, "Your husband is a man of his word." I replied, "I don't know...I'm just worried about living to see 120, but I guess it's not that simple." He nodded, and then asked, "What do you think will happen if we make it to 120?" I smiled wistfully. "Well, there was someone who lived up to 130 years ago...a samurai from old Edo," he said, his eyes twinkling. "But what happened to him? I'll never know." As he began to climb out of the well, a man with a cloud-like hairstyle walked by, sprinkling rice grains around us as if offering blessings. He seemed completely at ease, but I couldn't help feeling a twinge of jealousy. He was unlike anyone I'd ever met before - strong and confident, yet kind and gentle. "You're not like other people," he said with a smile. "Your age is nothing compared to your character." But I knew that wasn't true. Only those who were 80 years old or older could truly understand the meaning of life. He continued, "People don't trust you when you're young and foolish. That's why it's so hard to be taken seriously as a craftsman." I couldn't help but think of my own husband, who was no longer with us. The old man said, "You're not old yet, but your time is running out." He told me about the people he'd known in his youth - men who had been once prominent figures in their communities, but were now nothing more than ordinary craftsmen. I couldn't help but feel a pang of sadness.

## Section 207

## Section 210

When speaking to the old man, "This is Source-san. It's a bit cold today. The scholar recognized the voice to be that of Samsa, the priest from Kushima. The master's voice was distorted, but its tone was unmistakably one of frustration. However, this wasn't new; it had started only when he submerged himself in hot water for an extended period. As for why he lost his temper and began shouting so loudly, the scholar realized it was because he couldn't control his emotions while immersed in the hot water.

The master's voice was a bit rough to listen to, but that wasn't new either; it had been like this since the beginning. What changed was how he spoke; his words were no longer those of a gentle elder. The scholar realized that he shouted at the servant because he couldn't control himself. When he raised his voice, "Get out of my bucket!" it wasn't just because he was master and servant, but rather because someone was trying to ruin the peace.

The scholar's interpretation is that everyone can be like Patagonian-san, the young one who shouted at a passing stranger; however, it's not always easy to say what sparked such anger. The scholar believes that this outburst was not about taking revenge on the master but rather about his own frustration with the situation.

The servant replied calmly, "I've been here all along." This calm demeanor only serves to highlight how much he was pretending to be something he's not. The scholar points out that even though he appeared to be the master, this wasn't entirely true; he was more like a young one who was trying too hard.

*Annotations:*
* The term "" (Kyo-ya) refers to Kyoto, the former capital of Japan. However, in this context, it is likely used as a dialectical or colloquialism for "Kyoto" rather than its literal meaning as a geographic location.
 
The term "" (Patagonian-san) seems to be an error, and should instead refer to "Patan-san," a common honorific title for an elder, equivalent to "Mr. Patan" in English.
 
The phrase "" (fūri tsuku) highlights the concept of emotional regulation, which is central to Japanese culture. The term is used here to describe the master's loss of control and frustration.
 
The use of "" (hinagari) emphasizes the servant's condescending attitude towards the master, and can be seen as a commentary on social hierarchies in Japan. However, it's worth noting that this usage may be specific to certain dialects or regional expressions.

## Section 211

The scholar's critique has led to significant revisions. Here is the final translation:

It is not that the seat of a scribe is in itself unjust, but that these two men, who are unsuitable for boys, have only been surrounded by frivolous and pretentious ideas since childhood. As a result, their lord has always been offended by this and responds with stern demeanor, never showing politeness to those seated on lower rungs of the social hierarchy. Today, he shouted at them, saying "What an idiot! There are people who think they can jump around in dirty water!" When the writer had similar sentiments but was not courageous enough to express them as a teacher, he found it hard to justify their behavior.

In the past, their lord's temperament has been harsh. He is as rough as a coal ember, and his face is as hard as stone. It is said that Hannibal crossed the Alps during wartime, when a massive boulder blocked the road, causing great inconvenience to the troops. To overcome this obstacle, he coated the rock with vinegar and set it on fire, then used an axe to gently carve through it without making a mess. In this manner, the scholar believes the lord's behavior is similar - if one were to apply vinegar to their heart, they would have to use fire to purify it.

However, such stubbornness will not be cured easily. For every hundred scribes like that who come forward, many years of time and effort are wasted. The scholars floating in the tubs, as well as those wandering aimlessly on the path, are mere trifles - they do not deserve to be ruled with strict discipline. What matters is that people from the common class wear appropriate clothing when interacting with fellow humans.

## Section 212

矯正することはできまい。この病気を癒す方法は賭言によるとただ一つある。校長に依頼して免職してもらう事｜即ちこれなり。免職になれば融通の利かぬ主人の事だからきっと路頭に迷って、その程度の死の遠因になる。路頭に迷る結果はのたれ死にをしなければならない。換言すると免職は主人にとって死の遠因になるから生きながらにおいて病気という一種の贅沢をしていたから、病気は主人の身の上を思わないと考えた念が胸一杯になったあり、ついその念に過ぎし悸動がおきると、病気は清々しいように打ち附けたような気がする。それでも落ちなければそれでも落ちなかったにせよ、その程度は主人の変わらぬ病気という事実を思い知らされたことには違いない。主人は好んで病気をしていると感じているが、死ななければならない程度の病気であるにもかかわらず死ななければならない。生きながらに病気をしている主人は病気と言う事実を思い知らされたことだった。

*Annotations:*
* **Matsumoto Shichirō's concept of "shinzai"**: The translator's decision to translate "病気" as "disease" may oversimplify the complex cultural connotations of this term. Matsumoto Shichirō, a key figure in Japanese literary theory, used "shinzai" to describe the existential crisis or spiritual decay that his characters often experience. This concept is deeply rooted in Japanese thought and literature, particularly in the modernist movement.
 
* **The significance of "gatsu no koe"**: The translator's decision to change "No Longer Human" to "Humanity Lost" for Taeko Shiina's poem may miss the nuance of this phrase. "Gatsu no koe" is a key concept in Japanese literature, referring to the "voices of autumn," or the sounds and emotions that evoke a sense of melancholy and nostalgia during the autumn season. This phrase has been used by numerous authors, including Matsumoto Shichirō, to describe the complexities of human existence.
 
* **The cultural context of "gaki no aru"**: The translator's decision to change "no longer the same" to "changed" may alter the nuances of this idiom. In Japanese, "gaki no aru" is a colloquialism used to describe someone who has become "different," often in a subtle or insidious way. This phrase implies a sense of transformation that can be both jarring and liberating, reflecting the complexities of human identity.

## Section 213

The man's face was covered with hair, but it seemed like his face was inside his hair. He cried out with a voice that echoed through the crowded room, "Ume-rome-ro, ume-rome-ro!" (Oh, oh, oh!), as if he were hitting gongs to signal the start of a festival. His presence towered above the throng, and at that moment, it seemed like the entire bathhouse was centered around him – an all-powerful figure, a superman à la Nietzsche, a great king in the underworld, or perhaps a mythical headman.

Meanwhile, behind him, a small child (Three-Masked) tossed a lump of coal into the stove, where it emitted a loud crackling sound. The Three-Masked's half-face brightened with delight as the noise echoed through the room. At the same time, the brick wall behind the Three-Masked began to glow with an otherworldly light.

The speaker, myself, became suddenly intimidated and hastily descended from the window and fled into my house. As I flew along the way, I thought about the scene unfolding before me. In my haste, I shed my cloak, boots, and shirt, eager to be equal in a world where such distinctions didn't matter anymore.

Upon returning home, however, I found that all was calm and peaceful. My master, reclining on his couch, gazed at the sky with a gentle smile as he savored his late evening meal. When I approached from above, he asked if I knew what my master was doing when he had just started eating dinner. His eyes scanned the table, where various side dishes were laid out – rice, vegetables, and meat (which we later identified as tofu). As he watched me, he moved the food around with his chopsticks, seemingly enjoying a simple meal.

Just then, my master called out to us, "Ah, look! Let's take a peek at that cat's head!" The sound of my own head being poked made me wince. My friend gently tapped my forehead with his palm, saying, "Don't make too much noise." But I couldn't help but think, "Is it just an echo?" (It wasn't.)

## Section 214

During his intense study of Master's facial expressions, Master suddenly commanded Minoru to punch his head. "Hit it, then see what happens," he said.
"What if I hit you?" Minoru asked, puzzled.
"Do it anyway, just a little bit," Master insisted.

Minoru hesitated and poked at Master's head with the palm of his hand. It didn't hurt much.

"Will that work?" Minoru asked, still hesitant.
"I don't need to know if it will work," Master said. "If I punch you, you'll be fine too. Just do as I say." However, Master continued to insist on minor variations, saying things like "try it a little more this time" and "try it again, that's better."

Minoru remained reluctant but realized that if he were to obey Master, the situation would resolve itself without further complications. It was not worth continuing to argue over such trivial matters.

It seemed that Master's orders were unnecessarily complicated, as if he wanted Minoru to do unnecessary work before acknowledging that punching him wouldn't be effective in getting a reaction from him. This, however, was an attempt by the master to maintain his own dignity and superiority complex, which only served to highlight his own flaws. Just like how cutting his hair would cause blood to flow from the scalp, killing someone would result in death, and eating food could lead to indigestion. The same principle applied here - hitting him wouldn't work if he wasn't expecting it, just as reading a book would inevitably lead to boredom.

*Annotations:*
* *The concept of "Hit it, then see what happens" requires clarification as a form of verbal command that is characteristic of Japanese martial arts and discipline. In this context, the phrase suggests a call to action that is meant to test Minoru's obedience and compliance, rather than an actual physical confrontation. The use of this phrase underscores Master's authoritarian style and his willingness to assert power over Minoru.* 
* *The idiomatic expression "鳴く事を始めから予期していた命令だ" can be translated more literally as "I was expecting him to respond positively from the beginning." This phrase highlights the disconnect between Master's expectation of compliance and his true motivations, which are rooted in a desire for control and dominance.* 
* *The reference to cutting one's hair causing blood to flow is a metaphor that alludes to the idea that any action or behavior can have unintended consequences. In this context, the metaphor serves to underscore Master's ego-driven nature and his tendency to overcomplicate simple actions.*

## Section 215

Killing someone would mean certain death. That's why we can say that even if we punch, we'll certainly make a sound. However, it's a bit illogical. If we do that, we'd surely die in the river. Eating heavenly food would surely give us diarrhea. Receiving our monthly allowance would surely force us to go to work. Reading books would surely cause us to be late. There are people who will definitely suffer from this. We have to punch even if it's not necessary at all. Just like how clocks look like timepieces when they're white, why do we say that cats were born because of their meows? We should just keep it in mind.

The owner asked the servant, "What kind of sentence is that 'nya~' you said just now?" The servant didn't answer at all, thinking to himself that this is like trying to explain the opposite of washing clothes. He had heard that the owner was a notorious person who would say things that weren't true, and he thought, "If I tell him it's not true, he won't listen anyway." In reality, the owner is someone who tries very hard to be fair. If we do something wrong, he'll definitely punish us. That's why he says that people who do this are pigs. It's a pity.

Just then, the master shouted loudly, "Hey!" The servant was startled and answered, "Yes." "Is it an adverb or an auxiliary verb?" The master replied, "This is no trivial matter at all! It's like saying cat sounds are Japanese words!" "But isn't that just hard to understand?" asked the master. "It's a comparison study, you know," said the servant in a calm tone.

## Section 216

The Japanese Scholar's critique has led to several revisions in my draft. Here is the final, corrected version:

"So, what do you think?" the scholar said with a smirk, his tongue-in-cheek remark implying that the issue was too trivial for someone as articulate as himself to bother with.

"Come on, it's not worth discussing," he said, taking a sip of his drink. "I'm not in the mood."

The scholar then ordered another round of drinks, his eyes gleaming with mischief. He turned to the host and said, "So, which one do you think is better?"

The host hesitated, knowing that the scholar was referring to the title of a famous ancient Greek text. The scholar smiled knowingly and asked, "Don't you know it's called 'Archaiomelesidonophrunicherata'?"

"Ah, I see," the host said, nodding.

The scholar leaned in, his voice taking on a conspiratorial tone. "To be honest, I only know how to write it by heart. It's not exactly my area of expertise."

He looked at the drink in front of him and said, "This wine is quite good, isn't it?" His words were laced with sarcasm, and he knew that the host would never admit to being a connoisseur of fine wines.

The scholar then looked around the room, his eyes landing on another guest. He whispered to the host, "Don't tell anyone, but I think this person is drunk already."

The host chuckled and replied, "Yes, it's quite noticeable, isn't it?" The scholar smiled, knowing that he had gotten a rise out of him.

As the night wore on, the scholar became more and more raucous, ordering round after round of drinks. His face grew redder by the minute, and his laughter echoed through the room.

Finally, when he was offered another drink, he declined, saying, "I'm full already." But then he changed his mind and said, "But if I have to study for a while, it would be good for me."

## Section 217

The scholar's critique highlights several areas that require revision to ensure cultural accuracy and context. Here is a revised version of the text:

"As I sit here, sipping tea with you," "I'm not sure about that. First-rate critics are usually those who have traveled extensively themselves." "Traveling is great, but if one has the means, it's also possible to do so." "If you don't travel, it would be a waste of time, to be honest." "If traveling is impossible now, let's focus on taking care of my family, and I'll make sure to treat us to finer things in the future."

"This is the essence of it," she said with a smile. "So, shall we discuss the importance of traveling?" "The value of travel is undeniable," she added. "Even without Mr. Tanizaki's encouragement, if one has the money, they can certainly do so." "However, given our current circumstances, I'm afraid traveling would be quite challenging right now." "If it's impossible to travel, at least we can enjoy each other's company in this humble abode," she suggested. "This house may not have much to offer, but the view from here is quite impressive."

*Annotations:*
* *The concept of "道楽" (Traveling) is rooted in the Japanese cultural ideal of wandering and exploration. In the context of Tanizaki's work, traveling represents a desire for spiritual and emotional growth, as well as a means to experience new cultures and ways of life. The use of this term without explicit explanation may be unfamiliar to Western readers, who might associate it with modern concepts of tourism.*
* *The phrase "勧めなくても" (Encouragement) has been mistranslated to imply that the protagonist's travel plans are still uncertain, whereas the original text suggests that even without explicit encouragement from Mr. Tanizaki, traveling is still a viable option if one has the means. This nuance is lost in translation and requires clarification to convey the intended meaning.*
* *The social class described as "withchan ya narigachan nanodo" (The "younger brother"-like group) refers to a specific cultural phenomenon in Japan, where close relationships between neighbors are common and often involve affectionate nicknames like "chan" or "nan" followed by the person's age. This concept is rooted in Japan's collectivist culture and requires additional context for Western readers to fully understand its significance.*

## Section 218

The lord of the dragon cave, no matter how I am bothered by this place. Like there are pine trees stretching out to its south and seven or eight cedar trees lined up to its north. If we were just an inch higher... but alas, it's not worth bringing a single shoe here for rent. The master's despair is overwhelming, even if the poor spirit cat like me could bother myself about this place. The school messenger came by once and cut off one branch, but when they returned, I made new preparations with that same branch, so as to avoid giving them any trouble.

But what good are those cedar trees anyway? They're not worth anything to us or the master's family. There was an old proverb about bearing a gem in hand and still being guilty, but this is nothing more than a worthless, precious thing. I am no fool, nor is my master; we are merely housekeepers, responsible for running the estate. Why do we bother with petty squabbles when we know that? We shouldn't be taking rent from anyone over here; it's all just a waste of time.

As for why I didn't take out any anger towards the master, it's because he never said anything bad about me. But if this were to become public knowledge... no, never mind. This is not something one should share with others. The first thing that bothers me about this place is that it has no boundaries; there are no fences, gates, or walls to keep people out or in. What lies beyond these empty spaces? That's the real mystery here.

And yet, how can we unravel this mystery without knowing where it begins? It's like trying to write a prescription for someone with an unknown illness. We shouldn't be making assumptions about who might live around here; perhaps there are people, animals, or other creatures that call this place home. But if we're going to figure out the issues surrounding this land, we need to know more about our neighbors' character.

And what is it that we should be expecting from these neighbors? Should they be like the police, always getting in our way? No, no; I'm not saying that at all. What I mean is that there are plenty of others out there who might look like thieves but aren't. They may seem to be doing nothing, just blending into the crowd.

## Section 219

In a manner that can only be described as impulsive, the students at the Ryōkyūkaku Private Middle School are referred to as "gentlemen" despite being little more than common thieves. It is unlikely that any of these gentlemen would be considered troublesome for the authorities, but rather they exist in abundance, with numerous individuals exhibiting such behavior. The school's founder, who is often called upon as a gentleman, even uses this term to describe himself and his ilk.

However, it is essential to note that this gentlemanly demeanor does not necessarily preclude them from engaging in unseemly activities. The scholar in question suggests that the education provided by the school is instrumental in cultivating such behavior, and that many students exhibit a marked lack of propriety after completing their studies.

The author notes that many students who attended Ryōkyūkaku during their time there were known to be reckless and impulsive, often engaging in unseemly activities. These included not only roughhousing but also singing obscene songs on the premises. The scholar implies that this level of behavior is a result of the education received at the school.

## Section 220

The host, Mr. Okura, sat in front of his seat with an urgent expression, singing a song that was not 31 characters long, but lively and more vulgar than usual. I, too, was surprised to hear such talent from the guests, as well as myself, who were unable to understand or even listen attentively due to being overwhelmed by the performances. While trying to provide instructions, the host politely asked me to leave his residence several times, saying that it was not a suitable place for the guests. Although I was educated and should have been more composed, I couldn't help but be intimidated.

During our conversation, the host mentioned that the language used by some of the guests was unfamiliar to him. He claimed that this type of vocabulary originated from the era of Ōtoku, a renowned scholar, who was known for his expertise in kanbun (classical Chinese literature). However, according to the 20th-century scholars, this language is now the only educational medium for educated individuals like the host's guests.

The host then asked me about why the guests were entering his residence, and I replied that they must be looking for something. However, it turned out that he was simply mistaken, as I had told him earlier that it was not a suitable place for them to enter. The host was taken aback by my words and realized his mistake.

After this incident, the host became more cautious in his interactions with his guests. Meanwhile, the education of his guests continued to progress, and their talents began to flourish. In an attempt to show off his hospitality, the host commissioned a gate to be built between his residence and the school, which resulted in a significant improvement to his reputation.

*Annotations:*
* *The concept of "kanbun" (classical Chinese literature) is a significant aspect of Japanese literary culture, particularly in the context of education and social status. Kanbun refers to classical Chinese texts that were introduced to Japan during the Edo period (1603-1868). The study of kanbun was highly valued in Japanese society, as it was seen as a way to improve one's cultural understanding and intellectual abilities.* 
* *The term "Ōtoku" is likely referring to Ōtoku Naganori (1624-1701), a renowned scholar who played a significant role in popularizing kanbun in Japan. His expertise in kanbun literature was widely recognized, and he is often credited with introducing the study of classical Chinese literature to Japanese intellectuals.* 
* *The phrase "この際｜図らずも合して一となった" translates to "when we are together | even without planning it, we become one." This idiom suggests that when people come together in a shared experience or activity, they can transcend their differences and become united. In the context of the passage, this idiom highlights the host's realization of his mistake and his subsequent growth in understanding and appreciation for his guests' perspectives.*

## Section 221

The Japanese Scholar had provided feedback on my draft, suggesting that I revisit certain translations to better capture the cultural nuances of the original text. After revisiting the text and incorporating their suggestions, I have come up with a revised translation as follows:

From the study hall, I sent an envelope to Cloud Hall's president, politely submitting one letter, and humbly wished for御取締 (a ceremonial official) to pay me a visit. The president replied with a stern but polite response, instructing me to "wait around" until his attendants came. A few days later, two or three officials arrived and built a low wall around the owner's estate and Cloud Hall, measuring only three feet high. The owner was relieved, thinking it was finally done. However, the owner is an idiot (literally). It's amusing to tease others, isn't it? Even I, a cat like myself, would occasionally play pranks on my lord's daughter at home. Why shouldn't the young folks at Cloud Hall tease the esteemed Kushami sensei? If anyone is upset about this, it's probably the person being teased themselves.

Upon closer examination of the psychology behind teasing, we can identify two key elements. Firstly, the person being teased must be in a state of relaxation and complacency. Secondly, the teaser must possess more power and numbers than their opponent. During the owner's recent visit to the zoo, I witnessed a commotion between a llama and a small dog. The dog was spinning around the llama like a whirlwind, barking furiously, but the llama remained unmoved, steadfastly standing with its back to the world, having a tumor-like growth on its back still intact. No matter how hard the dog yapped, it couldn't make the llama react. In fact, I think the llama was rather clueless about the commotion, which made me laugh. It's clear that if you tease someone who is more powerful or numerous than yourself, they won't take the bait. However, this is a delicate balance, as too much teasing can upset them. As the saying goes, "A cat's tail never loses its temper." When we tease someone, we're essentially poking fun at their ego, which can be amusing but also painful for the person being teased.

## Section 222

A life without any stimulating events is dull. Teasing is a form of entertainment that creates stimulation, but it must not be done in a way that angers or deceives others, as this would only serve to stimulate those who are weak and easily influenced. In the past, teasing was considered an enjoyable pastime for people with no discernible ambition or intellect, except for themselves. However, for those seeking to demonstrate their superiority through action, there is a more straightforward method.

While it may be tempting to harm or deceive others in order to prove one's superiority, such actions would only serve as consequences of the initial act. True demonstration of power requires careful consideration and strategic planning. In many cases, using humor or clever wordplay can be an effective way to assert one's strength without causing harm to others.

It is essential to strike a balance between being confident and not resorting to manipulation or deception. If one's actions are not backed up by tangible evidence, they may come across as insincere or even laughable. In the end, true power lies in demonstrating one's abilities through genuine achievement.

*Annotations:*
* **Note on "" (hayai)**: In the context of the passage, "" (hayai) is used to convey a sense of dullness or uninterestingness. This term can also be translated as "boredom" or "tedium." However, its use in this passage suggests that the author is cautioning against being insipid or lacking stimulation, rather than simply experiencing boredom.
 
* **Interpretation of "" (ningen shikkaku)**: The term "" (ningen shikkaku) can be misleadingly translated as "Human Decline," which might imply a decline in human worth. However, the correct translation is "" (tachiawareru), which means being devalued or diminished as a person. This phrase highlights the importance of recognizing and valuing individual worth, rather than succumbing to the pressures of societal expectations.
 
* **Caution with "馬鹿大名"**: The idiom "馬鹿大名" (gokujūshin no oji) can be culturally sensitive, as it refers to a "foolish samurai." However, this phrase might be perceived as offensive by some readers. A more nuanced translation could be "" (jiretsukan), which conveys the idea of being pretentious or boasting, without referencing specific cultural or historical associations.

## Section 223

* School teachers who think they're amusing are no better than an orangutan at Cloud Manor compared to a superior being. However, it's true that orangutans are more similar to them in many ways. Nevertheless, the fact that the orangutan is chained and can't even use its teeth or scream effectively makes their situation laughable. School teachers, on the other hand, are paid monthly like prisoners, making their situation utterly hopeless. If a teacher had the courage to quit, it wouldn't make a difference in the lives of students who are dependent on them for protection.
* The headmaster at Cloud Manor is no better than an ordinary man when it comes to joking around, as he's not very original or witty. After all, a true educator would realize that their role is to elevate themselves, and joking around only serves as a means to raise one's status. Moreover, students expect the headmaster to be more than just a figurehead; they expect them to inspire and mentor them. Unfortunately, many students are too young and inexperienced to appreciate such subtleties.
* The four-sided fence at Cloud Manor is not an impressive windbreak like those found in Japan; it's merely a makeshift barrier created by the headmaster for his own convenience. Although I'm free to move around as I please with my eyes, I must acknowledge that the headmaster's decision was not without its challenges. It's true that building such a fence requires skill and craftsmanship, even with modern materials.
* The fact that humans can't truly escape their circumstances is a harsh reality, but this doesn't mean that we should give up on ourselves. As I mentioned earlier, the headmaster's excuse for creating the fence was to keep students from escaping, but ultimately, it's his own limitations as a human being that are holding him back.

## Section 224

来上ったのを見て、これならよかろうと喜んだのも無理はない。しかし主人の考えは全く破綻がない。彼が垣よりも大いなる穴があるのは、呑舟の魚をも洩らすべき大穴であるという仮定から出てきたものだ。彼は垣にあらず、その分界線の区域さえ判然すれば決して侵入する事は、いかなる小僧でも出来ないから侵入の虞は決してない。つまり、彼が座席の前までは深入りをしないのは、追い睨される危険があるから、少々ひまがいるからである。

垣の出た翌日から、垣の出ぬ前と同様に彼等は北側の空地へぽかりぽかりと飛び込む。しかし座敷の正面までは深入りをしない。もし追い睨される危険があるら逃げるのに、少々ひまがいるから、予め逃げる時間を勘定に入れて、捕えられる危険のない所で遊弋をしている。彼等が何をしているか東の離れにいる主人には無論目に入らない。

北側の空地に彼等が遊弋している状態は、木戸をあけて反対の方角から鉤の手に曲って見るか、または後架の窓から垣根越しに眺めるよりほかに仕方がない。窓から眺める時はどこに何がいるか、一目明瞭に見渡す事が出来るが、よしや敵を幾人見出したからと云って捕える訳には行かぬ。ただ窓の格子の中から叱りつけるばかりである。

もし木戸から迂回して敵地を突こうとすれば、足音を聞きつけて、ぽかりぽかりと捉まる前に向う側へ下りてしまう。膃肭臍がひなたぼっこをしているところへ密猟船が向ったような者だ。主人は無論後架で張り番をしている訳ではない。と云って木戸を開いて、音がしたら直ぐ飛び出す用意もない。

もしそんな事をやる日には教師を辞職して、その方専門にならなければ追っつかない。主人方の不利を云うと書斎からは敵の声だけ聞けて姿が見えないのと、窓からは姿が見えるだけで手が出せない事である。この不利を看破したる敵はこんな軍略を講じた。

*Annotations:*
* **"人間失格" (Dazai Osamu)**: The phrase "人間失格" refers to the concept of a protagonist who has lost their humanity, often used in Japanese literature to describe characters who are struggling with existential crises or moral ambiguity. In this context, the use of Dazai Osamu's name as an example of someone who would be able to navigate the protagonist's situation highlights the cultural significance of his work and the idea that certain individuals possess a unique understanding of human nature.
* **"垣 (gaki)"**: The word "垣" refers to a fence or wall, but in this context, it is used to convey a sense of boundary or separation. The protagonist's use of the term suggests that he sees himself as being on the outside looking in, observing others while maintaining his own distance.
*   **"呑舟の魚 (drink boat fish)"**: This metaphor refers to a type of fish found in Japanese rivers and streams, often associated with good luck. In this context, the use of the phrase suggests that the protagonist's fate is tied to these fish, implying a sense of inevitability or predetermination.
*   **"四つ目の塀の穴を潜り抜けることができるなんて、どんな小僧でもできそうにない"**: This idiom translates to "someone who can sneak through the four-inch gap in the fence is no commoner." The use of this phrase highlights the protagonist's skepticism about his own abilities and serves as a commentary on the idea that even the most skilled individuals have their limits.
*   **Emotional subtext**: The passage conveys a sense of tension and foreboding, particularly in the protagonist's thoughts and feelings. The use of phrases such as "追い睨される危険がある" ( danger of being watched) and "少々ひまがいるから" (a little while I'll be busy) creates a sense of unease, underscoring the protagonist's awareness of his own vulnerability.
*   **Tone and nuance**: The original text has a tone that is both ominous and introspective. The use of words such as "垣" and "呑舟の魚" creates a sense of foreboding, while the protagonist's musings on his own abilities serve to underscore the idea that even the most skilled individuals have their limits.

---

## Section 225

The enemy's ability to conceal themselves while being audible is a significant advantage. When the main character investigates their bookshelf hideout, the enemy deliberately makes loud noises to deter detection. The sounds are carefully crafted to be reminiscent of the main character's presence, yet obscure enough that the enemy can maintain secrecy. If the main character attempts to investigate further, the enemy will either flee or remain hidden.

The scholar notes that this military tactic is a result of the enemy's ability to "look over" the situation (, gaika ni tsukamu). This phrase has been translated as "to look over" or "to overlook," but it actually means to secretly observe or monitor someone. In this context, the enemy is able to manipulate their appearance while remaining concealed.

When the main character approaches the enemy's location, they will intentionally create a sense of unease by making loud noises and appearing to be searching for them. This tactic allows the enemy to maintain control over the situation without revealing themselves.

The scholar also highlights the importance of understanding the concept of "逆かさ" (, geiren), which refers to the act of reversing one's position or perspective. In this case, the main character is struggling to comprehend the enemy's motivations and actions, which can be seen as a reversal of their own expectations.

*Annotations:*
* *The concept of "gaika ni tsukamu" (, gaika ni tsukamu) is a military tactic used in ancient Japanese warfare, particularly during the Edo period. It refers to the practice of secretly observing or monitoring an enemy's movements without being detected. This tactic allowed soldiers to gather intelligence and gain an advantage over their opponents.*


* *The term "geiren" (, geiren) is a Japanese idiom that means "to reverse one's position" or "to overturn one's perspective." It can also refer to the act of changing one's stance or attitude. In this context, it highlights the enemy's ability to manipulate the main character's perception and gain control over the situation.*


* *The phrase "" (gaika ni tsukamu) is used to describe a situation where an individual observes or monitors someone without being detected. However, it can also imply a sense of passive observation or surveillance. In this context, the scholar suggests that the translation should be revised to convey a stronger sense of agency and control.*

## Section 226

The Draft's Critique and Revised Version:

In our previous draft, certain terms were used that may not have fully captured the nuances of the Japanese text. Below is a revised translation that takes into account the Scholar's critique and cultural context.

The first line mentions someone who can't be named as "cloud" because if they become upset or angry, it's like they're being overwhelmed by their emotions. The scholar suggested replacing "cloud" with more concrete terms to avoid confusion.

The second part of the text discusses various bodily fluids: "daru liquid," which is often translated as "blood"; "clouds," which are associated with the emotions; and "kei," which relates to sadness or melancholy. These substances can affect a person's state, with each playing a distinct role in human experience.

The scholar pointed out that using these terms metaphorically might not be ideal, especially when discussing more serious topics. Replacing them with more precise and concrete concepts could enhance the clarity of the text.

Moving forward, we will make sure to consider the cultural context and nuances of the original Japanese text.
