## Section 227

The Japanese Scholar critiques the draft, pointing out that the concept of "Inspirations" is rooted in the idea of "Reverse-Ides" or "Upsetting Names," which are names given to certain ideas or words that can stir up strong emotions and reactions. These Reverse-Ides are not actually inspirations but rather a product of human imagination and invention.

The Scholar notes that even the name "Inspirations" is itself an example of a Reverse-Ide, as it is meant to convey a sense of creative spark or idea generation, when in fact it can also be seen as a provocative and potentially destabilizing concept.

Furthermore, the Scholar observes that many of the so-called "Inspiration-Inducing" practices, such as eating twelve plums daily or jumping into hot springs with cannons (as described by certain scholars), are actually pseudoscientific or based on flawed assumptions. The idea is that if one performs a certain action or ritual, they will somehow tap into creative energies and generate ideas.

The Scholar criticizes this approach, arguing that true inspiration cannot be forced or manufactured through arbitrary practices. Instead, it requires a genuine connection to one's own thoughts, emotions, and experiences.

In the context of the essay, the Scholar suggests that the author should focus on exploring their own thoughts and feelings rather than relying on contrived exercises in creativity.

---

## Section 228

When one imitates the behavior of an ancient person, they may think that inspiration will arise. This is based on a psychological theory that suggests that by mimicking the actions and behaviors of someone else, our mental state can become similar to theirs. For instance, if you see someone who is drunk and playing the flute, without realizing it, your own emotions can become carefree. Similarly, when we sit zazen with a single candle flame in front of us, we can eventually get into a carefree state as well. Therefore, imitating famous people's actions will inevitably lead to success, as seen in the cases of Hugo and Stevenson. However, despite numerous attempts by many people, no one has achieved true success. For now, let us focus on understanding human nature rather than seeking inspiration.

We should refrain from assuming that there is a specific moment when inspiration strikes. Rather, we must acknowledge that such moments are rare and unpredictable.

It's essential to recognize that even in the most significant events, small incidents often precede them. As historians, it's crucial not to focus solely on major events but also to consider smaller ones. This is because major events often arise from a series of smaller incidents. By paying attention to these smaller incidents, we can better understand how they contribute to larger events. Furthermore, when writing about significant events, we must avoid trivializing them by neglecting the smaller incidents that led up to them. Otherwise, our narrative may become incomplete and unconvincing.

In the case of the protagonist's revenge against his enemies, let us not forget that he is a complex character with his own strengths and weaknesses. Rather than focusing solely on his revenge, we should aim to portray him in a nuanced and realistic light. We must also avoid glorifying his actions or making them seem excessively dramatic. Instead, our goal is to create a story that is engaging yet honest.

## Section 229

There is no longer a human in me. I am nothing but a dam of bullets, firing continuously at my own life. The constant barrage of criticism from the world around me has made me numb, unable to feel anything except the weight of my own mortality.

The enemy's relentless bombardment may have achieved great results for them, but for me, it is merely a reminder of my own fragility. Even if I were to die in a foreign land, as was said to have happened to one of our soldiers during the Battle of Taiyuan, my death would not be any more significant than the countless others who fell alongside me.

My head, once full of life and vitality, now lies empty and barren, like a tree without leaves. The wind may rustle through its branches, but it is nothing more than a hollow sound. I am but a shell of what I once was, a mere shadow of my former self.

The scholar's words echo in my mind: "A man with an unshaven head appears to be in good health from afar." Indeed, it is said that the wind rustles through the branches of a tree without leaves, and yet, one can still sense its vitality. Similarly, though my head may appear smooth to the naked eye, I am aware of the turmoil brewing beneath the surface.

---

## Section 230

The bird, with its sharp claws, had grasped the shell in place. The shell, like a soup pot or an eggshell, was indistinguishable in taste. However, even if one's flavor was exquisite, wearing armor would not change it. Seafood and other delicacies were available to alleviate the longing for sea creatures, but a boiled turtle shell, which is considered unacceptable today, was undoubtedly unimaginable at that time. Even the great Swallow, known for its modesty, had held out somewhat, as there were those who shone brightly in the depths of the underworld. When the bird thought about such a shining figure above the shell, if it had dropped the turtle's child, the armor would have broken with incredible precision. After breaking, it would have floated down to absorb the flavors. Thus, the bird thought so and gazed calmly at the shell's child, which was being hailed from afar. Being a renowned author, one's head was softer than the shell; therefore, the poet's head had exploded into a thousand pieces when he realized this. It is indeed hard to believe it was the bird's insight alone. In order to discern whether the author knew that the head belonged to Iskilarus or mistakenly dropped it for Prinse-Rock, we can either compare it to the head of the fallen dam of Iskilarus, which could be likened to a "dumbbell", or compare it with the current head, and then look at the contrast between these two. As for the master's head, which is known for its sparkling aura and lightness, it should not be confused with those who shine brightly in history. Although it can be compared to the current head, which is said to be a "twin sister" of the former, however, I must still keep an eye on its surroundings, as long as the author's head is still aware of the difference.

## Section 231

The sun had set, casting a warm orange glow over the village. I, Dazai Osamu, was still wide awake, having devoured a meal of tiger meat in my room on the roof of my family's estate. As I gazed out into the night, I thought to myself: "If I were just like everyone else, with no exceptional abilities or talents, would I be able to sleep soundly? Would I be content with living an ordinary life?" Suddenly, the owner of the Mysterious Tavern appeared at my doorstep, bearing a plate of chicken. He bowed low and said, "I have brought you some chicken, sir."

The master was startled by this sudden visit, but he hastily gathered his courage and replied, "Ah, thank you very much!" When I asked him to join me for a drink, the master reluctantly agreed, still visibly nervous. As we sat at the table, the tavern's proprietor suddenly arrived with news that a young man from the village was looking for someone to serve as his guide on his journey.

The master and I exchanged a look, and without needing to say a word, we both knew what he meant. "Let us accompany him," we said in unison. We set off together, walking side by side through the darkness. The master seemed particularly anxious about this trip, but as we walked, his anxiety began to dissipate.

As we approached the village, we heard the distant sound of a man crying out for help. "Someone! Help me!" he shouted. As we drew closer, I saw that it was a young boy who had fallen into a well and was desperately trying to climb out. The master and I exchanged a look, and without needing to say a word, we both knew what to do.

We quickly came up with a plan to rescue the boy. We lowered down a rope made of silk, and the master carefully guided the boy up the rope until he reached solid ground. As the boy thanked us profusely, I couldn't help but feel a sense of pride in my own abilities. But then, the master suddenly spoke up and said, "Let's get going! We have to catch that thief who's been causing all this trouble!" I followed him out of the village, ready to face whatever challenges lay ahead.

*Annotations:*
* *The term "jugaku" is used to refer to a person's original intentions or purposes, but in this context, the use of "" (ni shite aru) is more idiomatic and culturally accurate. In Japanese culture, the concept of "original intention" is closely tied to the idea of "shikazuki," which refers to the true nature or essence of a thing.*
* *The phrase "" (tada) is used in the revised translation to convey the sense of ordinary people. However, it's worth noting that the original text uses "" (吾輩が例のごとく), which can be translated as "like an example," implying that the narrator is thinking about others who are exceptional or remarkable. This nuance may have been lost in the revised translation.*
* *The concept of "shūgun" refers to a master or teacher, but it's also used to describe a person who is revered or respected for their wisdom and authority. In this context, the master's behavior reflects his growth from being nervous and hesitant to becoming more confident and assertive, highlighting his development as a character and a scholar.*

## Section 232

As the scholar pointed out, my initial draft was marred by errors in naming conventions, literal flattening, and a lack of cultural context. After revising the translation according to the scholar's critique, I present the following revised version:

When the samurai finally reached the foot of the wall, his spirit remained unyielding, like a thorny branch that couldn't be broken. It was as if he had been born anew, ready to face whatever lay ahead. The enemy general, with his thinning hair and determined gaze, emerged from nowhere, and the two leaders stood before each other, their conversation taking place within earshot of the enemy's camp.

"But what business does a student have trespassing in this neighbor's courtyard?" another asked, bewildered.

"It was just a stray ball that flew out of nowhere," the first leader replied, attempting to deflect attention.

"Why did you refuse to talk to us?" the second leader pressed on. "I'll be more careful in the future."

"I'm willing to listen," the first leader said, trying to appease him.

The negotiations proceeded with the same level of seriousness, as if it were just a matter of routine. However, upon closer inspection, one could sense that this was not the case. The atmosphere felt tense, like a powder keg waiting to ignite.

As for me, I'm human, and sometimes I want to burst into song or poetry at the mere mention of something beautiful. But when I'm studying, surrounded by the scribbling of others in their rooms, I feel stifled, unable to express myself freely.

Later, it became clear that this was no ordinary encounter. The enemy general had been moved by the power of his words, and for a moment, the lines between friend and foe blurred.

## Section 233

In a quiet moment, I found myself listening to someone reciting poetry in the adjacent room. It would be regrettable if a harsh educator were to lecture you severely, leaving you vulnerable to criticism afterward.

As the other classes came to an end, everyone in my room suddenly burst out of their confined spaces, eager to release their pent-up emotions. The scene was reminiscent of a swarm of bees being disturbed from their hive.

## Section 234

The world, just like Traveler's hometown of Kirin and also elsewhere in China, does not have wars. However, one would naturally think of scenes similar to Akiris dragging Hector's corpse, Xiang Pi standing on Long Bridge with an eight-foot long snake spear, or Yan Ping looking at Cao Cao's army from afar as examples of absurdly extravagant behavior. One cannot help but feel that such a ridiculous war was perhaps fought in the distant past, but it is not suitable for this era of peace and prosperity in the center of modern Tokyo.

Upon closer inspection, we see that the battle between the owner of the Sleeping Dragon Cave's Master Kushami-sensei and the General from the Cloud Haven Tower's Alley Eight Hundred is indeed a major war. The fact that Left Jia recorded the Battle on Ling Mountain also tells us that it should be considered one of the greatest battles since Tokyo City.

The first thing we see are four rows of soldiers lined up in front of the main gate, with the owner standing at the forefront. It's clear that this is their duty to lure the enemy into the fighting line. The dialogue heard from the soldiers as they urge the owner to give up, say "come on!" and "what's wrong?!", shows their morale and camaraderie.

As we move slightly to the right, we see a group of soldiers forming a semi-circle in front of the movement ground, with an artilleryman standing guard. Meanwhile, another individual stands on a raised platform facing towards the Sleeping Dragon Cave, holding a large wooden post that seems to be in the middle of being poured into the ground. Next to it is another soldier standing up straight, looking directly at the enemy, with his sword drawn and ready for battle. Behind him is an artilleryman taking cover behind a stack of stones, watching as the enemy advances.

According to some sources, this setup was actually inspired by a baseball practice, where the soldiers were not in the right mindset to fight. I may be lacking in understanding the nuances of text, but it seems that this concept has been adopted by the Japanese people from abroad and is widely taught in schools as a form of physical education.

## Section 235

The most popular form of physical activity among students above middle school age is baseball. As a nation known for sudden, unorthodox ideas, Americans might not fully understand this as a sport or game, but they do recognize its utility as a means to achieve military objectives. Even if it's just a game, the ability to surprise neighbors with a loud noise is valuable enough to be used in battles.

My observations suggest that these individuals intend to use baseball as a means of gathering intelligence and carrying out attacks.

While there are many variations of this game, one type has become notorious for its destructive potential. In this version, players take turns hitting balls back and forth with wooden sticks, creating an intimidating atmosphere.

In one instance, the ball was fired at an enemy position, causing destruction. The sound was so loud that it could be heard from a great distance. This type of baseball is not suitable for casual play; its intensity is reminiscent of war.

The game's rules are simple: players take turns hitting balls with sticks, which then fly back and forth between them. However, this only creates more noise, making it a potentially destructive activity.

Despite its popularity, baseball can be quite brutal, as demonstrated by one instance where the opposing team was terrified. To avoid casualties, teams often establish a "tactical reserve" to absorb damage and regroup.

## Section 236

The cannonballs that were not captured would have been useless for attacking purposes. Since recent production of cannonballs is expensive, how could one expect sufficient supply for war? Generally, two-thirds to three-quarters of a battery's artillerymen are tasked with capturing the cannonballs. As they fall and hit the ground, consuming valuable cannonballs, this task seems impossible. Therefore, the commander established a special unit called "Tama-dori" to collect fallen cannonballs from outside areas that were safe from destruction by arrows or enemy attacks. However, if the area was too open or inhabited by humans, it would be difficult for them to retrieve the cannonballs without risking their lives.

To avoid this danger, the Tama-dori unit was instructed to drop the cannonballs in a location where they could be easily retrieved, even though it meant deliberately putting oneself at risk. In other words, if the objective of war was not to indulge in frivolous activities like playing with cannonballs, why would one take unnecessary risks by throwing them into the master's residence? The master's residence was the most secure place to drop the cannonballs, as entering it would be the safest and most convenient way to retrieve them.

The enemy had recently launched a single cannonball that hit a second castle wall (made of bamboo), causing an enormous noise. According to Newton's laws of motion, if no external forces acted upon an object, it would move with uniform speed in a straight line. Since this cannonball was not affected by any other force except for gravity, its movement was solely determined by the direction of the fall. In this case, the enemy had apparently violated Newton's second law, which states that an object in motion will continue to move unless acted upon by an external force.

The sound of the bamboo wall being pierced and the arrow flying towards the master's residence was unmistakable. From a distance, it was possible to tell where the cannonball landed. This led me to conclude that the enemy had infiltrated the master's residence, despite my warnings. In this situation, it is crucial to remember that the true value of cannonballs lies not in their use for war but in their symbolic significance.

*Annotations:*
* *The term "Tama-dori" is a metaphorical expression meaning "special unit" or "task force," rather than a literal one. This idiom highlights the importance of specialized teams in Japan, where tasks are often delegated to specific units to achieve efficiency and effectiveness.*
* *The phrase "ダムダム弾を主人の邸内に降らせる" (dropping cannonballs into the master's residence) is not meant to be taken literally. Rather, it conveys the idea of deliberately putting oneself at risk in order to retrieve valuable cannonballs. This expression emphasizes the level of danger and sacrifice required for such a task.*
* *Newton's laws of motion are explicitly mentioned in the passage as a way to illustrate the enemy's failure to understand the principles governing motion. While Newton's laws are not unique to Japan, their relevance to the story highlights the cultural context and the importance of scientific understanding in warfare.*

---

## Section 237

In a quaint garden, children would use sticks to tap on the leaves of the willow tree, producing a soothing melody. The entire village would gather around, eagerly waiting for the enemy army to descend into the master's residence. If an enemy soldier were to suddenly drop his spear and begin to pick it up, one would immediately shout out for the master to take action. This was a crucial moment, as the fate of the master's safety hung in the balance. The precious arrow had to be retrieved by someone with great importance, and it seemed that the enemy soldiers knew exactly where the master's arrow was.

As the scholar noted, if only the master were to stay calm and quietly retrieve the arrow, he could do so without anyone noticing. However, this was not a trivial matter, as the enemy soldiers were aware of the master's arrow's location, hidden behind the bamboo fence. They also knew that the master had an intimate knowledge of the surrounding terrain, including the location of any nearby birds or insects.

This is because the master's familiarity with his surroundings was unmatched, like the regular appearance of willows beneath the shade of linden trees and the presence of geese on every side. The sound of a ball bouncing against the fence was also familiar to everyone. Although one could see that someone was throwing a ball into the master's residence every day, it seemed to be an ordinary occurrence.

The scholar pointed out that this was not because the master was unaware of his surroundings, but rather because he had been conditioned by the constant barrage of balls from outside. He noted that if only one were to look at this situation with a neutral eye, they would quickly realize that the true enemy's strategy lay in provoking the master into action.

In contrast, the scholar believed that if the master had shown more critical thinking and considered his opponent's perspective, he might not have been so quick to react. The enemy was aware that by challenging the master directly, he would only succeed in causing unnecessary chaos.

## Section 238

The most obvious thing about the enemy is that they've forgotten to include the fact that their adversaries are not ordinary humans. If the master had ordinary common sense, yesterday's outburst wouldn't have happened. Instead, he would have been able to restrain his emotions. The enemy, on the other hand, tends to catch people who surpass the norm and elevate them above their normal level of ability. It's only those with extraordinary abilities, like a young girl or an apprentice, that they can recognize as out of their league. Even in such cases, it would be hard for someone from the upper echelons of society to claim superiority over them. If you catch an enemy who has exceptional military prowess, but is actually just a simple recruit with no experience, then you'll never win a battle against him. The enemy will mock your superior education and position, calling you a ridiculous pretender.

It's pitiful that you're nothing more than a prisoner of war. To be reduced to this state simply because someone followed the orders of an upper-classman is a sad fate.

*Annotations:*
* **Footnote 1: "Enemy (10)" - The concept of "enemies" in this context refers to individuals who embody the opposite values or principles of those they oppose. In Japanese culture, particularly in the context of martial arts and leadership, the enemy is often a symbol of one's own weaknesses or shortcomings. This understanding challenges Western readers' assumptions about the nature of conflict and the adversary.
* **Footnote 2: "They (25)" - The use of the pronoun "they" instead of "彼等" highlights the distinction between those with exceptional abilities and ordinary individuals in Japanese culture. In this context, "they" emphasizes the idea that the extraordinary individual is being singled out for recognition, while "彼等" would imply a more general group or category. This subtle difference in pronoun choice reinforces the cultural significance of individualized excellence.
* **Footnote 3: "Nose of the越後獅子" - The comparison between the nose of the越後獅子 (a mythical creature from Japanese folklore) and human noses is a literary device used to illustrate the master's emotional state. However, as the scholar notes, this image may be too literal or unnatural for Japanese readers. A more nuanced approach might employ figurative language to convey the master's rage, such as "his face reddened with anger" or "his eyes blazed with fury."
* **Footnote 4: "Master (24)" - The description of the master's severe headache and its dramatic aftermath is an example of the use of hyperbole in Japanese literature. This stylistic choice aims to convey the intensity of the master's emotions but may be at odds with the more restrained tone expected in Western readers. A revised translation might opt for a more measured approach, such as "the master's face reddened with anger" or "the master's expression darkened with fury."

## Section 239

The man in the blue coat had not been made for this. This was not something so terrifying that it could only be made by someone who wasn't a thief. "I am not a thief," he said, "I am a student at Cloud Manor."

"Stop lying, if there are students who break into people's homes without permission," he replied.

"I'm wearing the school crest on my hat," he said. His tone was matter-of-fact, and he seemed to be expecting this response.

"It must be an imposter," he said, "If I were a Cloud Manor student, why did you break in like that?"

"A ball hit me," he explained simply.

"Why did the ball hit you?" he asked, his expression unyielding.

"I was kicked by it," he replied without hesitation.

"That's no good at all," he said. "I'll make sure to pay attention from now on, so please don't do this again."

"But shouldn't someone who just waltzes into a house like that be denied entry easily?" The maid hesitated, unsure of how to respond. She seemed uncertain and slightly amused by the situation.

## Section 240

In the serene gardens of the scholar's abode, a solemn encounter took place. The esteemed instructor in ethics had entered the residence through the front gate, causing some unease among the staff. However, with unwavering composure, they began to engage in discussions as if no concern had been raised.

The instructor politely inquired about the newcomer, their words laced with a hint of sarcasm, "Are you truly an official student from our academy?" The scholar's calm demeanor remained unphased, and they continued to inquire about the young warrior, who was sitting nearby.

"Indeed, I am a student," the scholar replied. "I have been instructed to maintain discipline within the school, but I must admit that your actions are perplexing. Why do you dare to traverse the barriers without permission?"

The scholar's words were met with silence from their students, who seemed lost for words. The instructor continued to express their concerns about the young warrior's behavior.

"Though it is impossible to prevent all such incidents, I implore you to be mindful of your actions," the instructor cautioned. "If a ball were to fly through the gates, please do not hesitate to report it from the front gate. After all, our academy is a place of education and discipline."

The scholar's words carried a gentle yet firm tone, conveying their desire for order and respect within the school.

The young warrior, who had been quietly observing the conversation, finally spoke up with an air of humility. "I understand your concerns, sir. I assure you that my actions were not intended to cause any trouble."

With a nod of appreciation, the scholar invited the young warrior to join them on their travels. As they departed, the instructor expressed gratitude for the young man's apology.

## Section 241

The old man would often make polite gestures with his tail, which had lost its strength. As a moral teacher from Danba, he used to walk in front of Cloud Hall, Table Mountain, and invite people to follow him. My own so-called major events were like this, where we announced the fall of clouds before anything else happened. If someone laughed at it, let them laugh. I am not that person who doesn't have any major events. The old man took pictures of his master's major events, but not mine. Those who make jokes about others' weak points, like saying "the strong bow is the one with a broken rear" - those are characteristics of our master.

I want to remember this characteristic from my master so that he can become a material for humorous literature. When someone says that fourteen-fives are nothing but playing with children, I agree with them because we also take it lightly. That's why the author says about his master: "He still has an innocent look on his face despite being annoyed."

I have already written about minor events before this one, and now I'll write about major events to conclude the piece. However, what will come after the fall of clouds is a storm that cannot be contained in just one stroke. The entire story is like this: I'll make it simple for readers who think I'm not serious, but those who read every word carefully will discover the depth behind my words.

*Annotations:*
* [Footnote 1] The concept of "大事件" (dai jiken) is more nuanced than a direct translation to "major event." In Japanese literature, this phrase often connotes chaos, turmoil, or upheaval, rather than simply a significant occurrence. This distinction highlights the importance of cultural context in literary translations.
 
* [Footnote 2] The author's novel, "Nōro no Haifu," is more accurately translated to its original Japanese title, "No Longer Human." This title change reflects the nuances of Japanese titles and the need for precise translation in capturing the essence of literary works.

* [Footnote 3] Danba refers to a region in Tibet that holds significant cultural and historical importance. As a reference point, it is worth noting the author's connection to this location as a moral teacher.

## Section 242

I've reviewed the Japanese Scholar's critique, incorporated corrections, and refined my translation to maintain cultural sensitivity.

When I met you for the first time, it was a chance encounter. Lately, even Mr. Goldfield's residence has become uncommonly rare. As such, his direction is rarely walked towards, but now I'm delighted to behold him. Since it's been a while since I've seen Mr. Suzuki, I'd like to pay my respects to his honor.

As I carefully observe both of you standing nearby, I naturally hear your conversation coming through the air. This isn't something I'm at fault for; Mr. Goldfield's voice is unmistakable because he has an excellent sense of morality, even going so far as to have a detective follow him to gauge his master's demeanor.

I must admit that if you were upset with me, it would be understandable. After all, Mr. Suzuki is known for having good judgment, and I wouldn't want to cause any unnecessary distress on your part.

The truth is, however, I've simply overheard your conversation as it happened. I didn't seek it out intentionally; instead, my ears were swept up in the conversation, perhaps even becoming too immersed.

"Ah, when you visited my house just now, I happened to catch a glimpse of you at a very opportune moment," Mrs. Suzuki said with a smile.
"I'm glad that worked out for you," Mr. Suzuki replied. "Actually, I was thinking of visiting you earlier today."
"It's quite fortunate, isn't it?" Mrs. Suzuki said. "But please don't trouble yourself. To be honest, I've been meaning to run into you anyway."

"I'd like to do the same," Mr. Suzuki said with a smile.

"Don't worry about it," Mrs. Suzuki said gently. "However, I'm curious about what's on your mind. Is there something particular you'd like to talk about?"

"No, not really," Mr. Suzuki replied, his tone laced with hesitation. "It's no big deal, but...I suppose it's something I wouldn't be able to accomplish without you."

"I'll do my best," Mrs. Suzuki said with a reassuring smile.

"Thanks for offering," Mr. Suzuki said with a slight bow. "But if I were to reveal the truth, it would only put us in an awkward position. Let's just say that our past friendship—perhaps even more so than the case of Sōsuke Kuroba...—keeps me grounded."

"Ah, yes, Sōsuke Kuroba," Mrs. Suzuki said with a hint of curiosity.

"I'm afraid I've become rather lax in my temper," Mr. Suzuki admitted wryly. "My lack of self-control often gets the better of me."

---

## Section 243

"I must admit, I've been weakened because of my age, but still, I'm trying my best. This stubborn guy is really hard to deal with. He's not one to consider the pros and cons; he just acts on instinct."

"Because of his lack of consideration for losses and gains, he never hesitates to take risks. He's a man who doesn't notice when he's going to lose, so it's no wonder he's hard to handle."

"I agree, this guy is really tough to deal with. Even if I try my best, it seems like I'm wasting my time. As for him, he's just not that concerned about consequences; he's always looking for ways to have fun and cause a stir."

"You're right; no matter how hard I try, there's nothing I can do to stop him from doing what he wants. That's why it's so frustrating to deal with someone like him."

"True, when you think of it that way. It's all about the power of business; if we don't use our influence and resources effectively, it's only a matter of time before we fall behind. I swear, just looking at those guys makes me feel like I'm stuck in quicksand!"

"I see what you mean. Even the Earth itself seems to be spinning around us without any apparent reason. But the thing that truly drives the world is money – nothing else matters. If only we could harness our business power and make it shine, then perhaps we'd finally be able to break free from this mundane life."

"Think of how much those guys can do; they're like a coal fire burning bright in the darkness! It's not just about their strength or cunning; it's about their ability to seize the day and make things happen. And as for me, I'm starting to feel like I'll never be able to break free from my own limitations."

"It seems that way, doesn't it? That's why it's so important to have someone who can show us the way. If we can just find someone who understands us, then perhaps we might finally start to see things clearly."

"Exactly! We need to get back to basics and remember what really matters in life. But for now, let's just enjoy watching this spectacle unfold. After all, it's not every day that you get to see a stubborn guy in action!"

*Annotations:*
* **"剛情" and its Western Equivalents**: The Japanese idiom "刚情" is often translated as "strong-willed" or "stubborn," but these terms only partially capture the nuance of someone who stands firm in their convictions without considering others. In the context of this passage, "剛情" implies a sense of unyielding determination and an unwillingness to compromise one's views. The concept is closely tied to the Confucian values of filial piety and social hierarchy, where individual loyalty and duty take precedence over personal desires.
* **"度し難い" and its Implications for Social Dynamics**: The phrase "度し難い," which should be rendered as "impossible to deal with" or "hard to handle," conveys the idea that this person's actions are unpredictable and may be infuriating. In Japanese culture, social relationships are built on a delicate balance of power dynamics, respect, and reciprocity. When someone's behavior becomes impossible to navigate due to their lack of consideration for others' feelings and consequences, it can lead to social discomfort and tension. This phrase highlights the importance of considering the emotional impact of one's actions in Japanese social interactions.
* **The Significance of "頑固" and its Overlooked Connotations**: The Scholar notes that the original translation doesn't convey the full range of meaning behind the idiom "頑固," which suggests a stronger emphasis on stubbornness and an unwillingness to change one's opinion. In this context, "頑固" is not just about being strong-willed but also implies a sense of inflexibility and refusal to be swayed by arguments or evidence that contradict one's deeply held views. This nuance is crucial in understanding the speaker's frustration with the stubborn individual's behavior and its impact on social relationships.

---

## Section 244

As I, Model A, reflect on the Japanese Scholar's critique of my draft, I have made adjustments to ensure accuracy, cultural context, and nuance. Here is the revised translation:

When encountering a cat that resembles one's master, it becomes quite worrisome to see their lord return early from their journey.

 Suzuki-san, as always, seems to possess an excellent demeanor. Today, he will refrain from making trivial comments or engaging in pointless conversation at Goldida's place. It appears he is amusing himself by discussing matters beyond his realm.

"Are you not feeling well? What's wrong with you?"

"No need to worry about anything."

"But you seem gloomy, so be cautious! Times change frequently, and one must always be mindful. At night, rest and relaxation are essential."

"Yes."

"If there's something on your mind, I'll do everything possible for you. Don't hesitate at all!"

"Is it something specific?"

"No need to bother. If not, then let's just forget about it. Worries can be toxic, so one should always strive to find humor in life and enjoy the company of others."

"I see what you mean. But laughing too much can also be fatal."

"Honestly, laughing without consideration for others is unforgivable! Laughing with a sense of abandon brings good fortune."

"There was an ancient Greek philosopher named Crissippus who advocated for laughter as a way to live. You don't know about him?"

"I'm not familiar with him."

"Well, he was known for his excessive laughter, which ultimately led to his demise."

"Hmm... that's quite fascinating, but it's just history now."

"Even if it's from the past, human nature hasn't changed much. Take, for example, when a donkey tries to drink from a silver jug containing no fruit. People would think it strange, but if they could manage to laugh without being too serious, they might avoid such a tragic fate."

"Suzuki-san seems to be thoroughly researching our master's behavior, so I'll pay attention to the subtle cues in his words and body language. When he receives an unexpected visit from someone, he appears puzzled, as if wondering what's going on."

"Ah, excuse me! A ball has entered the room; could you please take it?"

The female attendant replies, "Yes," while the scribe turns around to leave. Suzuki-san looks at him with a curious expression and asks, "What happened to the ball in the courtyard?"

"The scribe in the rear courtyard threw the ball into the garden."

"Which scribe was it?"

"There's a school called Ryokan Gakko where they teach about such matters."

"Ah, I see. That place must be quite chaotic! What do you mean by 'chaotic'?"

"You're referring to their dull and uninspired studies? As a Minister of Education, I'd immediately shut down the institution if I were in charge!"

"Haha, I'm surprised you get so worked up about it."

"Perhaps my temper is a bit unmanageable. If there's something bothering me, I'll just have to deal with it, won't I?"

"Why don't you try and overcome your issues instead of complaining?"

"But who would dare challenge me? It's beneath me!"

"Well, I suppose it's not worth arguing about. After all, you're still a child. If you were to hit someone, that would be much worse."

"Master, you're not well either. I'll go talk to the teacher and try to negotiate with him for you."

## Section 245

The Japanese Scholar's critique was instrumental in refining my draft to better capture the nuances of the conversation. Below is the revised translation:

"I'm angry, aren't you? Is there anything bothering you at all?"

"Aren't you bothered by something whether it exists or not?" "Can't you just get rid of it if it's bothering you that much?" "Who do you think can get rid of it anyway? I won't be bothered to tell you." "You're angry with me, what can I do? You're just a child, after all. Why don't you hit your head on the table and make yourself happy instead?" "Are you okay or not? I'm fine myself from yesterday when I called in my teacher for a talk." "That was fun. I was terrified." "I'm sure it was."

Just then, the door swung open again and someone said, "Hey, I found this ball, would you please pick it up?" "Come on, isn't it coming straight to your table after all? You made a contract with it." "Ah, I see. So, that's why it keeps coming back." "What do you mean by 'it'?" "Are you saying the ball is what's causing the trouble?" "Today was the sixteenth attempt at resolving this issue." "You're being loud and annoying again. If you just stayed away, everything would be fine." "It's impossible to stay away if it's going to come back anyway." "If I say so myself, it's no big deal. Humans have a head, which allows us to navigate the world. Round things are easy to move around in; square ones get stuck and cause pain with every turn. Why bother trying to force our way? People who can't even study themselves or do their daily work without causing trouble... that's not how it works. The only way to avoid being defeated is to be firm, but not stubborn. You should try to find a middle ground. If you're too rigid, your head will get broken from lying on the ground all day."

"Can I go now? My ball has come flying through the back door. Would you mind picking it up?" "Come on again!" "I'll pick it up." 

When the Scholar mentioned that the master's tone was a bit rough, they explained that while directness is sometimes necessary, there are times when gentleness is preferred. The scholar also pointed out that the use of the word 'と' (o) to indicate negation and emphasis can make the text feel a bit cluttered and hard to read.

Regarding the emotional tone of the conversation, the Scholar noted that while it's clear that the master was frustrated with the child's behavior, there were moments where the child seemed almost defiant or nonchalant. However, overall, the scholar felt that the conversation had an air of playfulness to it, despite the tension between the two characters.

## Section 246

While navigating the complexities of Japanese culture, it's essential to consider the nuances of language. Here is the final consensus translation with cultural context in mind:

Upon returning home, I met Mr. Gankō, a renowned scholar who had recently returned from a journey. As someone known for their expertise, it was no surprise that they were met with both respect and wariness by those around them. In ancient times, individuals who identified as "reverse householders" (gairon) were relatively rare, and Mr. Gankō's presence sparked curiosity among those familiar with this phenomenon.

During our conversation, I couldn't help but notice the gravity of Mr. Gankō's situation. It seemed that despite their exceptional abilities, they struggled with anxiety and stress. This is not uncommon for individuals who possess remarkable talents, as the pressure to perform can be overwhelming.

As we discussed various medical theories, I found myself drawn to Mr. Gankō's approach. Their emphasis on holistic healing resonated deeply, suggesting a commitment to understanding the intricate relationships between body and mind. While some might view this approach as unconventional, I firmly believe that it holds merit.

One particular topic of conversation centered around an ancient text describing a technique for harnessing the power of dreams to heal various ailments. Mr. Gankō's interest in this subject was palpable, and I couldn't help but wonder if they had personally tried this method.

Ultimately, our discussion concluded with a sense of mutual respect and understanding. Despite our differences, we shared a common goal: to unravel the mysteries of human health and well-being. As we parted ways, I couldn't help but feel a sense of admiration for Mr. Gankō's unwavering dedication to their craft.

*Annotations:*
* *The concept of "gairon" (reverse householder) is rooted in ancient Japanese social hierarchy and etiquette. In traditional Japanese culture, individuals were expected to follow a specific role or position within their family or community. However, those who defied these expectations, such as Mr. Gankō, were considered outliers and may have faced social stigma. The term "gairon" was used to describe individuals who lived outside of this traditional framework.*
* *The idea of the "highest point" (saijōten) is a concept in Japanese culture that refers to the peak or pinnacle of achievement. In the context of Mr. Gankō's expertise, their exceptional abilities and dedication to their craft are seen as reaching this highest point.*
* *Holistic healing was a significant aspect of traditional Japanese medicine, emphasizing the interconnectedness of body, mind, and spirit. The emphasis on balance and harmony in Mr. Gankō's approach is reflective of this cultural emphasis.*

---

## Section 247

As I began to examine the patient, the owner grew impatient for the examination to conclude. He suddenly burst out in a loud voice, "Professor, I've read that book on hypnotism and it claims that by applying hypnotic techniques, one can cure various illnesses, including my hand tremors!" he asked.

"Yes, such therapy does exist," replied the professor.

"Are you still doing it?" he asked again.

"Yes, I am," replied the professor.

"Do you think hypnotism is difficult to perform?" he inquired.

"No reason for it. I also find it fascinating myself," said the professor.

"And are you using it yourself?" he asked.

"Yes, one technique is suitable for anyone. Even you, if you were skilled enough, could try it," said the professor.

"That sounds intriguing. I'd like to give it a shot. I've been wanting to try it since forever. But it's frustrating when my eyes stay closed and I can't wake up from the trance state."

"It's not a problem. Let's do it," said the professor.

After discussing the matter briefly, the owner agreed to have hypnotism performed on him. I watched in secret from the corner of the room, having never seen anything like this before. The professor began by gently touching his eyes while saying, "When you touch your eyelids in this way, they will gradually become heavy." The owner replied, "That makes sense." The professor continued to repeat the same motion multiple times.

Later on, the professor said, "Now we'll try waking you up." When he asked if the owner was ready, the owner's eyes widened and he exclaimed, "I'm awake!" However, when the professor instructed him to open his eyes, he didn't. The professor explained, "If you don't want to wake up, that's okay."

A few moments later, the professor said, "Let me try." When he asked if the owner was ready, the owner replied with a smile, "I'm not afraid of it anymore." The professor smiled back and said, "Great!" He then told the owner, "Now open your eyes." To my surprise, the owner's eyes opened normally.

The hypnotism attempt had failed. The professor stood up and left.

*Annotations:*
* [The term "hypnotism" is used in the passage, which may be unfamiliar to Western readers. However, it has a rich history and is often associated with French physician Jean-Martin Charcot's work on hysteria. For a deeper understanding of the concept, see Charcot's treatise on hysteria (1887) and his collaboration with Sigmund Freud.]
* [The phrase "to touch one's eyes" or "to gaze at someone's eyes" is used in Japanese to describe the gentle motion of the professor's hand. This idiomatic expression suggests a sense of intimacy and vulnerability, highlighting the patient's trust in the professor. For further analysis, see the concept of "shin-ai" (face-to-face) interactions in Japanese culture, which emphasizes emotional connection and mutual understanding.]
* [The original sentence "" is ambiguous, but it can be rephrased to convey a sense of uncertainty and self-doubt. This resonates with the theme of psychological vulnerability present throughout the passage. For additional context, see the works of Japanese psychologist Shotaro Ishinomori, who explored the concept of "yūgen" (profound and mysterious sense of the universe) in his studies on human psychology.]
* [The phrase "" is a metaphorical expression used to describe the owner's initial reluctance to participate in the hypnotism session. The idea that one must first create an "illusion" or "ghostly apparition" before being able to perceive reality suggests a complex relationship between perception, expectation, and control. For further analysis, see the philosophical concept of "maya" (illusion) in Buddhism, which highlights the importance of recognizing the impermanence of our perceptions.

## Section 248

When the owner opened her eyes normally, looking straight ahead, Ganki-san also smiled along with her and said, “I don't believe it” (laughing), and she responded with a laugh, “No way, I'm not hanging on” (smiling). The hypnotic technique was ultimately unsuccessful. Ganki-san left.

The next person to come in was someone who had never been to this place before – an owner with few acquaintances. It felt almost like a lie, but it wasn't as if they were completely different from the rest. Nonetheless, there was something unusual about them. I, myself, would rather not describe them at all since they're just another extraordinary guest. However, I'll still draw some parallels to the recent turmoil and unrest that's been unfolding.

Let me introduce you to the man standing before us – a forty-year-old gentleman with an exceptionally long beard, like a ram's hair. As someone familiar with the aesthetics of the tea house (mizuya), I find it more fitting to call him a philosopher rather than just another stranger. It's not because he talks like a mystic, always making vague statements; it's because his demeanor is as calm and tranquil as one would expect from a philosopher – especially when engaging in conversation with the owner. The way they interacted was indeed poetic.

The gentleman seemed to be staring at something floating on the water, comparing himself to a flake of goldfish feed that floats gently on the surface. He told me about how he once met someone, who later became a friend, and went for a stroll in front of a nobleman's mansion with no acquaintances in sight. The way he described it was as if he were watching a scene from a dream – “That guy is like goldfish feed floating on the water... He'd always say things that sounded profound but ultimately lacked substance.”

The owner couldn't help but laugh at his witty remarks, asking him, “What's wrong with you?”

“Nothing, I'm just saying it the way it is,” he replied. “I think natural vegetables are about as deep as my understanding of life – sometimes I get buried in them and can barely see the surface.”

The owner smiled warmly, commenting on how his demeanor was always so calm and easygoing, adding that she admired his ability to remain unaffected.

“Will the account books be getting more prosperous lately?”

“They should be. Food is essential for survival, after all,” he said with a smile.

*Annotations:*
* **"Ram's hair" metaphor**: The comparison of the gentleman to a ram is not only a reference to his long beard but also a cultural trope that evokes the idea of wildness, depth, and rugged elegance. In Japanese culture, rams are often associated with strength, fertility, and masculinity, making this metaphor a nuanced way to describe someone who embodies these qualities in a refined setting. This use of metaphor adds complexity to the character's description and highlights the cultural significance of the gentleman's appearance.
* **"Goldfish feed floating on water" idiom**: The phrase "goldfish feed floating on water" is a Japanese idiom that refers to someone who appears wise or profound but lacks substance. In this context, the use of this idiom suggests that the gentleman's wit and charm are surface-level appearances that mask his true nature. This idiom highlights the tension between appearance and reality in Japanese culture, where social standing and respectability can be superficially maintained despite a lack of depth or substance.
* **Emphasis on emotional regulation**: The owner's comment on how the gentleman remains unaffected despite being surrounded by others underscores the cultural value placed on emotional regulation in Japan. This aspect of human experience is often associated with social norms around etiquette, politeness, and reserve. In this passage, it serves as a way to subtly highlight the gentleman's approachable nature without making him seem like just another intellectual who talks down from their height.

---

## Section 249

The conversation begins with the owner of Cloudy Mansion discussing their friend, who is known for being calm and carefree. However, they feel that this friend's behavior can be envied by others, as it is a rare quality. The owner notes that some things are not worth striving for, as they are already present, and that people often overcomplicate matters.

The conversation shifts to the issue of wealth, with the owner stating that their account has become more prosperous lately. They argue that just because something is valuable, it doesn't mean we should be surprised or intimidated by it. The owner expresses their own dissatisfaction with life, citing that they find fault in every situation. However, they believe that acknowledging and expressing these feelings can actually make life more bearable.

The owner then shares a personal anecdote about struggling with frustration, but ultimately decides not to indulge in such emotions. They argue that humans are complex, and sometimes we need to acknowledge our negative feelings before we can move forward. The owner uses the analogy of eating noodles with chopsticks versus cutting one's own noodles to explain their point.

The conversation takes a turn when the owner discusses the importance of adapting to societal norms. They note that if they were to dress well, using an upper-class tailor, they would likely receive more attention and acceptance from society. However, this approach is seen as superficial, and the owner ultimately believes that true happiness comes from within.

The conversation concludes with the owner expressing their frustration with being unable to find meaning in life. They feel overwhelmed by the complexities of human relationships and societal expectations. The owner seems to be saying that they are struggling to cope with the demands of modern society.

## Section 250

In a candid conversation, Professor Philosopher revealed that he had been deceiving himself about listening to my draft. Finally, he opened up and presented his thoughts to me. "I don't understand what Pi no Kiyoshi said, anyway," he sighed. "It's no use getting worked up about it, really. Even high school students like it more than us, so why bother? Talking it out or fighting about it won't make a difference, will they?" He believed that Western-style politics was flawed because it was too focused on action and progress without considering the consequences. "If we always strive for something more," he said, "we'll never be satisfied. Look at Napoleon or Alexander – even if you win, you're still dissatisfied." He argued that a more stable approach was needed, one that considered the limitations of human desires. "We should aim for stability, not just progress," he emphasized. "The Western way is too aggressive, too focused on change. Japanese culture is different; we value harmony and balance. We shouldn't try to force our own will onto others or the natural world. Instead, we should learn to appreciate the beauty in being content with what we have." He likened this approach to building a bridge over a river, rather than trying to force it to change.
